HEADLINE: The Stars, the Planets, the Cosmic Counsel

BYLINE: By DIANE SIERPINA 

 BODY:
WHEN was the last time you checked your horoscope? Before you signed an important contract or started a new business? Before agreeing to a blind date? Perhaps not since you were a teen-ager? But with 1998 only days away and New Year's resolutions still to be made, the alignment of the planets may have a lot to tell you about the year ahead -- and consultation could be at your cyber fingertips.
Stars, the celestial kind, do a lot of talking, says Eugenie Diserio. And she should know. For more than two years now, nearly 600,000 households a month have been visiting Astronet (the astrology web site she created for America Online from her mother's basement in New Canaan) looking for cosmic counsel on love, pets, finance, beauty, career and, of all things, cooking. In this cyberworld, Ms. Diserio is known as Genie Easy and her daily horoscopes hold a lot of weight. 
 So what's in store for 1998? Genie is predicting we'll see relationships re-evaluated, increased humanitarianism and, uh-oh, economic belt-tightening, both personally and professionally. But more on that later. For now, the former singer/songwriter is glad that, two years ago, she had a sign from above to follow her passion and create a business in astrology.
"You can't lose," Ms. Diserio, 44, says of astrology and, in particular, Astronet, which has grown into a $1 million-plus business. "On one hand, it's entertainment. On the other hand, it's almost, like, a daily inspiration. A little prayer. And, if you get deeper into it, it can be really good guidance."
Astronet provides horoscopes -- lots and lots of them -- daily, weekly and monthly. And there are horoscopes by category -- teen-agers, pets, celebrities. You also can visit chat rooms, get a tarot reading, have your natal chart analyzed, find an astromate, read astrology magazines and do some cosmic shopping. About 30 expert astrology writers and readers and another 80 volunteer chat room hosts serve the growing number of visitors to Astronet on AOL (1.2 million visits a day) and a smaller Internet site at www.astronet.com on the World Wide Web (1.5 million visits a month), 24 hours a day, seven days a week.
Why do so many online users visit Astronet? Ms. Diserio cites Roper polls indicating one-quarter of Americans admit they use astrology to help make decisions. On-line access gives them an immediate level of information, as well as feedback.
"Everybody wants to talk about themselves," explains Ms. Diserio. "Nobody can hear enough about themselves. And then, once you get off you, you become addicted to wanting to know more about your lover, your partner, your kids, your boss. It's human nature's fascination with each other. And, there's also this mystical thing -- everybody wants to know the future." But you never really get the future, the self-taught astrologer says. You must create your own using the advice you get. That's why most horoscopes are so generic, she says. They must get the reader to think about his or her own issues, reaffirm what is personally known and take action. "We all make choices in life," says Ms. Diserio. " When you see your chart, it's a road map for your life. It will show you hidden talents, opportunistic cycles and obstacles. Now you can choose to listen to this, work with it or you can just ignore it. But you'll often find that you're going to swim upstream."
Ms. Diserio and her chat host manager, Cris Kunicki, screen both the professional and volunteer chat hosts on Astronet. "We can assure people of the quality control," says Ms. Diserio. The best astrologers are over age 35, have years of experience and can charge up to $350 for an interpretive reading of a client's natal, or personality, chart that can last from one to two hours. Each astrologer interprets a chart in his or her own way. The cyber visitor choses an astrologer based on the biography available on Astronet.
"It's like anybody who's an important advisor to your life, you've got to shop around," says Ms. Diserio. "You've got to find somebody who speaks to you."
Ms. Diserio said she had an inkling as a graduate student at Columbia University 20 years ago that some day her life would come together in grand fashion.
An astrologer reading her chart at the time for a whopping $100 had predicted it. But he also had warned that the preceding years would be a struggle. "In a way," she recalled, "it was depressing."
Ms. Diserio was a struggling painter and a singer, songwriter and recording artist who traveled the world. After gigs, she would relax in the wee hours of the morning by reading horoscopes and doing charts by hand for others, sometimes earning a few dollars. She left the music business nearly 10 years ago and joined a startup marketing/licensing firm, learning the ins and outs of business.
In late 1994, she was laid off, finding solace exploring remote astrology sites on the Internet. Her entrepreneurial spirit -- and a $150,000 seed grant from AOL's Greenhouse program that nurtures new sites -- set her to work in her mother's basement.
Astronet was born Nov. 13, 1995, a Scorpio. It's chart hangs on the wall near her desk, next to those for herself and her son, Jake, 8. The planet configuration on Astronet's chart sent a message that, with a lot of hard work, the astrology service would be successful.
The company now operates out of offices on Elm Street in New Canaan, with a full-time staff of 12 and a new chief operating officer, Jim Turner. Ms. Diserio's office is filled with cactus plants ("I have a green thumb," she says) and an eclectic assortment of artwork made by herself and Jake.
On the mantel over the fireplace are Beanie Baby stuffed animals and a Genie Lamp from Disney World. The boss works late nights and on weekends, is host to monthly zodiac birthday parties on-line and pops in on chat rooms for teen-agers and adults.
"You wouldn't believe some of the letters I get," she says, clutching her chest to emphasize the despair.
Astronet's visitors are predominately female (74 percent), ages 18 to 54 (78 percent), single (60 percent), earn over $50,000 a year (64 percent) and have college degrees (64 percent). Twenty-five percent are students and 35 percent are in professional or managerial careers. The audience appeals to major sponsors, among them Avon and Godiva Chocolates.
Astronet is now ranked No. 2 of all original content providers on the world's busiest on-line service provider, behind Love@aol.com, the singles website.
"She's done a spectacular job," says Charlie Fink, chief creative officer for AOL's Greenhouse Networks.
Ms. Diserio has held up major changes to Astronet because the planet Mercury, which rules our rational/conscious mind, is in retrograde (going back on its orbit) until Dec. 29. Beginning in January, however, Astronet will take on a redesigned look and offer many new features, including a month-long predictathon during which professional astrologers will give their forecasts for the year on such issues as finance, sports, work and career, health, love, glamour and alternative life styles. Contests are planned with prizes including a trip to the Bahamas and a shopping spree at Avon.
Visitors to Astronet's new Readers Network also will be able to book private time with a professional astrologer who will interpret their natal chart (now created quickly with special software) for a fee and they can order charts, reports or cassettes. In the future, they may also be able to order perfumes for every sign of the zodiac.
Now back to predictions for 1998. Ms. Desirio says Venus went retrograde on Dec. 26 and stays that way until February. That means we will be reviewing our relationships, personal and otherwise.
"Venus blesses us with a new awareness of how to negotiate and rebalance our responsibilities to ourselves and others," she says. "Go after what you want; dream of achieving and help those around you to do the same. It's a reminder that we can never do it alone."
By early February, Neptune, the planet of idealism, joins Uranus, the reality planet, in Aquarius, an indicator that humanitarianism will drive the world and continue to do so for at least the next 12 years.
This spiritual awareness of service to others and care of the earth was signaled by the death of Diana, Princess of Wales, at the start of a solar eclipse/new moon in Virgo and the burial of Mother Theresa two weeks later at the lunar eclipse/full moon in Pisces, says Ms. Diserio.
"The universe sent us the message that compassionate service must become a part of our daily lives," she says.
The astrologer also predicts that more government corruption and dishonesty will be exposed and several leaders will fall from grace, offering public apologies. And that since Neptune also rules pollution, we will refocus our energies from landfills to the quality of our air.
By June, when Saturn enters Taurus, Ms. Desirio expects the economy to slow down so it can rebuild and restructure itself. That may apply to individuals, too.
"We may find ourselves downsizing, both personally and professionally, trimming the fat and getting back to a more manageable set of assets," she says. "On the individual scale, we will examine the balance in our lives between work and pleasure." Emphasis will be on the quality of the work environment and more income-producing work may be conducted from homes, she says.
"All in all, I believe 1998 will call upon us to restructure our lives and relationships so as to make room for personal freedom," she says."
Astrology will continue to grow in influence, says Ms. Diserio, attributing this to the upcoming millenium, a growth in insecurity and the appeal of everything New Age.
Although Jan. 1 is a natural time to re-evaluate your life, she says, the new moon each month, particulary in your birth month, is another chance for renewal.
Does Genie expect to make believers out of skeptics? Perhaps.
"You can come in as a cynic and a skeptic and everything," she says of the Astronet cyberworld, "but like with anything in life, you have to keep an open mind. Otherwise, you're cheating yourself. You're saying your instincts hold no weight."

HEADLINE: Veterans at Work, Building New Lives

BYLINE: By ROBIN F. DeMATTIA 

 BODY:
TOM SMITH of New Britain was a door gunner in the Army from 1969 through 1971. When his helicopter's engine failed near the Cambodian border and he had to wait for it to be repaired, he said he had time to explore and chat with people.
Mr. Smith, having always collected rocks and geodes, was intrigued, he remembers, by a piece of orange calcite that an old man was holding. Though they didn't speak the same language, the old man recognized Mr. Smith's interest and gave him the rock. 
 "That little gift kind of kept my sanity," Mr. Smith recalls. "I carried it with me all around Vietnam, and I explained the geology of the area to the people I flew with. It kept my humanity in an inhumane situation."
More than 25 years later, rocks are still helping Mr. Smith cope with his Vietnam experience. A disabled veteran who suffers from post-traumatic stress disorder, Mr. Smith last year founded Rox & Clox Etc., a company that employs disabled veterans to create "gifts from our earth."
The products are assembled at the Veterans Administration Community Care Center in West Haven and the North Hampton Veterans Administration and sold throughout the state. Though the company is not yet making a profit, there are other, perhaps more important, benefits.
"A lot of veterans are psychologically still focused in the past," explains Dr. Laurie Harkness, director of the Community Care Center and former director of the Veterans Administration stress disorder clinic. "One of the two main ways we know to bring them home and help them have meaning in the present is having work activities and doing something productive or giving back to others. Work is our tool for therapy."
Making the products for Rox & Clox -- agate pen holders, clock and pen sets, pendulum clocks, night lights, window watchers, Christmas tree ornaments and key chains -- is not the only job at the Community Care Center. Dr. Harkness says the group has contracts with Target Drugs, Sears, CVS, and Craftsman, among others, to do packaging, assembly, labeling and switch work.
"We're the pedicure kings of the world," she laughs, alluding to the amount of personal grooming sets the veterans work with. After receiving care for their acute issues, the veterans enter the jobs program and are paid based on their productivity, motivation and skills, with most averaging around $200 a week.
At any given time 40 to 50 veterans statewide participate in the jobs workshop, usually staying in the program for three to six months before moving on to jobs at the Veterans Administration hospital -- in engineering services, housekeeping, pharmacy, fire and safety -- and then positions in the community.
"We place approximately 58 percent of the veterans, the second-highest in the country," Dr. Harkness states.
The jobs workshop program, which is three years old, did $500,000 worth of business in 1996, $740,000 in 1997 and will soon be a million-dollar business, according to Dr. Harkness.
The Rox & Clox endeavor that Mr. Smith started is just another example of how the veterans program is ongoing, Dr. Harkness notes. "Vietnam veterans are finally saying 'I want to make a difference. I've lived with my symptoms and substance abuse for 20 years and I want to change that.' "
Mr. Smith agrees with that analysis. Having been homeless twice during the 1990's and having battled an alcohol dependency, he says the work "keeps me busy and productive and not obsessing about being disabled and thinking the world's terrible."
And he wants to pass along that help. "My vision is to bring geology to people and to help my fellow veterans out," says Mr. Smith, who holds a master's degree in geology from West Virginia University.
He started by enlisting the support of Vince Putignano of Norwalk, a disabled Vietnam veteran who served in the Marine Corps. Mr. Putignano, who had congestive heart failure in 1991 and cancer he attributes to exposure to Agent Orange, at one time owned United States Business Products in Westchester County, employing 165 people.
After his heart attack, his business failed and he spent several years unemployed. Mr. Smith's venture proved just the activity to help rejuvenate his business interests.
"Because of my business experience, I recognize the need for self-discipline in life and I bring that to Rox & Clox," he explains. "There are things I don't like to do, but I do them and the other guys see that and it rubs off."
He adds that veterans helping other veterans is key: "One of the aspects of someone suffering from post-traumatic stress syndrome is that they have a real big authority problem. So if something is dictated to them, they're not as affable at accepting that premise. But if they see a fellow veteran do that, they may say 'I'd like to do that too.' If they see a vet with the same problems and nightmares being productive, it spurs them on to copy the activity and get involved themselves."
Dr. Harkness echoes that thought. "The work setting is like a big family, with the veterans helping each other. They think 'If I don't show up today, so-and-so will worry about me. So though I'd rather stay in bed today, I'll go to work.' "
Another key, for the Rox & Clox business, is that the veterans are making products that people want. The items are sold in the Connecticut Made store in Waterbury, were featured in several shopping malls this holiday season, and are beginning to appear in stores like Cape Cod Crafters in Orange.
Robin Hoffemberg, owner of the Conversation Pieces gift store in Wilton, recently starting selling geodes cut and polished by the group. "The geodes make nice bookends and are pretty to look at," she says, "but they're also good for people who have a social conscience and want to buy things that will help people. It's nice to help the veterans not only financially but in expressing their creative ways."
Mr. Smith and Mr. Putignano also arranged to be vendors at large companies, selling to employees during lunch and coffee breaks.
"We have a lot of vendors who mostly sell jewelry and we were looking for something different," says Debbie Fouche, manager of the Aetna Employee Store in Hartford. "We like the Rox & Clox story, the fact that Tom is a veteran and he hires other disabled veterans. We've had very positive reaction from our employees and many requests to have them back."
The Rox & Clox goals, besides getting out of the red financially, include officially establishing the venture as a nonprofit organization and then obtaining support from the Small Business Administration.
"It's been an out-of-pocket venture so far, so things have been a little tight," Mr. Putignano says. "But we have to show some kind of a track record before we can sit down with anybody and ask them to help us build what we want to do."
In the long term, Mr. Smith and Mr. Putignano envision working with disabled veterans groups in each state that would function under their auspices and sell similar products in their state stores.
"Everyone would have a job and be paid," Mr. Putignano enthuses. "They'd make themselves a couple of bucks and they'd build their self-esteem and self-image."
Dr. Harkness believes their goals are achievable. "Tom is very committed and he's got a great cause," he said. "If anybody's going to do it, he'll do it."
And as more and more disabled veterans take on such roles, Dr. Harkness hopes, "the old stigma of the Vietnam veteran as being this dysfunctional person is going to largely go by the wayside."

GRAPHIC: Photos: At the Aetna Employee Store in Hartford, Tom Smith, right, and Vince Pitignano, both Vietnam veterans, show a customer a selection of Rox & Clox products, made by themselves and other veterans. Rox & Clox highlights the beauty of natural rock forms in useful items. (Photographs by Steve Miller for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Streetscapes/The Jones Wood Gardens;
A Leafy East Side Enclave With Just 12 Keys

BYLINE: By CHRISTOPHER GRAY 

 BODY:
THERE are only 12 keys that will get you into the Jones Wood Gardens, which is why only a few New Yorkers know about the leafy backyard enclave established between Lexington and Third Avenues from 65th and 66th Streets in 1920.
Now the owners are concerned that an apartment project replacing several buildings at the northwest corner of 65th Street and Third Avenue, one of which contains the Sign of the Dove restaurant, will compromise this special place. 
 Post-Civil War construction brought brownstone development up the spine of the East Side, especially along the line of the horsedrawn rail cars on Third Avenue. The car depot occupied the entire east blockfront of Third Avenue, from 65th to 66th Streets. In 1868, Jacob Korn, a Warren Street cloth dealer, built up the west blockfront with 10 small four-story apartment houses, designed by John B. Snook. The buildings had one family to a floor and a store below.
Snook also designed 13 rowhouses on the side streets, 157-167 East 65th Street and 154-166 East 66th Street. There was nothing special about these three-story and basement brownstones and nobody of renown lived there. A typical early tenant was Moses Lauterbach, a cattle dealer, who had been living on Second Avenue and moved into 167 East 65th Street. All the buildings were rentals.
In 1919, The New York Times reflected in an article that "for years the private dwelling was considered a very poor asset from a real estate standpoint."
"The 10- and 12-story apartment house, with its up-to-date improvements and service, was responsible for this condition of affairs," The Times asserted.
But World War I brought more people into the city while creating shortages of the building materials and labor necessary for the new wave of apartments. So investors had begun to look with new eyes on existing housing stock, with specific results for the 65th and 66th Street houses.
The 1919 article reported that a syndicate headed by the architect Edward Shepard Hewitt had acquired 12 of the 13 houses and would remake them to face not the streets, but a "huge sunken garden" in the rear, measuring 100 by 108 feet. These houses had kitchens and dining rooms on ground floor, libraries and parlors on the second; and seven bedrooms and four baths on the upper two floors.
Hewitt, working with the architect William Emerson as his associate, removed the high stoops and shaved off most of the facade detail, which was becoming the preferred solution to economically modernizing old brownstones.
The Times said the garden foliage "will look best in winter" and a 1922 article in House Beautiful said that the plantings had been selected for "hardy growth and easy maintenance" while "giving to the city house some of the advantages of the country estate." Early photographs show mostly grass plots and evergreens with a network of flagstone paths.
No credit for a landscape architect appears in period articles; presumably Hewitt was familiar with the common-garden idea through his friend and sometime partner, William Lawrence Bottomley. Bottomley was already at work on the much better known Turtle Bay Gardens, running from 48th to 49th Street and Third to Second Avenues.
The early owners of the 65th and 66th Street houses were quiet professional people, like Gordon Knox Bell, an attorney at Mitchell, Taylor, Capron & Marsh, who bought 154 East 66th Street, and Franklin B. Lord, fourth generation in his family to practice law at Lord, Day & Lord, who bought 160 East 66th Street. Bell moved from an old town house at 72d Street and Park Avenue; Lord had been living in Cedarhurst, L.I. As with other such developments, the purchasers were all subject to an agreement governing their use of the common garden.
EARLY magazine and news articles refer to the Hewitt project only as the "65th and 66th Street Gardens" and the renewed garden agreement of 1950 is the earliest surviving document with the name Jones Wood Association. That name recalls Jones's Wood, a weekend and summer pleasure ground a few blocks to the northeast that was popular in the mid-19th century.
The Third Avenue carbarns lasted until the late 1940's, when they were demolished for the New York Life Insurance Company's huge, white brick Manhattan House, running to Second Avenue. In planning Manhattan House, New York Life had purchased key parcels surrounding its building as light protectors, including 1116 and 1118 Third Avenue.
These had the effect of protecting the east light to the Jones Wood Gardens, too, and, after the building boom of the 50's and 60's, the Jones Wood block emerged as one of the few low-rise blocks left on the East Side, with the St. Vincent Ferrer Church complex on the Lexington Avenue frontage.
But New York Life sold its buildings in 1989 and now The Related Companies, a development group, has control over several of the Third Avenue properties, including the Sign of the Dove restaurant. David Wine, president of the company's residential development division, said that an as-of-right, 31-story luxury condominium, with perhaps 100 apartments, is now planned.
"We are working with members of the community, notably people who own the gardens to the west, to make sure the siting of the tower is responsive both to their needs and our needs" said Mr. Wine.
He declined to specify which properties he controls, but Lawrence Hickey, president of the Jones Wood Association, said that it includes 1110-1118 Third Avenue and 166 East 66th Street, the only rowhouse not included in the Jones Wood Gardens.
He said that the Jones Wood Association fought some neighborhood development in the 80's, like the apartment house at 200 East 65th Street and a possible redevelopment of the armory at 66th Street and Lexington Avenue. But he is avoiding an adversary tone, or even discussing exactly how the homeowners plan to go ahead.
BUT there are several ways the matter might proceed. The association might extract what concessions it can from the builder, although they are probably going to be slight, even though the new tower will almost certainly trade on the view into the Jones Wood Gardens.
A more surprising scenario might be for the owners to sell their collective air rights to the builder. That could establish a chain for the St. Vincent Ferrer complex to transfer its air rights also; Mr. Hickey is president of the St. Vincent Ferrer Parish Council. Some residents have talked about last- minute landmark designation for the garden and the Third Avenue tenements, but that is a distant possibility, since the garden is private and was planned without formal reliance on the low-rise buildings on Third.
It is even conceivable that, from a design point of view, a new apartment tower might be good for Jones Wood Gardens, in the way that new buildings sometimes leaven the old. But the garden seems like one of the last civilized spots in New York: a quiet, intimate green well surrounded by low buildings on all sides, a place where it is hard to calculate any improvement.

HEADLINE: The Changing Face of Park Avenue

BYLINE: By DAVID W. DUNLAP 

 BODY:
BANKING on Park Avenue? Naturally. Merging and acquiring? Of course. But shopping? Who would think of Park Avenue as a retail destination?
A growing number of customers, to judge from the crowds in the new four-level Syms clothing store at 400 Park Avenue, on the corner of 54th Street. "We have been very pleased with the performance of the store in its first year," said Marcy Syms, president and chief operating officer of Syms Corporation. 
 Three blocks south, patrons huddle with their cell phones while being fitted for made-to-measure suits in Saint Laurie Merchant Tailors at No. 350. After 84 years downtown, Saint Laurie has found Park Avenue to its liking. "My sales are up about 30 percent," said the chairman, Andrew Kozinn. (Granted, that is a difference of only three suits a day, but at prices ranging up to $1,095, it is no small achievement.)
"Look at the natural light coming in," he said. "Look at the dignity of the area, how pleasant it is, how convenient. So what if Park is not a retail strip the way other sections are? That's almost inconsequential."
Park Avenue is not in danger any time soon of being mistaken for a mall. But it is changing. Best known as a corporate canyon extending north from Grand Central Terminal, linking the blue-chip world to the blue-blooded one, Park Avenue is assuming a more public face. Ground-floor spaces that were once the undisputed province of understated financial institutions are increasingly tenanted by glitzy showrooms, upscale merchants, chain stores and even -- gasp! -- a restaurant.
At the depth of the recession, about 70 percent of the retail space on Park Avenue south of 51st Street was empty or available, said Robert L. Billingsley, executive vice president of Colliers ABR, agents for several of the towers. Today, there are few vacancies. "The mausoleums have all filled up," Mr. Billingsley said.
With good reason. Rents on Park Avenue might run around $100 to $150 a square foot, one half to two thirds the cost of comparable space on Madison Avenue, said Kate D. Coburn, director of retail services at Cushman & Wakefield. And pedestrian traffic is more than ample.
"There is a constant stream of people on Park Avenue that's been ignored for a while," Ms. Coburn said. "The north end has had retail. It's now moving south."
New Yorkers could sip the trend themselves beginning in 1996 when a Starbucks opened at 430 Park Avenue, near 55th Street. Across the avenue, the Staples office-product chain planted its bright-red corporate flag.
Two months ago, Borders Books and Music opened a four-level store in the Ritz Tower at 57th Street. And earlier this month, Audi opened a seven-car showroom at No. 250, at 47th Street, as the first of what it said will be a worldwide network of "showcase dealerships."
The year ahead promises more big changes:
* Direct entrances to the Metro-North train platforms at Grand Central Terminal are to be opened at Park Avenue and 48th Street, Madison Avenue and 47th Street and in the pedestrian walkways that run through the Helmsley Building, at 46th Street.
* A condominium apartment tower, the first new building along this stretch of Park Avenue in almost two decades, is to be built at the southeast corner of 60th Street by Zeckendorf Realty and a Whitehall Street Real Estate Fund managed by Goldman Sachs & Company.
* Xerox Corporation will open a 20,000-square-foot showroom on 47th Street at the base of the Bear Stearns Building, No. 245, where the lobby and plaza are also undergoing a $13 million renovation.
* Lever House will be looking for a new tenant after 600 Lever Brothers employees move to Connecticut. The Lever Brothers, Chesebrough-Pond and Helene Curtis units are being joined to form Unilever Home and Personal Care USA, with offices in Greenwich and Trumbull.
Some 100 employees of the parent company, Unilever, will stay on Park Avenue for the time being, but the future occupancy of the glass-walled landmark at No. 390, between 53d and 54th Streets, is in question.
(So, too, is the fate of the charming Christmas carousel that has been on display for 45 holiday seasons. "We're going to pack it away with every intention that it will be there for the 46th year," said John T. Gould Jr., a Unilever spokesman. But the tradition is threatened by the impending departure of Lever Brothers and the death earlier this year of Jack Lowery, who designed the carousel each year.)
WHILE Lever Brothers moves off the avenue, the Colgate-Palmolive Company is to stay in place at No. 300 into the next century, as its 41-year-old headquarters building gets a much-needed renovation. Already, new mahogany paneling can be seen in the lobby, between 49th and 50th Streets.
Colgate's lease, which was to have expired in 2000, has been extended to 2010, said Jennifer Marcus Barbara, a spokeswoman.
As part of that renewal agreement, the Uris estate, which owns the building, has undertaken an $18 million upgrade. The tower is getting "a whole new inside," said Joseph C. Peters, senior managing director of Cushman & Wakefield, the managing and rental agent for the building.
Besides the work in the lobby, the heating, ventilating and air-conditioning systems are being modernized, elevators are being speeded up and electrical power is being boosted. Roofs and sidewalks are also being repaired. The renovation is by Swanke Hayden Connell Architects, which also designed the reconstruction of 320 Park Avenue as the new headquarters of the Mutual of America Life Insurance Company.
Both 300 and 320 Park Avenue were originally designed by Emery Roth & Sons, as were a majority of the 23 office towers built after World War II between 46th and 60th Streets. Park Avenue was the vineyard where the shapes of Roth were stored, built in an extraordinarily coherent style, love it or hate it: the corporate glass box.
Because image was so important at these buildings, no retailers needed apply. That legacy will endure at the Zeckendorf tower, which is to rise 35 or 36 stories.
"To create a great residential building, retail is not appropriate," said Arthur W. Zeckendorf, a principal in Zeckendorf Realty. The developers would prefer to have a grand lobby and a sitting or dining room.
Clearly aiming for the top of the market, the Zeckendorf project will have only one apartment per floor, ranging from about 5,200 square feet (five bedrooms) to 3,000 square feet (four bedrooms) on the uppermost floors. Ceilings will be 10 feet high and living rooms will be 20 by 30 feet. Condominium units like these might sell between $1,000 and $2,000 a square foot.
THE new building will replace a 12-story structure at No. 515 that was built as an apartment house in 1912, to designs by Denby & Nute, and converted to office use in 1958. It once housed the World Zionist Organization but has been abandoned for years. Demolition is to begin soon.
Frank Williams & Associates, an architectural firm whose credits include the Four Seasons and Rihga Royal Hotels, is designing the Zeckendorf building. "We are going to try hard to draw heavily on prewar buildings," said William Lie Zeckendorf, a principal in Zeckendorf Realty. The facade will be clad in brick, cast stone or limestone.
The Zeckendorfs would not would not assign a development cost to the project, in part because plans are still being drawn up. They said the project did not need any zoning variances or special permits.
Their building will sit in a transitional zone between mid-Manhattan and the Upper East Side. It was just this mix that drew Borders Books and Music, based in Ann Arbor, Mich., to the Ritz Tower at No. 461.
"We're the most conveniently located book-music superstore for one of the finest, most educated populations in the world," said Robert F. DiRomualdo, chairman and chief executive of Borders. In addition, he said, office workers fill the place at lunchtime and tourists provide steady traffic.
While Borders has more than 185 stores nationwide, it tailors its inventory to some degree. Mr. DiRomualdo said the 42,600-square-foot Park Avenue store would feature literature, business, computers, arts and fine arts. "If we don't sell more furnishing magazines and home decor books there than in any other store in our company, I'd be surprised," he said.
Drawing on the same well-to-do clientele, Simply Caviar, a mail-order house based in Long Island City, Queens, will open a 60-seat restaurant and food store in February in No. 350, next door to Saint Laurie. A private dining room with room for 20 is to open later, said Irvin Crusoe, a partner in Simply Caviar with David Mills. The architects are Yui Bloch Design.
Both the dining room and store will specialize in caviar, smoked salmon, foie gras and lobster. "A lot of people in the area indulge in that sort of thing," Mr. Crusoe explained. "They want to have caviar and Scottish salmon for lunch. Now they can walk downstairs." At dinner, he said, the restaurant expects to attract guests from nearby hotels.
The hotel trade has proved to be a boon for Saint Laurie, said Mr. Kozinn, its chairman. "I'm getting tourists that I never got before," he said. "South Americans. Midwesterners. Staying at the Waldorf. Staying at the Palace. I guess they don't have stores where they come from."
Saint Laurie is a small (2,700-square-foot) but signficant addition to Park Avenue -- first, because it has pushed retail farther south and second, because it has been a downtown fixture since it was founded in 1913 as the Independent Trouser Company by Mr. Kozinn's grandfather, Samuel Kozinsky. Indeed, its office and factory remain at 895 Broadway, near 20th Street.
The new store was designed by Milton Glaser and features a giant yellow tape measure on the wall. Tabletops brim with bolts of heathery tweeds and silk-like "Super 100" wools, as well as cashmeres, cheviots, saxonies and flannels.
It is a far cry from a teller's cage.
"If you were an owner on Park Avenue, your choice upfront would probably always be a financial institution," said David A. Green, senior managing director in the retail division of the Insignia/Edward S. Gordon Company, agents for 350 Park Avenue.
"With the downsizing in the financial industry," he said, "there just haven't been enough players to fill the space on the ground floors of these buildings."
Mr. Billingsley, of Colliers ABR, does not count out financial institutions -- "Banking is not all going to be done in a match box," he said -- but neither does he deny the retail allure of the Park Avenue denizen.
"Wouldn't you love to have an Audi dealership and all those guys with their year-end bonuses strolling across the street?" he asked. Colliers is the rental agent at No. 250, where the corner space had been empty four or five years after the Banco di Sicilia relocated in the building. Mr. Billingsley said he had been approached by discount drugstores and "gourmet" delis but turned them away. Then came Audi.
"The fact that the banks were no longer moving into these locations made this an almost classic institutional use," said Leon Casper, senior director at Cushman & Wakefield, which represented Audi. At 47th Street, a showroom would be seen by exactly the right audience for a luxury automaker: commuters on their way to Westchester County and Connecticut.
AUDI, a unit of Volkswagen A.G., plans to build showrooms around the world to serve not only as retail dealerships but as high-profile display cases. The New York showroom is the first. "There was something natural about being on Park Avenue," said Kenneth J. Moriarty, marketing director of Audi of America, referring to the BMW Gallery and Mercedes-Benz showroom, designed by Frank Lloyd Wright.
The 6,450-square-foot Audi Park Avenue showroom, as it is called, opened Dec. 5. It was designed by Jon Greenberg & Associates and Grid/3 International.
Another showroom, for Xerox products, is to open across the avenue next June in a space once occupied by the Bank of New York. Within the showroom, but visible from the street, will be a curving wall with an illuminated version of the digital "X" trademark and corporate motto, "The Document Company." There will also be an array of video screens. The design is by Ideo Environments Group and Highland Associates.
At the same time, a renovation is proceeding on the lobby and plaza at No. 245, which is expected to lose its main tenant, Bear Stearns, when its lease expires in 2002.
"We're trying to make the building accessible and welcoming to people and to enliven that very important address," said Raul de Armas of Moed de Armas & Shannon, architects of the renovation.
Among other features, the brick-covered plaza will be repaved in granite and the entrance will get a new facade of sheer glass. A larger lobby is being created out of what had been a long corridor. The ceiling of the lobby will be clad in anegre, a honey-colored hardwood, and will seem to glow from within, as it will be brightly illuminated from below.
Besides its showroom at No. 245, Xerox is also taking 100,000 square feet of office space in the building, which is owned by World Financial Properties, successor to Olympia & York. Xerox is consolidating operations there from 135 East 57th Street, 201 East 42d Street and 40 Rector Street.
A critical factor in the choice of 245 Park Avenue was the fact that 69 percent of Xerox's Manhattan work force comes to work through Grand Central, either as suburban or subway commuters, said Joseph A. Catalano, vice president and general manager for New York City operations.
Not many months after they arrive at No. 245, Xerox workers who come into Grand Central on Metro-North -- as well as the 35,000 to 40,000 other commuters who work north of the terminal -- should find one leg of their daily journey easier and faster than they had ever imagined.
Commuters with uptown destinations will no longer have to walk south from their platforms into the terminal, then reverse course, head up the escalators and swarm through the lobby of the Met Life Building.
Instead, they will be able to pop up into the sidewalks above -- almost like cosmopolitan prairie dogs -- through four new northern entrances to the platforms.
The $87.7 million construction project, known as "North End Access," may be completed by November, said Donald N. Nelson, the president of Metro-North.
"We will be reaching way out with this network," Mr. Nelson said. "We have high hopes of increasing ridership significantly. We believe there are a lot of commuters who drive and park in the upper 50's and 60's rather than come into the terminal and walk or take the subway whom we can now nudge onto the trains."
An average of 10 minutes of travel time might be shaved off the trip, said Donna Evans, a spokeswoman for Metro-North, adding, "There's no train operation we could do that would save that much time."
When the project is complete, it will be possible to enter Grand Central from as far north as 48th Street, through a small glass-enclosed structure outside the Westvaco Building, at 299 Park Avenue. A construction fence now marks the spot.
A SECOND entrance will be built on the Madison Avenue side of the Chase Manhattan Bank headquarters, 270 Park Avenue, at 47th Street. This entrance, which is also currently marked by a fence, will have an elevator.
Two more entrances will be tucked into the sides of the "Helmsley Walks," running through the Helmsley Building, between 45th and 45th Streets.
The entrances will be linked to the platforms through a cross-hatched network of two north-south "spines" and two east-west "cross passageways."
The spines are platforms that were taken out of service along Tracks 22 and 31.
The 45th Street passageway has been created from a former baggage and mail tunnel that runs under the lower level tracks. It is about 400 feet long and is connected to 11 lower-level platforms. It is so far along in construction that terrazzo flooring and wall panels are already in place at the west end, under Track 117.
The 47th Street passageway is entirely new, shoehorned structurally below the upper-level tracks, with enough clearance to permit passage of trains on the lower level. "We're using all the available space -- down to the inch," said John R. Seaboldt, deputy director of facilities engineering. The passageway is about 700 feet long and is connected to 15 upper-level platforms.
Wherever a platform is connected to a passageway, there is an enclosure made of glass block, the first visible sign to railroad passengers of a project that is actually unfolding all around them.
For a project of such complexity, with so many far-reaching implications, North End Access has proceeded with an astonishingly low profile. "The only disruption for our customers has been a more frequent change of platform assignment," Mr. Nelson said. "It's been almost invisible from the street." That will soon change.

SECTION: Section 14NJ; Page 12; Column 3; New Jersey Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 965 words

HEADLINE: ART REVIEWS;
Elevation of the Everyday, as Glorified by Pop

BYLINE: By BARRY SCHWABSKY

DATELINE: NEW BRUNSWICK

 BODY:
EVERYONE knows that with Pop Art, everyday, mass-produced objects became subject matter for art. Think of Andy Warhol's paintings of Campbell's Soup cans, or Claes Oldenburg's monumental sculptures of clothespins and the like. But that aspect of Pop Art can be seen as just a logical extension of the traditional still life genre. What's often forgotten is the more radical gesture by which Pop artists turned art into something very much like an everyday, mass-produced object.
The story of that gesture is ably recounted in "The Great American Pop Art Store: Multiples of the 60's," at the Zimmerli Art Museum here. The exhibition was organized by the University Art Museum at California State University at Long Beach, whose director, Constance W. Glenn, was the curator. 
 It includes items like shopping bags and wallpaper (by both Warhol and the late Roy Lichtenstein), china tableware and paper plates (Lichtenstein again). Others could easily be classified as toys (Robert Watts's plastic "Egg Box With Eggs," 1964) or among those miscellaneous little mass-produced curiosities, neither functional nor esthetic in intention, that my grandmother used to call tchotchkes: Mr. Oldenburg's painted cast plaster "Fire Plug Souvenir -- Chicago August 1968," or Alan D'Arcangelo's "Side-View Mirror" (1966), with one of his signature open highway images screen-printed in.
Of course, many of the objects are more obviously representative of "that old thing, art" (to borrow the title of the French critic Roland Barthes's famous essay on Pop). Here are all kinds of prints -- silk screens, etchings, lithographs -- and editioned sculptures made through traditional casting processes, as well as books ranging from Jim Dine's lush artist's book "The Picture of Dorian Gray" (1968) to Ed Ruscha's cheaply offset photo books like "26 Gasoline Stations" (1963) and "Every Building on the Sunset Strip" (1966).
The reasons for the explosion of multiples in the 1960's were many, ranging from the politically idealistic to the economically mercenary, and the Pop artists were hardly the only ones on the bandwagon. But it was all made possible by a radical questioning of both the nature of the art object and of the role of the artist, and in Pop Art the multiple as a method was bonded to mass culture as a subject in an unusually close way.
For one thing, the market in original paintings and sculpture is still based on an aristocratic model of social and economic relations. Jasper Johns remarked that "artists are the elite of the servant class," and the accuracy of his observation would have been recognized by courtier-artists like Van Dyck or Velazquez in the 17th century. But its embrace of the multiple showed that Pop Art envisioned not only a broader audience for its work, but also a different, more impersonal relation with that audience, now conceived more as customers than as patrons. To attract them, art had to become its own packaging.
Perhaps Warhol's and Lichtenstein's shopping bags are the emblematic items in this show. They practically cry out to be filled with as many of the others as possible and taken home. In Pop Art, the manufactured object as such becomes the most glamorous, desirable thing imaginable. Even in Tom Wesselman's "Little Nude" (1966), it is the voluptuous sheen of spray-painted, vacuum-formed Plexiglas that lends its aura to those rosy lips and nipples, and not vice versa.
C ERTAINLY it is hard to pass through here without feeling the stirrings of the possessive urge -- an urge that can be all the more difficult to suppress when its objects are many and affordable, rather than rare and unattainable. Who wouldn't want to live with such witty, thought-provoking works as Lichtenstein's eye-dazzling relief "Seascape (II)," from 1965, or Mr. Oldenburg's "Miniature Soft Drum Set" (1969)? Not to mention the 1966 "Bacon, Lettuce and Tomato Sandwich" (colored wax encased in clear, bread-shaped plastic) by the underrated Watts, an artist who is more often identified with the Fluxus group but participated in many of the early Pop exhibitions. Such works are touching souvenirs of art.
A pile of Warhol's silk-screened "Brillo Boxes," from a group made in 1969, are the first and last thing we see at "The Great American Pop Art Store." Much ink has been spilled over the implications of the supposed indistiguishability of these objects from the manufacturer's originals. Seeing them once again serves as a reminder of how misleading that line of thought is. No one could possibly mistake the Warhol box for the real one, if only because the former is so obviously made of wood and not cardboard. Just as surely as any bronze statesman, something relatively ephemeral has been monumentalized by representation in a more durable material. Warhol eulogized consumer culture, yet he did so in a form that resists consumption, after all.
In Pop Art, consumer culture came to reflect upon itself self-consciously, and that self-consciousness was at once celebratory and uneasy -- uneasy because the very fact of self-consciousness contradicts thoughtless consumption even as its allure is acknowledged and appeased. Perhaps that's why so many of these objects seem seductive but hollow; in fact, they seem all the more seductive, fun and beautiful because of their obvious hollowness. "What's the difference between Warhol's 'Brillo Box' and the one in the store?" a friend once asked. The answer: 'Warhol's is the one that's empty."
 
THE GREAT AMERICAN POP ART STORE: MULTIPLES OF THE 60's
Jane Voorhees Zimmerli Art Museum
Rutgers University, George and Hamilton Streets, New Brunswick
Through Feb. 22. Hours: Tuesdays through Fridays, 10 A.M. to 4:30 P.M.; Saturdays and Sundays, noon to 5 P.M.

PUB-DATE: December 28, 1997

SECTION: Section 14NJ; Page 4; Column 1; New Jersey Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 1911 words

HEADLINE: IN PERSON;
Help From the Casino Floor

BYLINE: By BILL KENT 

DATELINE: ATLANTIC CITY

 BODY:
IT'S not quite a question of who has been naughty or nice. Redenia Gilliam-Mosee doesn't see herself as a pin-striped Santa Claus, even if many people here do.
"I like to joke that even though she's paid by the casinos, She's done so much good in the city she might as well be working for me," Mayor James Whelan said. 
 Robert Ruffalo, president of the city's Historical Society, characterizes her as "a person who never stops giving."
"She has made herself absolutely essential to just about everything positive that's being done here," he said. "From a historical point of view, this city wouldn't have come half as far as it has without her."
The fact that Ms. Gilliam-Mosee, vice president for governmental relations and planning for the Hilton and Bally's Park Place casinos hotels, typically gives away $400,000 of her employer's money a year to more than 50 charitable organizations and non-profit community groups in the region is "just one part of larger responsibility," she said.
"I'm the way the community touches this company. I'm the way the employees of this company can see that their jobs are having a positive effect on the community. I'm the way that this company can get whatever needs to be done involving government as efficiently as possible.
"And I live here. So when somebody talks about the impact of gaming on the community, they're talking about my backyard. The gaming industry made a lot of promises to the community when gambling was approved in Atlantic City. I'm here to see that those promises are kept."
Such a statement sounds improbable in a city that has yet to become the vacation paradise New Jersey residents envisioned when they approved casino gambling in 1976. And yet, in her two decades as Bally's and Hilton's government and community liaison, Ms. Gilliam-Mosee has used her casinos' money and influence to keep that goal in sight.
In 1982, she persuaded Bally's to spend $5.8 million on what became the city's first low- and middle-income housing project financed by casinos, the Jacobs Family Terrace at Baltic and Kentucky Avenues. When local banks cited credit problems as an excuse not to lend money to low-income families who wanted to buy houses in Atlantic City, Ms. Gilliam-Mosee set up a program in which Bally guaranteed low-interest mortgages to them, as well as casino employees. She made Bally's Park Place the first casino to adopt a public school in the city, giving the Indiana Avenue elementary school everything from computer equipment to carpeting. In the last five years, she has distributed more than $300,000 from the Bally and Hilton casinos to restore the Pop Lloyd Baseball Stadium. She sits on the boards of six community and regional organizations and is a member of two dozen others. She is supervising a $2.8 million residential renovation at Atlantic and Boston Avenues sponsored by the Atlantic City Hilton.
Other casinos employ community and government liaisons who have made similar contributions of time and money. (Both Caesars and Harrah's have sponsored housing projects.) But Ms. Gilliam-Mosee's role "has put Bally in the lead," said City Council Vice President Lorenzo Langford.
"The other casinos could take a lesson from Bally and find qualified local people and employ them in such influential positions," said Mr. Langford, who has been a consistent critic of the casino industry's tendency to hire outside the city. "A lot of her zeal is that due to the fact that she's home-grown."
Wallace E. Barr, who heads both the Bally and Hilton casinos, said he had never opposed or even disagreed with Ms. Gilliam-Mosee's use of his casinos' money in the 17 years he had known her.
"She understands our corporate philosophies, and she always does the right thing," Mr. Barr said. "Her relationship throughout the industry and the city, and among city officials, has enabled us to move efficiently and effectively in our public dealings."
It is this last characteristic that has made Ms. Gilliam's role as a government liaison so crucial. Other casinos have endured long, expensive legal battles over plans and policies affecting Atlantic City politicians, residents and businesses. These have ranged from Mirage Resorts' two-year struggle to win approval for a $330 million commuter tunnel through a middle-class neighborhood to Trump Hotels' embarrassing failure to acquire Sabatini's Italian restaurant to make room for the Trump Plaza casino expansion.
In comparison, the Bally and Hilton casinos have had no visible opposition to several casino expansion plans, most recently the extension of a Michigan Avenue parking garage that required the demolition of St. Andrew's by-the-Sea Lutheran Church in September.
"Leaving a historic location was not an easy decision for the congregation to make," said the Rev. Neil Jaggie, St. Andrew's interim pastor. "But Bally's was a fine organization to negotiate with and do business with, and the sale and the arrangements went through to the congregation's satisfaction."
It helped that Ms. Gilliam-Mosee was friendly with Charles Fischer, president of St. Andrew's congregation. "Redenia is a great human being," he said. "Bally was fair and square, and it worked to out to everyone's advantage. The congregation can now relocate to an area with more room, and Bally can have its transportation center."
Ms. Gilliam-Mosee said her friendship with Mr. Fischer "was an asset" but added, "I didn't do anything differently with St. Andrew's than I've done with any other member of the community that is effected by our business."
She said she went to every member of the congregation concerned with Bally's plans. "I respect people on their turf," she said. "It's my No. 1 rule. Atlantic City has always been a small town, and you can't hide in a small town. At least, I can't. These are people who know where I live, and I'm not about to move."
Ms. Gilliam-Mosee's residency here makes her unusual among upper-management casino executives. Although her salary in the middle six figures would permit her to live in an affluent suburban enclave far from the city, she and her husband, Israel, live in the Maryland Avenue rowhouse in which she grew up. In addition to renovating that row house, the Mosees have bought and renovated five other row houses as rental properties on Maryland Avenue between Arctic and Atlantic Avenues. Mr. Mosee, former manager of the city's bus terminal, now looks after their properties. They have no children.
"My family moved here from Newark when I was 10, and I grew up working in the hotels, as a chambermaid and an elevator operator," Ms. Gilliam-Mosee said. "I had the assumption, like most people who grew up here, that I had to go somewhere else if I wanted to do anything significant with my life. I had completed the work for my doctorate in city planning at Rutgers when I was hired as a consultant by Bally. I was aware that people had been losing money in Atlantic City casinos long before they were legalized, and I had initially been opposed to legalized gaming. I said to myself I would have to take a long look at the business, to see if it could really meet its potential, before I would ever get involved."
In 1978, "there were a lot of expectations about the gaming that were founded on misunderstandings," she said. "People were under the assumption that the casinos were outsiders coming into the community, when in reality most of the employees were local or regional people, and still are. Many of them I had known since childhood, and they wanted to use the economic engine that the casinos were providing to improve their lives. Their enthusiasm rubbed off on me, though even my mother told me I was crazy to live in the city. I told her I just had to live here."
"Besides," she added. "For what it cost me, I got a lot of house and enough left over to fix it up. I decided to set myself up for the long haul."
As a casino executive in Atlantic City, "I was there for anybody who rang my door," she said. "I also had to be careful about politics."
Because state law forbids senior executives of casinos from contributing to political campaigns in any way, Ms. Gilliam-Mosee would not permit her brother-in-law, former City Councilman Harold Mosee, to ride with in her car. "I had to be careful about that, and all the affairs and luncheons and honorary dinners my job required me to attend," she said. "I had to learn to take small bites."
She said she had not expected Atlantic City to open the way for legalized gambling in other states outside Nevada. "But now that it has, it is important to me that people understand that, as a business, gaming can give people the potential to improve their lives and their community," she said. "I've looked at this from every side of the issue, and to me it's not what hasn't happened to Atlantic City so far, but what is happening and what will happen because I want it to happen, our company wants it to happen, and the community wants it to happen."
But don't think of her as Santa Claus.
"Our company supports many activities with material and services that I don't expect to be acknowledged," she said. "But when I commit our company's money, I want to see our casino's name on the program, or included on the list of donors. People know me well enough to know that I do not buy favors or publicity. I want people to know that what this company is doing in the community is worth doing."
 
RESUME
Redenia Gilliam-Mosee
 
EDUCATION Graduated from Atlantic City High School. Bachelor's degree, magna cum laude, in economics and political science from Wilberforce University. Master's degree in city and regional planning from Rutgers University. Has completed course work toward a doctorate in city planning from Rutgers.
 
CURRENT JOB Vice president for government relations and planning, Bally's Park Place and Atlantic City Hilton casino hotels. As community and government liaison, she supervises the distribution of $400,000 a year in casino money to more than 50 regional charities, community groups, ethnic and cultural festivals.
 
COMMUNITY PROJECTS Planned and supervised the construction of Atlantic City's first casino-financed middle- and low-income housing project, Jacobs Family Terrace. Helped establish a low-interest mortgage program for casino employees who want to buy houses in the city. Made Bally's Park Place the first casino to adopt a city public school. Gave more than $300,000 to restore Pop Lloyd Baseball Stadium. Supervising a $2.8 31-unit residential renovation at Atlantic and Boston Avenues.
 
CASINO PROJECTS Supervised all government and community affairs projects involving regarding Bally's and Hilton casino operations, including the recent demolition of St. Andrew's by-the-Sea Lutheran Church for an expansion of Bally's parking garage.
 
AFFILIATIONS Chairwoman of the Atlantic-Cape May Private Industry Council. President of the Atlantic City Boys and Girls Club. Vice president of the Greater Atlantic City Chamber of Commerce. Board member of the Atlantic City Arts Center, Atlantic City Historical Museum, Atlantic City Medical Center, Ladies Professional Golf Association Advisory Board and St. James A.M.E. Church. Attends as many as five community meetings, business luncheons, honorary dinners and other civic gatherings each day. "If I can't make a meeting," she says, "I make it my business to find out what happens."

HEADLINE: MAKING IT WORK;
Cover to Cover

BYLINE: By ANDREW JACOBS 

 BODY:
For the first time in years, Tito Mahones can imagine a future. Over the last few months, the 40-year-old handyman who was profiled in "Tito Mahones: A Life on the Edge" (Aug. 13), has had a flood of work renovating the apartments of New Yorkers who are benefiting from the city's economic boom. He recently bought a used car for $100 and he occasionally hires friends to help him on jobs. His self-esteem, he said, is on the rise.
"I think I'm on a good track," he said, "but things aren't perfect." He still has no heat in his Bedford-Stuyvesant apartment. But with a steady income, Tito has been able to provide more support for his six children and he has begun patching things up with his ex-wife, the mother of his youngest child. There is even talk of getting back together. 
 "I think I've grown up more in the past six months than I have in six years," he said. ANDREW JACOBS

HEADLINE: EARNING IT;
Not Ready for Medicare, and Uninsured

BYLINE: By JULIA LAWLOR 

 BODY:
WITH nothing to protect her but a vial of heart medicine, Ruth Kain spent the last year and a half willing herself to stay well. Then, a week before Thanksgiving, she felt a crushing pain in her chest, jaw and left arm so severe that it took her breath away. She was taken to the hospital, where doctors discovered a major blockage in an artery.
Mrs. Kain, 64, of Ava, Mo., is one of millions of Americans aged 55 to 64 who find it difficult -- or impossible -- to obtain health insurance. While her pain has gone away, for now at least, her worries have not. Since her husband's retirement and the expiration of his employer-sponsored health insurance in 1995, Mrs. Kain has been unable to find coverage for her heart condition. 
 She estimates that her hospital stay last month will cost her $20,000, in addition to an $8,000 bill from last January when she had a pacemaker placed in her chest. Until she turns 65 next September and becomes eligible for Medicare, every penny must be paid out of her pocket.
"I have 10 more months of sitting on pins and needles, waiting and praying that nothing else happens to my heart," she said. "If it does, we'll have to mortgage our house. I have to stay healthy. That's all there is to it."
After focusing its efforts on getting coverage for millions of uninsured children, the White House is now turning its attention to the group of people typified by Mrs. Kain. Too young for Medicare, yet old enough to be forced into early retirement or pushed out the door in corporate downsizings, three million Americans between 55 and 65 now have no health insurance coverage, according to the Employee Benefit Research Institute in Washington.
And this is the fastest-growing age group of the American population, now at 21.5 million but expected to increase to 30 million by 2005 as baby boomers approach retirement age, the institute said. Recent research has shown that people in this group are losing health benefits at a faster rate than any other group, except children.
In some cases, this is because pre-existing health conditions disqualify them from some insurance plans. In others, insurance companies view them as high risks and charge more than they can afford. In still other instances, early retirees lose coverage when their former employers cancel retiree health benefits to cut costs.
In any case, the loss of coverage can mean disaster for people like Mrs. Kain. The cruel fact is that "people tend to get sicker when they get older," noted Richard Coorsh, a spokesman for the Health Insurance Association of America, which represents 250 of the nation's for-profit health insurance carriers.
According to Paul Fronstin, an economist at the Employee Benefit Research Institute, a majority of people in the 55-to-64 age group are working; it is those who have retired who are more likely to lack insurance.
And women have a greater chance than men of being uninsured, Mr. Fronstin said. In part, that is because they are more apt to have jobs that do not offer health insurance. Others have been carried on their husbands' policies, so after their husbands die, they are often left without coverage.
Mrs. Kain was a homemaker raising four children and working only occasional odd jobs during the 37 years that her husband, Rufus, was a maintenance worker for Louisiana Pacific. After she had heart bypass surgery at 39, she remained in relatively good health.
But the surgery came back to haunt her when she went shopping for health insurance after her husband's retirement. No company would insure her for anything related to her heart or circulatory system. So Mrs. Kain ended up buying a policy for $227 a month that excluded her pre-existing conditions. The Kains own their own home and have Mr. Kain's pension, so they are not eligible for Medicaid, the health program for the poor and disabled. Mr. Kain, however, is covered under Medicare.
In 1996, 13.9 percent of people aged 55 to 64 were uninsured, compared with 32.8 percent of 21- to 24-year-olds and 22.5 percent of 25- to 34-year-olds, according to the Employee Benefits Research Institute.
Some public policy experts argue that the numbers indicate that the health insurance woes of the near elderly are getting more attention than they deserve. David Helms, president of the Alpha Center, a nonprofit health policy center in Washington, said: "To me the most deserving group is low-income workers of all ages who aren't getting coverage through their employer. Many 55- to 64-year-olds are not low-income. We should be focusing where the need is greatest."
Others, however, say the near elderly end up suffering the most. Ronald F. Pollack, executive director of Families USA, a health care advocacy group, said, "This is the group that winds up having the most harmful consequences when they are uninsured."
Many older people have chronic health problems like diabetes, high blood pressure and cancer that are costly to treat. "When you're an uninsured adult, there is a very high likelihood that you will defer the care you need," Mr. Pollack said. "Sometimes, once you go to get help, it's too late."
THE problem could grow worse. While people are tending to retire at younger and younger ages -- today, the average retirement age is 62, and even lower at large companies -- a bipartisan Medicare commission is considering the option of phasing in an increase in the eligibility age for Medicare, to 67 from 65. Such a move would put even more people at risk of going uninsured, said John Rother, legislative director for the American Association of Retired Persons.
Once they are pressed into retirement or are laid off, many older workers have difficulty finding other jobs that offer health insurance coverage. Those who become self-employed can try to buy coverage on their own. Others take what they can get.
Barbara A. Pretti, 62, of Memphis, says she thinks some employers rejected her because of her age when she went job-hunting in 1993. She had lost her position as a buyer and assistant product manager at Holiday Inn Worldwide when the company moved to Atlanta. Under the Consolidated Omnibus Budget Reconciliation Act of 1986, or Cobra, she was able to continue her company health insurance plan for 18 months. But the Cobra coverage was expensive; Ms. Pretti paid $400 a month for the same coverage that cost her $96 a month as an employee.
Eventually, she found a new job in sales with a small company that offered no health insurance benefits. So when the Cobra period ran out, she went shopping again for insurance.
"It was a monster," Ms. Pretti recalled. Insurance companies turned her down because of surgery she had had on a broken ankle. Finally, she found a company that would insure her for $439 a month, with a $1,000 deductible. But money is so tight for her now that she tries not to use the coverage; she hasn't had a proper physical since 1991 because her policy has a $1,000 deductible.
Instead, her medical care now consists of visiting her cousin, a doctor, twice a year when he has a family party. "He stands at the door and takes everyone's blood pressure," Ms. Pretti said. Meanwhile, she has wiped out her savings paying $10,000 of dental bills for which she has no insurance.
"Right now I see no chance for retirement," said Ms. Pretti, who is divorced and lives alone. "Between the I.R.S. and the insurance companies, that's why I work."
INDEED, once Cobra coverage runs out, there are few good alternatives for people in the near-retirement age group.
The self-employed can obtain lower group rates by joining an association like the local Chamber of Commerce. In 13 states, including New York and New Jersey, "guarantee issue" laws require insurance companies that do business in the state to offer a policy to anyone, regardless of age or pre-existing conditions. But those policies can be expensive. Some states have created high-risk pools that let people who are disqualified by most insurers get policies. But this coverage, too, is often costly.
As health care costs skyrocketed in the early 90's, many employers reconsidered their health coverage for retired workers. A decade ago, 60 percent of large employers offered such coverage, according to Mr. Rother of the A.A.R.P. That has dropped to 40 percent today, he said.
James Huth, 55, said he was pushed into early retirement last year by the Pabst Brewing Company in Milwaukee, where he had worked for 32 years. Soon after, he said, he learned that his retiree health benefits had been eliminated. With a monthly pension of just $1,045, Mr. Huth, who suffers from a heart condition and diabetes, could not afford coverage on his own; the bill would have been $1,200 a month. So he became uninsured.
Then, last September, Mr. Huth had a stroke that landed him in the hospital. He is now trying to pay the $10,000 hospital bill. "I send them what I can," he said. "I have a choice. Do I want to eat and have a place to sleep, or pay for health insurance and sleep on the street?"

GRAPHIC: Photo: Ruth Kain, 64, has not been able to get insurance because of a heart condition. (Christina Dicken for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: The Dollar Standard;
Spinning Gold Into Dross

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
EVER since early civilizations began shaping it into tools and ornaments, gold has influenced human behavior like few other substances.
It has been at the root of wars, and of appalling acts of greed, and a symbol of kings and gods. 
 Gold long ago became the one material the world could agree upon as a measure and store of value. For much of the last two centuries it stood as a physical proxy for the financial strength of economies.
Gold can still make hearts flutter, and the recent revelations of how the Swiss handled the gold stolen by the Nazis from Holocaust victims are a dark reminder of the metal's powerful allure. But whatever its other enduring qualities, its economic role is in decline.
The gold standard, under which currency values were assigned in terms of gold, has long since fallen victim to an increasingly complex global economy. And now gold's role as a reserve -- the store of wealth that nominally backs an economy's soundness -- is slowly giving way to a belief among the central banks that are its biggest owners that holding vaults full of gold bars may be an anachronism.
In recent years, central banks in the Netherlands, Belgium, Canada and Australia have sold sizable portions of their reserves. Switzerland is considering selling some of its holdings. Eddie George, the governor of the Bank of England, predicted a few weeks ago that the new European Central Bank would choose not to hold substantial reserves at all when it begins operation in a few years. And although the United States does not have any plans to reduce its gold stores, the world's largest, economists within the Federal Reserve are tentatively exploring the case for selling.
"The question is what central banks and governments are going to do," said Ralph C. Bryant, a former Federal Reserve official who is now a senior fellow at the Brookings Institution. "Will the world eventually come back to gold as a reserve asset? Right now the probability looks fairly low."
The sales by central banks -- amounting to less than 6 percent of total government gold holdings from 1989 through 1996 -- have been a contributor to a sharp slide in the price of gold. From an all-time high of $850 an ounce in 1980, when inflation was raging and paper currency was losing its value, gold slid quickly to below $400 an ounce by 1982 and has more or less remained there. Last month it dipped below $300 for the first time in 12 years, and analysts see no compelling case for a substantial rebound anytime soon.
There are still fans of gold who believe it is the best hedge against inflation or some catastrophic upheaval that would leave paper money diminished or valueless. Indeed, supply-side economists say the decline of gold's role has been vastly exaggerated and that the world still operates in some ways on a gold standard, with gold serving an informal but critical role as a gauge with which to measure the underlying soundness of currencies.
"People still use gold as a way of assessing the promises of governments in dealing with their debt," said Jude Wanniski, a supply-side economist who is one of the most outspoken defenders of gold.
 
Alternatives
 But most analysts and economists believe that gold's importance has been permanently diminished. Investors looking for a hedge against inflation, they say, can find all kinds of alternatives.
The owners of capital now judge the strength of currencies based on the soundness of the monetary and fiscal policies in the countries that issue them. Money, the experts say, flows readily into currencies that are judged sound by the market, and flows even more rapidly out of currencies seen to be undermined by weak policies.
In the United States, Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, has gone a long way toward eliminating the threat of inflation. And since inflation was what drove many investors to hold gold rather than risk seeing the value of their paper currency deteriorate, gold has become correspondingly less attractive.
At the same time, the relatively strong growth rates enjoyed by the American economy as well as improvements on other fronts like the Federal budget deficit have made the United States the most economically sound of the world's rich nations. The result has been to solidify the dollar's worldwide appeal as a safe investment haven on a level quite unconnected to the nation's gold reserves. When turmoil hit the Asian financial markets, panicky investors funneled their money not into gold, once the investment of choice during times of crisis, but into American Treasury bonds.
"The ultimate value of a currency is not what is backing it in the vaults, but the management of the economy of that country, or of the world's perception of how well or badly managed it is," said Ted Arnold, who follows gold and other metals for Merrill Lynch in London.
 
Capital on the Move
"That is a very potent force in a world in which capital is free to move elsewhere," he said. "So long as Mr. Greenspan is seen to be doing a great job, it doesn't matter whether Fort Knox is bulging or doesn't have anything in it."
One effect of the dollar's stability and appeal is that it is gradually replacing gold as the reserve of choice for central banks. One reason is that holding dollars is more profitable than holding gold; United States Treasury bonds yield around 6 percent, while gold is expensive to guard and yields nothing (although central banks are increasingly trying to generate some profits by lending it out).
For private investors as well as governments, gold has been an abysmal investment. Total returns on the stocks that make up the Standard & Poor's 500 were 146 percent over the last five years and 266 percent over the last decade, according to Merrill Lynch, using figures through the end of August. High grade corporate bonds yielded 50 percent over five years and 175 percent over 10 years. Gold, by contrast, fell 4 percent over five years and 28 percent over 10 years.
Still, some influential people continue to see an important role for gold. Among them, paradoxically, may be Mr. Greenspan. A long-time advocate of the gold standard, he has spoken publicly in the last few years of the beneficial discipline that would come from linking the dollar's value to gold.
