HEADLINE: Slouching Toward 1998, Contentedly

BYLINE: By WILLIAM GRIMES 

 BODY:
AS Homo americanus approaches the millennium, he is beginning to adopt a recognizable posture. It's a low, simian slouch, the relaxed loping walk of a creature at ease with his loathsome self and content with the world, which, for all its ills, did manage to keep the stock market humming along in 1997.
The beast enjoyed a prosperous year. The stress level was manageable. Inflation remained quiescent, crime was on the decline, and the news was only averagely horrible. Well into its fin-de-siecle mode, the national culture generated myriad, irreconcilable signals, which, rather than creating chaos or alarm, simply canceled one another out and fused into a seamless post-modern nonstick coating. Everything was sort of O.K., except for "MMMBop." 
 With feminists looking the other way, men slipped back into their well-worn groove. They behaved like brutes.
Robert Downey Jr. earned jail time for feasting on alcohol and cocaine. Christian Slater earned jail time for, among other things, feasting on alcohol and cocaine, beating up his girlfriend and biting an acquaintance. Mike Tyson earned $27 million for biting an acquaintance. Marv Albert, the sportscaster, pleaded guilty but did not receive jail time for biting a very close acquaintance.
Men did seem to need a little fine-tuning on the sensitivity front. Earl Spencer looked like Galahad as he savaged the tabloid press for tormenting his sister, Princess Diana. The light of heroism burned for about as long as it takes a paparazzi's flashbulb to pop. It turned out that Earl Spencer was less than a model of gallantry when it came to his wife, but even he could not compete in the cad category with Tasos Michael, who not only left his fiancee at the altar, but also took off for Tahiti to enjoy the holiday intended for them both. Frank Gifford -- well, enough about Frank, and besides, he now wakes up to a sizzling-mad Kathie Lee for the rest of his natural life.
It makes perfect sense that one of the most highly regarded films of the year was "In the Company of Men," a charmer about two angry white-collar workers who express their hatred of women by seducing and then rejecting a deaf woman. The pop group Prodigy should have been hired for the soundtrack. Their contribution to the year in music was a gossamer meditation on the male experience called "Smack My Bitch Up." The song was No. 1 with a bullet for the director Quentin Tarantino, who got in touch with his feelings by, in his words, "bitch-slapping" Don Murphy, a film producer whose partner had made sniping remarks about Mr. Tarantino in a book.
For the record, Jack Nicholson's name was not connected to any of the above events.
The bad behavior seemed all the worse for being unaccompanied by shame. In America, 1997 was the year that the nonapology apology reached the status of an art form. Latrell Sprewell, a guard for the Golden State Warriors, choked his coach in a fit of anger, then discovered the kind of remorse that defendants usually do when sentencing looms. In a grudging performance, he expressed regret to basketball fans but pointedly avoided mentioning the name of P. J. Carlesimo, his coach. He implied that the attack was provoked and complained, "I've been looked on as a negative person."
Alex Kelly, convicted of raping a 16-year-old he had driven home from a party, reached deep into his heart and pulled out the following choice specimen for the judge: "I'm sorry that her perception differed from my perception." Top honors for in the non apology category go to Pol Pot, who, reflecting on the carnage he brought to Cambodia, could not quite manage to say, "Gee, I'm really sorry." "Am I a savage person?" he mused. The answer, clearly, was no. "My conscience is clear," Pol Pot said.
Whatever. A drowsy, self-absorbed apathy, interrupted by the occasional outburst of road rage, wrapped 1997 in a humid fog. In New York City, Ruth Messinger worked herself into a cappuccino-like froth of indignation as she tried to stir a somnolent electorate. Well into the mayoral campaign, nearly half of the eligible voters said that they had no particular opinion about her and many did not seem to have the faintest idea who she was. The post-ideological age found its natural home in New Jersey, where the gubernatorial election hinged on the issue of car-insurance rates.
On the national scene, the never-ending investigations, allegations and rumors swirling around the Democrats seemed like very bad daytime television. As political pundits tied themselves in knots over Whitewater, funny-money campaign financing, Paula Jones, this-Gate and that-Gate, the American people snoozed, sent to sleep by political static that has become, in a way, rather comforting, like the sound of traffic, or waves crashing on a beach.
It is symptomatic that the year was sandwiched by two civil trials, that both of them were re-runs and that neither stirred deep emotions. In Santa Monica, Calif., O. J. Simpson was found to have wrongfully caused the death of Ronald Goldman and Nicole Brown Simpson. The national reaction was a shrug.
In Poughkeepsie, N.Y., the once-inflammatory Tawana Brawley case resurfaced, as Stephen Pagones, a former assistant district attorney for Dutchess County, sued Ms. Brawley's advisers, the Rev. Al Sharpton, C. Vernon Mason and Alton Maddox, for accusing him of raping Ms. Brawley. A decade ago, the case brought racial tensions to the snapping point. This time, it was heard as a feeble echo.
Both the Simpson and Brawley cases seemed like those reunion specials in which the cast members of a once-popular television series gather, hoping to rekindle the old magic and goose the ratings for one night. They lost badly to the nanny trial, a weird exercise in interactive justice. A guilty verdict was delivered, the television audience didn't like it, and the judge changed it.
The economy also seemed like a rerun. It was 1987 without the hubris, a sequel to the original hit, and like a sequel, it seemed muffled and muted. Donald J. Trump was still there, but even he seemed quieter, as though aware that he is not, really, Donald Trump anymore, not in the 1987 sense. As James Poniewozik pointed out, reviewing Mr. Trump's book "The Art of the Comeback" in Salon: "The Donald Trump of 1987 was American power embodied. Today he's what? A rich landlord." Maybe, like the rest of America, Mr. Trump is tired. He wants a rest. He doesn't want to be bothered. Summing up his philosophy on women, he wrote: "There is high maintenance. There is low maintenance. I want no maintenance."
The year did have its moments of passion. The world witnessed mass outpourings of grief for Princess Diana, a distant figure, always seen in soft focus, whose trials and tribulations made her a kind of patron saint for the therapy age. Here, on unashamed display, were the emotions that current politics have not touched, unless you happen to live in New Jersey and just got your car-insurance bill. The Princess's death relegated the murder of Gianni Versace to a second-tier event and left the death of Mother Teresa a distant third in news coverage.
It took celebrity murder to pierce the national armor of indifference this year. The shocker memoir certainly didn't do the job. In "The Kiss," Kathryn Harrison recounted a long incestuous relationship with her father. The only reaction from the critics was annoyance. In the collection "Close to the Bone: Memoirs of Hurt, Rage and Desire," a writer called Terminator told of seducing his mother's boyfriend. The critics found the story nearly as irritating as the title of the book. There's only so much shock to go around, as Harmony Korine discovered with his film "Gummo," which could have been titled "Kids2 ." Snarling adolescents with funny clothes, funny faces and cryptic nicknames are so '96.
Clearly, artists are trying to work out a respectable strategy for the end of the millennium. Role models help, so it is hardly surprising that Oscar Wilde, Mr. Fin-de-Siecle himself, emerged as the man of the year. Wilde returned in triumph Off Broadway in "Gross Indecency: The Three Trials of Oscar Wilde," and in London in Tom Stoppard's "Invention of Love." Stephen Fry scored a bull's-eye in the title role in the film "Wilde," and Liam Neeson is preparing to take a crack at the man in David Hare's "Judas Kiss."
Wilde was the dream date for the millennial prom, a transgressive, slyly deconstructive, proto-Warholian master of media imagery, the first impresario of self-created celebrity, whose finest work, the fictional character known as Oscar Wilde, baffled the public by posing as a poseur. What could be more perfect for the late 90's?
The waning years of a century lend themselves to elegant doodling in the margins, as Wilde knew. In fashion, the stern rule of minimalism gave way last year to baroque detailing, ornamentation and florid romanticism in collections by the English designers Stella McCartney and John Galliano and the Americans Marc Jacobs and Calvin Klein, among others. Mr. Galliano predicted a new Belle Epoque. Liz Tilberis, the editor of Harper's Bazaar, decided that the world was tired of seeing scrawny models who looked like they had the flu. She uncorked a new motto for her photographers: S.E.S., or "Sex, Elegance and Smiling."
All in all, 1997 was a little lame, a tepid brew, a dull pudding, a year to be sorry for, if anyone knew how to apologize anymore. At the bar of history, 1997 can say only one thing: "I regret very much that this had to happen, and that certain people felt let down, but in a very real sense, I feel that I am being made the victim here."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Far left: Kathie Lee Gifford wasn't always smiling at her husband, Frank. Latrell Sprewell had no smiles for his coach. For Robert Downey Jr., it was a year to forget. For John Galliano, it was Belle Epoque II. Ruth Messinger had an identity crisis. Evander Holyfield had his ear chewed by Mike Tyson. Left: It was the year for the importance of being Oscar Wilde. The nanny verdict shocked onlookers, as well as the nanny; the verdict was reversed. Earl Spenser scolded the tabloids, then became fodder for them.

LENGTH: 1224 words

HEADLINE: EARNING IT;
Dear Mom and Dad: At Camp We Started Our Own Company

BYLINE: By VERNE G. KOPYTOFF 

 BODY:
MOST people think of summer camp as a place where children spend lazy days at the lake, roasting marshmallows and playing tug-of-war. Discussions of entrepreneurship, business and finance may seem out of place in such a carefree environment and, frankly, of little interest to most teen-agers.
But then there are young people like Heisi Figueroa, who started selling Avon cosmetics at age 16 -- when most children are starting to drive -- and now, a year later, oversees the work of two adults. For her, commerce is a hobby. And for Katy Meyer, 18, who devised a business plan to make hand-painted silk scarves at 14, and started selling them at a local store soon after, capitalism takes the place of high school sports. 
 To indulge their appetite for entrepreneurship, the two took part in Camp Start Up, where girls ages 13 to 18 ponder the meaning of words like "bootstrapping," create imaginary companies and learn to play golf because of the sport's importance in the business world. The camp, one of several youth summer programs in the country that focus on business, was founded in 1994 by Joline Godfrey, an author who runs An Income of Her Own, a nonprofit organization based in Santa Barbara, Calif., that promotes teaching girls about economics.
"Nearly 90 percent of all women will have to take care of themselves economically at some point in their lives," Ms. Godfrey said. "But girls tend to get less education about economics while they are growing up. The point is to show girls that owning their own company is accessible, that it is not just for extraordinary women whom they could never aspire to be."
Camp Start Up, which costs $1,400 for a two-week session, is held annually in late June at the Athenian School, a preparatory school in Danville, Calif., and a month later on the campus of Wellesley College, outside Boston. (Information is available by calling 805-646-1215.) While former students recalled traditional summer-camp pursuits like fawning over the swimming-pool lifeguard, they also said they worked until late at night on business presentations, talked with women entrepreneurs, and visited businesses owned by women, like some wineries in the Napa Valley of California.
A brochure says girls who graduate from Camp Start Up will leave with "a Rolodex full of names and addresses of new friends and new contacts." The curriculum for the roughly 30 campers each session includes topics like creating a business card, attending a "power lunch" and learning to dress for business.
To be sure, the camp's graduates still have much to learn about the more complicated aspects of commerce, including financing and business planning. Adolescents who are interested in business must generally wait until college to learn the details -- and those who become entrepreneurs, because of their age, must have their parents do the contractual paperwork.
Miss Figueroa, the high school junior from Chelsea, Mass., who sells cosmetics, said that attending camp last summer built her confidence and taught her about the arduous work it would take to realize her dream of opening a custom picture-framing store. She already makes picture frames in her spare time, selling them to relatives and friends.
"I like everything that has to do with money," she said. "At camp, our group of five girls had five different ideas for our business plan, so agreeing on something was difficult. I learned that if I want to make a business, I have to learn to communicate."
Boys are excluded from the program because they tend to monopolize discussions and make girls uncomfortable about offering opinions, Ms. Godfrey said. Or, in the words of Lelah Baker Rabe, 15, a former camper from Ojai, Calif., the absence of boys let girls "get beyond the flirty stuff and get to the heart of the business problems."
Of course, the benefits of a girls-only education are a subject of general debate. In addition, many champions of youth business programs that run year-round in many cities say their programs provide more opportunity for learning than a one- or two-week camp. The year-round programs, they say, allow students to build relationships with mentors and to obtain internships.
"It's fine to go to camp and have a brief exposure to business," said Susan Fisher, director of the Colorado Women's Business Office, which runs a yearlong after-school entrepreneurship program, Young Women of the West. "But that goes away. The question is: How do you have some ongoing exposure for these girls who six months later can hardly remember that they went to camp?"
Inevitably, some girls who attend Camp Start Up are pushed there by parents rather than by a personal desire to build a company from scratch. But Ms. Godfrey said most students left camp with a better understanding of business concepts.
Ms. Godfrey, who has written two books, including "No More Frogs to Kiss: 99 Ways to Give Economic Power to Girls" (Harper Business, 1995), began her career at Polaroid, where she worked in product development. She then founded Odysseum, a company that makes educational games for corporate employees, and later started An Income of Her Own. She also bought Independent Means, a company that sells games about economics, conducts seminars and sponsors Camp Start Up.
Miss Meyer, the former camper from Carlisle, Mass., now makes silk scarves at her home that sell in a local women's store for $100 to $180. She declined to be specific about her income, beyond saying that she makes more money from her business than is possible working as a sales clerk at the mall.
Miss Meyer is unsure about her career after college. Her only requirement is that she be in charge.
She says attending the camp was invaluable. "Programs like Camp Start Up really help young girls because they see women in business doing fabulously well and think 'Wow, I can be like that,' " she said.
 
Fun and Business
Camp Start Up is one of several business camps in the United States. Here is information on some others.

CAMP ENTREPRENEUR A weeklong, girls-only program organized by the National Education Center for Women in Business. Sites so far are Atlanta; Youngstown, Ohio; Jacksonville, Fla., and Locust Valley, N.Y.; more will be added. Dates to be determined. Ages: 12 to 19. Tuition: $225 to $495. Information: (800) 632-9248.
 
BIZ BUILDERS A coeducational camp sponsored by the National Foundation for Teaching Entrepreneurship, held in Wellesley, Mass. July 26 to Aug. 8. Tuition: $1,550. The foundation also offers Biz Camp, a free, coed camp for low-income students. Information for both camps: (800) 367-6383.
 
YOUTH ENTREPRENEURSHIP BUSINESS CAMP Educational Designs That Generate Excellence (E.D.G.E.), a company specializing in entrepreneurship education, conducts the one- and two-week coed camps, as well as weekend programs for students from elementary to high school. Locations include New York, Boston, Houston, San Diego and Atlanta. Tuition: $200 to $795. Some camps for low-income youths are free. Information: (800) 879-3343.
 
YOUNG AMERITOWNE CAMP A five-day camp in Denver sponsored by the Young Americans Education Foundation. Several programs will be offered June 16 to July 28, depending on the participant's age. No housing available. Tuition: $135. Information: (303) 321-2954.

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Caviar Dreams: The Robin Leach Portfolio

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
ROBIN LEACH, the British journalist turned television entertainer and entrepreneur, made his name as a chronicler of the rich and famous.
But Mr. Leach, who among other things is the spokesman for the Television Food Network and has a series, "Miracles and Wonders," on the USA Network, is himself an interesting case study of the pursuit of greater wealth. He invests widely in emerging markets and, following an interest born of his occupation, has big positions in the high-technology and telecommunications sectors. But he likes land, too, with property in Connecticut and a large stake in the Jumby Bay Club, a 300-acre resort off the coast of Antigua. 
 Mr. Leach, who is 56 and is married with three grown children and two grandchildren, recently explained his investment philosophy and described some of the successes and failures it has yielded.
Q. Have you always been interested in investing?
A. I had absolutely no interest in investing when I was growing up. It was only after I started to earn decent money in the United States -- I emigrated at age 21 -- that I learned there are vehicles other than certificates of deposit. Then, as you get the foot in the water you decide to put in your knee, and there is an immersion process.
Q. Just how immersed are you?
A. Well, my lawyer does my pension fund, I have two accounts at Bear, Stearns and five accounts at Bernard Harold & Company, a New York brokerage. But I don't have time to look after more than two of them.
One of those two is full of communications stocks, telephone and networking companies. More than half of that account is in telecommunications and technology. That account, which is more than seven figures, is where I do my really aggressive stuff, including investing in emerging markets.
The other portfolio, which is a good bit smaller, is a conservative one that includes stocks like Bethlehem Steel and Chrysler, as well as other telecommunications stocks.
Q. What would a pie chart of your assets look like?
A. About 59 percent of my money is in stocks, bonds and money market funds, with the bulk of that in stocks. Another 40 percent is in real estate -- the properties I own in Jumby Bay and in Connecticut. And the remaining 1 percent is in collectibles.
Q. How did you get interested in emerging markets?
A. Traveling overseas. You see how hard these countries are trying to put themselves on the same footing with the United States, and how little they spend on consumer pleasures. There are many things we can learn from people overseas about saving for the future, and about keeping the trilevel family intact.
Q. How long ago did you start investing in emerging markets?
A. About eight years ago, in a meaningful allocation.
Q. Has the meltdown in Asia hit you particularly hard?
A. I have lost money in Malaysia, but eventually I'll come out ahead. And I pulled out of Thailand, where I had done very well, some time ago.
Q. Which Malaysian stocks do you still own?
A. I still own shares in the YTL Corporation, which is a conglomerate in the hotel, power and construction businesses. I invested in it three years ago. It has slumped, but the company is large and still growing.
Q. Why did you get out of Thailand?
A. When I traveled there I noticed they had built all these amazing hotels and no one was in them, and prices were coming down. That was two years ago. I had made a reasonable profit there, and at that point I thought there were better opportunities in the United States.
Q. The high prices in the American market don't concern you?
A. Not really. I think this market has a long way to go. I think America has the world's leading economy. Just compare the way it dealt with the savings and loan crisis and how Asia is dealing with its problems. And there was much more money at stake in the American crisis.
But there are some problems with the market, which is becoming a division of show business. Stars are being made at CNBC and CNNfn by announcing financial results. I mean, the stock market is not Atlantic City and Las Vegas. You have to invest for the long term.
Q. How do you pick stocks?
A. I don't visit companies. It's mostly a gut feeling, plus a little help from my brokers. You mustn't be sold a stock; you must understand a stock. Pay attention to what is going on around you. You are your own best analyst.
I made a lot of money out of the mobile telephone business in Asia, for example, and the only reason I did was because I saw firsthand that many of their telephone systems are useless. That means buying cellular.
Q. How did you get so interested in high-tech and telecommunications stocks?
A. I have always been involved in the communications business, and I have always said you should stick to investing in what you know best. I don't think you have to be too knowledgeable, but you do have to follow your business and try to put yourself in management's shoes.
And right now, everybody even remotely connected with cable television, which is my business, is working on developing the best delivery system to get it into the home. In the end it will be the telephone companies buying the cable companies, or the cable companies buying the telephone companies. To me, all of these businesses, plus software companies like Microsoft, are so allied that they seem to be an expansion of the cable television business I am in.
Q. Which high-tech and telecommunications stocks do you own?
A. I have issues like Cox Cable, Intercable, CVC, Intel, Microsoft, Nokia and Sun Microsystems in my aggressive account. In my conservative account I own AT&T, Sprint, Lucent, Compaq Computer, Cisco Systems, Nextel, CBS, Bell Atlantic, GTE and Worldcom.
Q. Have you had any big winners recently?
A. It's funny, but the two stocks I have made the most money on in the last 12 months have nothing to do with telecommunications.
I own a stock called World Fuel Services, which is the only company that recycles aviation oil. It also provides fuel to non-major airlines. I bought the stock at about $11 and I own more than 11,000 shares. It is now trading at about $18.
The other one is AgribioTech, which I bought at about $1. I now own about 10,000 shares, and it is now trading at more than $16. That company will end up as the largest seed conglomerate in the world.
My broker at Bear, Stearns said they were both good companies with good growth prospects.
Q. Have you made any bad investments?
A. I have had minor losses in certain stocks, and in a few restaurants.
Q. Would you characterize yourself as an active investor?
A. Not really. I'm not all that serious about it. When I am at home I spend about 20 minutes a day on this. But I can go 10 days without looking at what I have. I am not what you would call an in-and-out trader.
Q. Many entertainers seem to be risk-averse investors. Is that true?
A. The rule of thumb among show-business people is that a certain amount of capital is invested in the most conservative of investments. That is because most of them are like freelance journalists: they are always worrying about where their next paycheck is coming from.
Q. You have met many rich and famous people. Did you ever get tips or hints from them about investing?
A. I have never asked for a stock tip in my life. I don't believe in them. I may have learned some lessons from the titans of industry we had on the show, but I have not taken any tips.
Q. From whom do you take your investing lessons?
A. I have tried to take the patient, value-oriented investment philosophy of a Warren Buffett or a Mark Mobius. Adnan Khashoggi, by contrast, would tend to be rather flippant with what he bought. You can learn both ways.

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
Panic Fighters Need a New J. P. Morgan

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS 

 BODY:
Just over 90 years ago, the world's financial system seemed to be teetering. The Alexandria Stock Exchange had collapsed, and when the Bank of England sent gold to Egypt, a run on the pound followed. A wave of bank failures swept Japan.
In New York, trust companies -- a sort of bank -- were overleveraged and vulnerable. When one failed, panic followed. 
 In those days, there was no Federal Reserve System, and the United States Treasury did not know what to do. But the bankers of the era did. They summoned J. P. Morgan from an Episcopal church conference in Virginia. And he solved the problem.
Under Morgan's leadership, and with the help of some cash from the Government, decisions were made about which trust companies were hopelessly in debt, and which could be saved. "This is the place to stop the trouble," Morgan remarked as money was committed to stop a run on one institution that was believed to be basically sound.
Thus did the Panic of 1907 come to an end.
Last week, the world dealt very differently with the Panic of 1997. Extra billions were shipped quickly to South Korea to buy time. But the approach this time may be wrong. What is needed is not more government-to-government aid. What is needed is a plan to determine what parts of the Korean private sector -- particularly the banks -- are fundamentally sound, and which are not. Those that are hopeless should be left to fail, with the international banks that lent to them suffering losses. The others should get the cash needed to keep operating.
Ideally, the separation of the solvent from the insolvent would be made by Koreans. But there are no Koreans with the international stature needed. The old Korean Government failed to make the tough choices, and bears some of the blame because it encouraged banks to lend to favored companies. The new Government of Kim Dae Jung won't take office until February.
What we need is a new Morgan. Names like Robert E. Rubin, the Treasury Secretary; Paul A. Volcker, the former Federal Reserve chairman, and Karl Otto Pohl, the longtime boss of the German Bundesbank, come to mind. The latter two are investment bankers now, with conflicts of interest, but those could be dealt with.
Two things need to be avoided here. A collapse of the Korean economy is one, and clearly the World Bank and International Monetary Fund are attuned to that. But the other, while more subtle, is no less real: Koreans will suffer no matter what happens, but they must not be given reason to believe that their sacrifices are for the sake of foreign banks that foolishly financed excesses by Korean banks.
There is talk of somehow having the Korean Government take over the international debts of its sick banks, and then negotiating stretched-out payments. That would be unfortunate. It would lump into one basket the loans that never should have been made with those that would have been fine but for the panic. And it would leave the new Korean Government saddled with huge debts.
The I.M.F.'s prescription for ailing economies makes sense for countries where the sins are those of governments that spent too much and taxed too little. But in the new world financial order, the sins are more likely to be in the private sector, albeit with Government connivance or, at best, inadequate regulation. The world must find a way to deal with such crises. Evaluating the credit of each big Korean bank, and acting on that basis, is a good way to start.

HEADLINE: NEW YORKERS & CO.;
The Year of the Bodega Bust

BYLINE: By JESSE McKINLEY 

 BODY:
IT's Christmas Day just after dusk, and at a corner delicatessen on Avenue D things seem pretty bleak.
Crudely taped-on posters and boxes of laundry detergent block out most of the windows, and paint peels in strips from the tin awning. The lights in the glassed refrigerators are off, and dusty cans of dog food and soup sit untouched on the shelves. A few customers breeze in and out, but most of the business seems to consist of 10-cent candies and the occasional cigar. 
 Despite appearances, the Police Department suspects that the owners of the deli are actually doing quite well for themselves.
The location is one of a dozen or so establishments -- delis, candy stores, beauty salons, ice cream parlors and other small businesses -- on the Lower East Side that are under surveillance by police officers who believe that heroin, cocaine, marijuana or other drugs are being sold covertly over (or under) the counter.
The investigation of the deli on Avenue D, which began last month, is part of an continuous crackdown on what law-enforcement officials say is one of more persistent presences in the city's drug trade: the storefront retail operation.
The police operation is having a profound effect on the sidewalk landscape of this neighborhood and several others, shuttering seedy drug fronts, scattering dealers and paving the way for grass-roots entrepreneurship in the tiny commercial spaces that illicit proprietors leave behind.
Indeed, while civic boosters may point to the new Times Square and bull-market prosperity as evidence of the city's renewal, the decline of the drug bodega on the Lower East Side offers a more immediate street-level picture of the boom.
The police have raided many of the storefronts before. What's new is the confidence with which new businesses -- coffee shops, bars, boutiques -- have gobbled up places that once repeatedly reopened under owners whose motives were suspect.
In this scenario, the storefront bust opens the way for commercial pioneers: the real estate risk takers and night-life entrepreneurs.
The crackdown began as a pilot program on the Lower East Side in 1995, and later expanded to northern Brooklyn, the central Bronx, upper Manhattan and central and eastern Queens, police officials said.
This year, police in the Seventh and Ninth Precincts on the east side of lower Manhattan have closed 65 locations suspected of being drug fronts, according to Police Department statistics.
By using a wide variety of nuisance-abatement laws, as well fire and health code inspections and undercover work, the police have closed 443 businesses citywide for criminal violations in the last nine months alone, Police Commissioner Howard Safir said. In addition to drug offenses, some businesses have been closed for permitting gambling, trafficking in stolen goods and prostitution.
The prospective new business owners who take over undergo periodic background checks. Mr. Safir called this a "holistic approach" that is intended to stop the cycle of raid, closing, opening, raid.
The Police Department's legal department is also working closely with landlords to evict tenants; in addition, some landlords who have allowed criminal activity to flourish are being prosecuted.
The community welcomes the busts. Armando Perez, Democratic district leader for the Lower East Side, said he had heard no complaints about police harassment from legitimate bodega owners.
"Anyone could tell what's a real bodega and what's a front," he said. "You walk in and there are only two types of cigarettes -- it's not a real good sign."
The signs of change are visible everywhere. Along a stretch of upper Avenue B between 10th and 14th Streets, the police have raided four drug fronts this year. In their place are a pair of restaurants, two refurbished retail spaces and Musical Box, a trendy bar.
In many cases, landlords are big winners. Tenants say they are commanding upward of $3 a square foot for spaces that previously rented for less than half of that.
At 133 Avenue C, between East Eighth and Ninth Streets, Dimitri Vlahakis and Stephan Gerville-Reache are hard at work preparing their new lounge, Baraza, for a late January opening. It sits across the street from an abandoned lot where wild dogs bark and squeal at every passer-by.
Still, Mr. Vlahakis says he feels lucky to have the space, which was a deli until March when it was closed on suspicion of selling cocaine. The orange Police Department sticker announcing the bust still clings to the front window.
"A lot of people prefer to be a little more out of the way, on the edge," Mr. Vlahakis said. "This area, this whole city, is changing and we're a good part of the change."

HEADLINE: The World;
Turks Reinvent Their World

BYLINE: By STEPHEN KINZER 

DATELINE: ISTANBUL

 BODY:
JUST a year ago, Turkey seemed to be setting out on a profoundly important geopolitical journey. Guided by an Islamic-oriented Prime Minister, Necmettin Erbakan, it was turning away from the West and toward the Muslim world. Mr. Erbakan sent shivers down spines in Washington as he turned his back on Turkey's traditional allies and embraced the leaders of Iran and Libya.
Today the circumstances are radically different. Mr. Erbakan has been forced from power, and his party may soon be banned on the ground that it seeks to undermine the secular and Western-oriented basis of Turkish society. But his successor, Mesut Yilmaz, evidently shares his belief that Turkey needs a new set of friends. 
 Mr. Yilmaz, with the backing of the military that helped oust Mr. Erbakan, disagrees deeply with his predecessor's belief that Turkey should embrace the Islamic world. But he agrees that Turkey's future no longer lies in embracing the exclusively European identity that many Turks have sought for generations.
 
New Partners
Under Mr. Yilmaz, Turkey is striving to redefine its place in the world, by tying its future to America, Russia, Israel and the new nations of the Caucasus, Central Asia and the Balkans.
Turkey first applied for membership in the European Union in 1963, and for much of the time since then it has sat patiently at Europe's closed gate. Two weeks ago, at a summit in Luxembourg, European leaders again rejected Turkey's plea that it should at least be given some encouragement, if it could not be admitted quickly. Mr. Yilmaz reacted angrily, freezing ties with the European Union and setting out to reshape his country's foreign policy.
In the world Mr. Yilmaz sees, Turkey will no longer base its policy on the suggestions, criticisms and occasional insults that issue forth from Brussels. Like a suitor too often spurned, it will stop banging at Europe's door. It may still seek to improve its human rights record, resolve the Kurdish conflict and improve relations with Greece, but it will do so without guidance from the European Union.
"They have used our application for full membership in the Union as a means of leverage," Mr. Yilmaz said in an interview. "Nobody should think that Turkey will be forced to accept some unacceptable formulas just because we don't want to be left out of the E.U."
Immediately after the disappointment in Luxembourg, Mr. Yilmaz met with Russian and American leaders. He was warmly received in Washington by President Clinton, who emphasized how important it is for the United States to have a dependable ally wedged between the turbulent Balkans, Caucasus and Middle East. Soon after his return to Ankara he received Prime Minister Viktor Chernomyrdin of Russia, and after signing a $13.5 billion gas deal, both men pledged to begin clearing the debris left by centuries of rivalry. Mr. Chernomyrdin said he was working to arrange a visit to Turkey by President Boris N. Yeltsin, and there were hints in Washington that President Clinton might also come calling.
Last week Mr. Yilmaz visited Turkmenistan, which controls part of the vast oil and gas reserve in and around the Caspian Sea. Turkey's booming economy is starved for energy, and able to offer much trade and expertise to neighbors in exchange for fuel.
The final major piece in Turkey's new strategic positioning is Israel. The two countries' armies were working together even under the Islamic-led Government, and now their Governments have become astonishingly close. Generals, cabinet ministers and legislators are working on scores of development projects and designing what they hope will be a long-term military alliance. Next month Turkish, Israeli and American warships will join in a military exercise in the Mediterranean code-named "Reliant Mermaid."
These steps add up to a very new message from Ankara, one that may reshape the political map of the region. Turkey is saying that it no longer needs to count on either the European Union or the Islamic world. Instead it will forge what Mr. Yilmaz calls "strategic partnerships" with the United States and Israel, try to strengthen its ties with Russia and renew its effort to make friends in the Caucasus, Central Asia and the Balkans.
 
The Risks
It is a high-stakes gamble. The Israeli piece of the new puzzle seems firm, based on each country's desperate need for a friend in a very dangerous neighborhood. Yet although many Turks are sympathetic to Israel, they are deeply upset with the current Israeli Government, which they view as oppressive and needlessly brutal to their Palestinian cousins. A close alliance between the two countries makes strategic sense to Turkish military planners, but it could stir popular resentment from which Islamic political leaders might profit.
The United States recognizes the value of Turkey's friendship, although powerful Greek, Armenian and pro-Kurdish lobbies in Washington will make it difficult for President Clinton to draw the two countries as close together as he would like. Russia, consumed with problems at home and tugged toward Greece by history and religion, cannot be counted upon. And while most of the Caucasian, Central Asian and Balkan countries are open to Turkey, Turkey must compete for influence there with other powerful players.
"It is a policy no longer fixated on Europe," said Foreign Minister Ismail Cem. "Turkey has a geo-strategic dimension that it previously was not very conscious of. With the downfall of the old Soviet order, a lot of new problems but also a lot of new chances surfaced. Something like 20 countries with whom we have historical and cultural affinities became independent states.
"These changes, and a growing consciousness of our European and Asian identity, have provided us with a new approach to this vast area instead of just being concentrated on one thing," Mr. Cem said.
"That fixation was the result of a limited outlook, of feeling that we had to resolve a conflict over whether we are European or Asian," he said. "Culturally, historically and geographically, we are a global state, and now we are becoming more aware of that."

HEADLINE: The World;
Hanoi Now, Meet Saigon Then

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: HO CHI MINH CITY, Vietnam

 BODY:
SAIGON, as most of the 5 million people who live here still call this place, was supposed to become Hanoi after the Vietnamese Communists won the war in 1975. Instead, Hanoi has become Saigon.
Up in the northern capital, loudly buzzing swarms of Honda motor scooters clog the narrow streets, which only 15 years ago were filled mostly with bicycles. Stalls run by small entrepreneurs selling or recycling everything conceivable pack the streets and market halls there just as they do here in Saigon. There is nothing that is not for sale someplace in either city, including sex. 
 Four decades after the French pulled out in 1954, the Communists have preserved Hanoi's spacious French villas and ochre-colored administrative buildings, not allowing them to be subjected to the ravages inflicted on similar tree-shaded neighborhoods in Saigon by the American influx of the 1960's. But except for the absence of G.I.'s, Hanoi now feels and sounds for all the world like Saigon did back then -- a wild, on-the-make city driven by powerful forces beyond its control.
Thirty years ago, those uncontrollable forces were the destructive winds of war. The Communists won the war, of course, but at first they didn't seem to know what to do with the victory and watched millions of their countrymen flee misery and oppression.
Then, a decade ago, Ho Chi Minh's successors changed economic course and opened Vietnam to the global economy, and recently as many as 200,000 overseas Vietnamese have been coming back each year to visit, and even to invest.
 
The B-52 Becomes a Drink
Yet it would be premature to conclude, as some have done after downing a B-52 cocktail in a trendy Hanoi eatery like the Indochine, that America or the values of democracy and free enterprise ultimately won the war.
The Vietnamese Communists -- like the Chinese authorities or the Government in Saigon during the war -- want free enterprise without undisciplined democracy. There is still only one party here, Ho Chi Minh's, and the authorities muzzle the Vietnamese press and cinema.
Foreign investors, on the other hand, are welcome to bring their money, and companies from Southeast Asian countries have poured it in in recent years, with more than $5 billion from Singapore alone since 1988, $4 billion from Taiwan, $3.7 billion from Hong Kong and $3.3 billion from Japan. One result is the emergence of skyscraper skylines both here and in Hanoi in just the past few years.
But the entrepreneurs are not welcome to bring in ideas the Government here regards as subversive. Any province or district chief can impose administrative restraints on the freedom of any citizen considered dangerous to the regime, and any overseas Vietnamese fitting that description soon find themselves on a plane back to wherever they came from.
Government censors watch vigilantly over every medium of expression, and their threats can be Draconian. They told one Vietnamese artist who returned to produce a commercial film in Vietnam that if he failed to abide by their strictures, his relatives living here would be held responsible.
"There are dissidents in this country who have tried to work with overseas Vietnamese against the Government, but military and police measures have kept them under control," said Dr. Nguyen Ngoc Ha, the senior official in Saigon dealing with Vietnamese from abroad.
Still, the same kind of heady excitement that was in the air in Moscow in the late 1980's, when Mikhail S. Gorbachev was trying to reform Communism there, is blowing in the wind here.
Fifteen years ago, when the Marxist-Leninist vise had Saigon tightly by the throat, few Vietnamese dared disobey an official ban on talking with foreign visitors. Today, with nearly 2 million tourists a year in the country, the injunction is on the books but rarely enforced.
 
Freedom to Profit
In addition, economic freedom has vastly expanded the sphere in which ordinary Vietnamese are able to make decisions for themselves, and they are using it to the full. "The police are still there, but they put a lot less pressure on us," said one small entrepreneur who, because he had worked for Americans during the war, found himself frozen out of the Socialist economy for years afterward.
Even at that, he was one of the comparatively lucky ones; officers who had served in the South Vietnamese Army were put through "re-education" in prison camps for as long as 10 years. Others tainted by association with the Saigon regime or with the Americans were cast aside to scrape by as best they could, while committed Communists and people from North Vietnam got preference for jobs in state-run companies and in admission to universities that are the training ground for the governing and commercial elite.
Nowadays, some people here in Saigon say that northerners continue to benefit from reverse discrimination in the state sector, but because the private sector is growing so fast, it doesn't matter as much as it used to. "Private companies don't care which side your father was on during the war," one 25-year-old office worker here said. Conversations with a number of Vietnamese her age leave the impression that the office worker's generation is not interested in settling historical scores -- just in catching up with history.
"As they grow more prosperous, there'll be more respect for human rights," said Douglas B. (Pete) Peterson, who returned to Hanoi as the American Ambassador last spring, having spent six and a half years there as a prisoner of war after his Air Force plane was shot down in 1966.
Dr. Ha, who spent 28 years in France before coming back in 1976, agreed. "We will be more tolerant as we get stronger," he said.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Expensive lingerie is visible now in the shop windows of Vietnam's capital and other major cities, where some women can afford it. In Hanoi, Japanese electronic goods are flooding the market, one more sign that average incomes are continuing to rise. (Photographs by Agence France-Presse)

HEADLINE: Easy Money;
Borrowing Asia's Troubles

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE 

 BODY:
WANT to make some easy money? No problem. Drop in at Chase Manhattan Bank and ask to borrow some yen. Chase and other big banks will happily lend yen at an interest rate that can't be beat: 2.75 percent, all charges included. Even a desperate used car salesman won't lend money that cheap.
Now take those borrowed yen and convert them to dollars. With the dollars, buy a Government-guaranteed Treasury bond, yielding nearly 6 percent a year. Sit back and enjoy the spread of three percentage points. If you borrow $1 million, that's the easiest $30,000 a year you'll ever see. Or better yet, put $1 million in stock. The Dow Jones industrial average is up nearly 20 percent this year, and if 1998 is only half as good, that is still a $72,000 return on $1 million in borrowed yen. 
 The Asian tigers (remember them?) borrowed lots of yen to help finance their booms. Japan has kept interest rates absurdly low in hopes that Japanese would borrow and spend and lift their own economy out of its severe doldrums. The Japanese haven't borrowed, despite the lure. But everyone else has. Chase pays less than 1 percent for yen, which it re-lends at interest rates two or three times higher. Other banks and brokerage firms do the same.
 
Loans to Debts
Naturally, there's a catch. Asia's boom has famously collapsed, and those yen loans that looked like can't-miss free money are now huge unpaid debts. And it's worth noting that the borrowing of yen, which helped Asia boom and then collapse, is going on unabated in the United States, too. Bankers call it the "carry trade."
"It is a very dangerous game, this carry trade, a house of cards," said Stephen S. Roach, chief economist at Morgan Stanley, Dean Witter, Discover & Company, a big Wall Street house that engages in the carry trade itself. "With Japan weak and Asia getting weaker by the minute, the players in this game find themselves turning to American Treasuries and stocks."
The risks should give pause. The biggest is that the Japanese yen will rise in value. A 4 percent rise in the $1 million worth of yen borrowed by Chase's customer suddenly takes $1,040,000 to repay, turning what seemed like an easy profit into a loss. That very fear, that the yen was about to rise in value, hit the securities market earlier this month for about two hours, and there was a rush to sell Treasury notes to cover outstanding loans. But the marketplace soon decided the yen would not appreciate, and trading subsided. The gamble sustaining the carry trade is that the yen will continue to lose value.
Chase, of course, protects itself. Its borrower has to be a preferred customer, someone worth millions, whose savings can be taken if the speculation goes wrong. But the big players in this game are not individuals. They are investment houses like Morgan Stanley and Merrill Lynch and hedge funds that specialize in risky investments, the carry trade being one of them.
"A whole panoply of financial institutions are engaged in this business, trading for themselves," said Henry Kaufman, an economist and money manager. "The level is unprecedented."
No one really knows how much of this risky money is coming into the American stock and bond markets through the carry trade, from foreigners or Americans. The amounts invested from overseas are clearly way up in recent quarters, the Commerce Department's Bureau of Economic Analysis reports. But neither the bureau nor the Bank for International Settlement, which tracks this sort of data, has breakdowns by currency or by type of transaction.
"What you have to do is infer," said Russell Scholl, a bureau economist. "There is not going to be hard evidence."
What he and others infer is that the carry trade is alive and thriving on Wall Street, but the amounts involved, $100 billion to $200 billion, are relatively small in the multitrillion-dollar American markets. If the gamble backfires, stock and bond prices might gyrate for a while. The Federal Reserve might be alarmed. And some big financial institutions will report eye-catching losses for the quarter in question.
Asia is another matter. Lending yen in the American market has far less impact on the national economy than lending foreign currencies in much smaller Thailand or South Korea. The huge bailouts by the International Monetary Fund are in large part to reimburse foreign lenders whose gambles went wrong. Japanese banks and European banks with operations in Japan were the biggest players, but American banks lent plenty, too.
The borrower in this case may have been a Korean industrialist seeking to build a factory. He went to a Korean bank and learned that the cheapest loan originated in yen. The industrialist borrowed in the Korean currency, the won, and agreed to make repayment in won. Say he borrowed $10 million worth of won. His bank then financed that loan by borrowing an equal amount in yen from a Japanese or European or American bank.
These foreign bankers borrowed the yen at less than 1 percent, re-lent it at 2.5 to 3 percent to the Korean banker, who charged the industrialist a higher rate, say 8 or 9 percent -- all nice markups for the banks. Sometimes the industrialist went directly to the foreign lender, bypassing his local banker and borrowing for less than 8 or 9 percent. And sometimes the loans were not in yen, but in dollars. After all, dollar loans are available for as little as 5 percent and, re-lent in Korea, can still produce a nice markup.
There was, of course, a gamble. This lending worked only if the won kept its value against the yen and the dollar. The industrialist made his monthly loan payment of, say, $150,000 in won and his Korean banker converted these won into an equal sum in yen or dollars to repay the overseas lender. That worked for a long time, and then two things happened. The won fell in value so that the industrialist's monthly payment no longer was enough to purchase $150,000 in yen or dollars. And many industrialists found that their new factories failed to make money, so they defaulted on their loans.
In various forms in different countries, that is the essence of the Asian crisis. By the best and most recent estimate available, South Korea owed $100 billion as of last December to foreign banks, mainly in Japan, Germany, the United States and France. The International Monetary Fund's $57 billion bailout is intended in large part to help Korea repay these creditors. So is the Clinton Administration's decision, announced last week, to join other industrial countries in supplying an additional $10 billion in emergency aid to Korea. The Administration has also prodded American banks to postpone their deadlines for loan repayments. Unless they do, the won is likely to keep falling in value.
 
Fault All Over
So who's to blame for all this mess, and what can be done? The Koreans are lambasted for reckless borrowing. And the foreign banks are increasingly singled out for encouraging the reckless borrowing, which for the banks was so profitable. But the system is also at fault. It periodically creates situations in which money can be borrowed at a low rate in one currency and re-lent in another at a much higher rate. The loans are invariably short-term, for a year or less, but always with the promise that they will be rolled over indefinitely as long as one currency or another does not lose value.
That is like betting on a lame horse to win. Devaluation always seems to catch lenders and borrowers off guard, although they bounce back for more, the lure of easy money being rather powerful. The solution, some experts say, is new global regulations or capital controls that impose restrictions on such lending across borders or make it less profitable. As Mr. Kaufman, the economist and money manager, said, "Markets don't always work the way they should."

HEADLINE: Trouble in Zambia

 BODY:
Zambia, one of the first African countries to hold multiparty elections in the early 1990's, now seems to be sliding back toward authoritarianism. A particularly worrisome development was the Christmas Day arrest of former President Kenneth Kaunda, the man who led Zambia to independence. The Government hopes to link Mr. Kaunda to a failed coup attempt last October, when he was out of the country. Under an emergency law that permits detention without charges or trial, Mr. Kaunda can be held for four weeks, during which his supporters fear he could be tortured or killed.
Mr. Kaunda was no liberal democrat during the 27 years he ruled Zambia. He stifled the press, jailed critics and permitted only one party. Protests forced him into free elections in 1991, which he lost to Frederick Chiluba, a trade union leader he had earlier jailed. Mr. Chiluba's promises of democracy and free markets excited broad hopes. But six years later, his rule has turned increasingly arbitrary. 
 Before last year's election, he rewrote the Constitution to bar Mr. Kaunda and another opposition leader from running. The United States and other countries rightly responded by reducing their aid programs. Earlier this year, donor nations agreed to restore the cuts provided Zambia improved its performance on privatizing industry and combating corruption.
Then in October, a group of military officers staged a bumbling coup attempt, which was easily suppressed. Since then, Mr. Chiluba has sharply stepped up his repression, jailing scores of political opponents. Mr. Kaunda may indeed have had some link to the coup. But that can only be determined by a fair trial. To fulfill its promises of economic freedom and governmental accountability, the Chiluba Government must return to the rule of law. Foreign donors should hold up their aid until it does.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Essay;
Office Pool, 1998

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
Within each question, you're allowed the choice of one, none or all.
1. Most startling intelligence coup: (a) neo-Angletonian Waldemar Skotzko finds Primakov's mole; (b) U.N. inspectors learn that Saddam's cache of anthrax spores has been moved outside Iraq; (c) Castro's Cuban intelligence, voted "best network in the U.S." by world spookery, switches allegiance from Russia to China. 
 2. Busiest new-book buzz among the cognoscenti will be about (a) Syracuse U. Prof. Mary Karr's "Viper Rum" poems; (b) Peggy Noonan's golden-voiced oratorical advice to a tongue-tangled generation, "Simply Speaking"; (c) Restaurateur George Lang's "Nobody Knows the Truffles I've Seen"; (d) Anna Quindlen's best novel, "Black and Blue."

4. In the 1998 Senate races, (a) Illinois Republicans choose moderate woman who then trounces Carol Moseley-Braun to attain filibuster-breaking majority of 60; (b) Democrats overcome retirements of Glenn, Ford and Bumpers to hold their 55-45 minority.

6. 7. SECTION: Section 3; Page 2; Column 3; Money and Business/Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1277 words

HEADLINE: DIARY

BYLINE: By DAVID RAMPE

 BODY:

RETAILING
Barely Merry
A sparkling economy failed to deliver the snappy holiday season that retailers were counting on. Analysts and merchants estimated that sales were up a scant 2 to 3 percent from December of last year. This was the third lackluster Christmas season in a row, despite the sterling economy and the wealth-generating gains of the stock market. Analysts said the disappointment underscored the harsh necessity for retailers to come to grips with the season's diminishing value to them. "It took stores years to learn that Easter had disappeared as a selling holiday," said Peter Schaeffer, a retail analyst on Wall Street. "They have to realize that you can't count on Christmas anymore either." And even where sales were strong, profit margins were often weak because of discounting. Economists cited high consumer debt, the diminishing tendency of extended families to gather around the Christmas tree and the simple exhaustion of working couples trying to muster the energy to go to the mall. 

SOUTH KOREA
More Aid, and Faster
South Korea got another $10 billion in emergency aid, as Washington and its allies decided that they could not wait for Seoul to get through its economic chaos without further help. The $57 billion bailout announced on Dec. 3 was led by the International Monetary Fund, and further aid was mentioned then only as a "second line of defense." Now Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin has reversed himself and agreed to lend $1.7 billion in early January to stabilize the Korean currency, which has sunk far below its summer levels. He also agreed to press big commercial lenders from Japan, Europe and the United States to extend the deadlines on the repayment of Korean loans.
 
PROFIT RISKS
Eyes on Asia
Moody's downgraded the government debt of South Korea, Indonesia and Thailand to "junk" status. That hurts their ability to raise money to pull themselves out of their financial crises. American technology companies say Asia hasn't yet caused them much of a problem, but they are watching vigilantly to see whether first-quarter profits will suffer. Intel, for instance, says the impact has been negligible, because some of the hard-hit countries aren't big markets for computer chips. But it is keeping China and India under scrutiny, wary that the turmoil might spill over.
 
WALL STREET
Stock-Pricing Settlement
Thirty brokerage firms, including some of the biggest and most trusted names on Wall Street, agreed to pay about $900 million to settle a civil suit contending that they schemed with one another to fix prices on the Nasdaq stock market. The case was unusual, focusing not on investor complaints about theft and fraud, but on accusations of collusion among competing brokers to fix stock prices. Investigations of the practices led to reforms in Nasdaq pricing. Brokerages taking part in the settlement included Merrill Lynch; Goldman, Sachs, and Morgan Stanley, Dean Witter, Discover.
 
HEALTH AND BEAUTY
Tossing That Toupee
The Food and Drug Administration has approved the first-ever anti-baldness pill, from Merck. In studies, the pill promoted the growth of hair or at least stopped hair loss in 83 percent of the men who took it. "This is not a panacea," but it is promising, said Dr. E. William Frank, a dermatologist in Boston. The studies also show a slight risk of impotence, but critics mainly say the whole quest for a baldness cure is frivolous. Still, "one man's frivolity," said Dr. Michael Weintraub, the director of the F.D.A. office that evaluated the drug, Propecia, "is another man's serious problem." He should know; he, himself, is bald, like 33 million other American men. None of the men who took the pill in experiments grew back a full head of hair. But before-and-after photographs showed visible improvement in two-thirds of those taking the pill. Propecia is to be available in pharmacies in January, at a cost of $45 to $49 a month.
 
SPYING
Checking Out the Neighbors
With the latest spy satellite, anyone with a credit card can view secret military bases and even look over the high fences of neighbors' backyards. The satellite, launched last week in Russia for an American company, Earthwatch, based near Denver, is the first of a dozen that companies around the world plan to put into space in the next decade. It is supposed to see features on the ground as small as 10 feet long -- even cars and trucks, as well as buildings, roads and bridges. And customers are said to be lining up, at prices that climb to as high as $725 an image. The devices raise knotty security and privacy questions, but the companies prefer to emphasize their usefulness in map-making, law enforcement, oil exploration and disaster relief. And in a surreal reversal of spying norms, they are even likely to be used by civilians checking out military targets; for example, public interest groups hope to monitor arms treaties.
 
BOND BOOMLET
Back to the 60's
Long-term interest rates may be closing in on levels that investors have not seen for almost 30 years. Whether yields fall to that range, around 5.75 percent, will depend on how quickly the robust economy begins to slow. The economy grew at an annual pace of nearly 4 percent through the third quarter.
 
FASHION
The Wandering Yuppie
Asians have been a mainstay of the fashion houses. Many design companies have pegged their growth to the Pacific, mostly with goodies like handbags and perfumes that fit in the overhead compartment of an airplane. But Asia's tumult is being felt in Paris, Milan and New York. "The wealthy Asian consumer just went over the cliff," said Edward Yardeni, chief economist at the investment bank Deutsche Morgan Grenfell. "Most of these high-end retailers that had been counting on Asian yuppies are stuck with American yuppies for now." Gucci's stock is down, and LVMH Moet Hennessy-Louis Vuitton, the largest luxury products company in the world, is suffering.
 
ENTERTAINMENT
Seinfeld Walks, NBC Hurts
"Seinfeld," the most popular television comedy of the 1990's, is calling it quits. Jerry Seinfeld, the show's creator and star, said that after nine seasons it was time to end production, and "as a comedian, my sense of timing is everything." The loss is a blow to NBC, which has lost prime-time strength this season. The show has anchored NBC's big Thursday night since 1993, leading the network to its No. 1 position in the ratings and to record-making profits, approaching $1 billion this year. "Seinfeld" alone has made profits estimated at more than $200 million a year, and for the last full year, including repeats, it has been the most-watched show ever.
 
TRAVEL AGENTS
Man vs. Machine
Surf the Web, but hold on to your travel agent. That's the advice from PC Magazine, which found that "if a lower fare comes along two minutes after you book yours, no travel Web site has any means of letting you know, as a travel agent can." It tried out 15 sites and said the best was www.biztravel.com, which among other things can book flights with frequent-flier miles.
 
TOURISM
Terror Stings Egypt
Winter has long been considered a choice time to see the Pyramids. But this year, the massacre at Luxor is leaving hotel rooms empty and tourist guides unemployed. Tourists lined up to leave Luxor right away after the attack. Egypt's Tourism Minister, Mamdou el-Beltagi, said, "We are facing the biggest crisis in the history of tourism in Egypt." Before the attack, 1997 seemed likely to be a record tourist year. Now, cancellations have left landmarks like the 110-year-old Winter Palace Hotel in Luxor all but empty.

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
On the Way Out the Door, Some Last Words on Finance

 BODY:
Taking stock is common at this time of year, but these five people, each from a different sphere of personal finance, have more reason than most to do so: They are leaving their jobs. Here, in interviews with Sharon R. King, are their parting shots on the achievements, failures and futures of their respective fields, as well as some practical advice for 1998.
 
MARGARET M. RICHARDSON left her post as Commissioner of the Internal Revenue Service in May. She is now a partner at Ernst & Young.
Whenever major tax-law changes are enacted, the effect on investments is of interest. But there is little evidence that the 1997 changes have had a significant impact. The reduction of the capital-gains tax rate may have persuaded some investors to sell low-basis assets and reinvest in more attractive investments, but such proceeds do not seem to have moved out of the financial markets. 
 But because the gap between capital gains tax rates and ordinary rates was widened, investors who can realize returns as capital gains instead of as ordinary income will generally be better off. And since qualified plans and I.R.A.'s are not subject to current tax liability, assets which produce ordinary income and short-term capital gains are best allocated to these vehicles, as long as immediate access is not needed. The new Roth I.R.A. can yield tax-free rather than tax-deferred distributions, so a more aggressive investment strategy for such an I.R.A. might be appropriate.
 During my four years as Commissioner, the I.R.S. worked hard to make things easier for taxpayers -- the creation of a Web site, the expansion of electronic and telephone filing and of electronic payment. But people have never liked the tax collector, and the Congressional overseers keep changing their minds about what they want the service to do. There have been charges that the I.R.S. has been too aggressive, but they have boiled down to about four cases where the service violated its own procedures, although the impression was left that this was company policy.
 I think there will be more oversight in the future. That is good; we need to remind people they work for the taxpayer.
 
FRANK LALLI, managing editor of Money magazine for eight years, will become senior executive editor of Time Inc. in early January.
Personal-finance journalism has democratized finance in the United States by teaching ordinary people how to take advantage of things once available only to the rich. We have also encouraged investors to move into higher-return assets like stocks.
 But we often succumb to a horse-race mentality, and overstress performance in the recent past instead of encouraging people to stay focused on the long term. And we have never been able to educate new small investors adequately about risk. They therefore may not be fully prepared for the next devastating bear market -- whenever that comes.
 If the market's volatility continues, you'll see less emphasis on short-term performance because it's not going to be there. Common sense will come back into reporting and editing. Until then, you just keep hammering at the need for caution.
 
STEVEN M. H. WALLMAN, a Securities and Exchange commissioner for three years, resigned this fall to become a senior fellow at the Brookings Institution.
The world no longer fits easily within our regulatory scheme. The cross-border raising of capital is pressuring our regulators to move toward international accounting standards, for example. Cross-border scams and frauds will demand greater communication, cooperation and coordination at the international regulatory level.
 And as financial services continue to converge here and elsewhere, the usual concept of regulating entities based on whether they are banks or securities firms or insurance companies is becoming antiquated. Britain and Australia are reorganizing their financial-sector regulation to reflect this. Eventually, we will have to do so also -- focusing not on the entity or product being regulated, but on systemic risk, investor protection, firm solvency and the like.
 Also, the on-line world is creating vast opportunities for new types of financial services. It allows for new types of scams, too, but the existing regulatory scheme frustrates the innovation. Regulators need to stop issuing detailed regulations that specify a particular mode of compliance and instead establish standards that market participants can comply with in a variety of ways
 
JOHN C. BOGLE has moved from chairman to senior chairman of the Vanguard Group, which is known for index funds, vehicles with low fees and low turnover.
 This industry must do a better job serving investors. Performance of the average mutual fund has lagged behind the S.& P. 500, primarily due to expenses. We have made staggering profits for the people who are running funds, but, since the industry began, expenses have risen 25 percent -- despite the huge economies of scale now in mutual funds.
 And portfolio turnover is too high. An average portfolio changes almost every year. I think that's the better part of insanity; it's also expensive for investors who are paying taxes. Costs matter. It's easy to forget about costs when your fund does well, but investors should be looking at that.
 In the rush to bring in 401(k) assets, funds are also ignoring investors' needs. Marketing brings in money, but it simply makes the funds bigger and bigger and puts them in a straightjacket because the number of significant investments you can make is limited by your size.
 
ROBERT E. DENHAM resigned as chairman and chief executive of Salomon Inc. in November, when the company completed its merger with Smith Barney. He has not decided what he will do next.
 Looking ahead to 1998, the caution light is on. We've had a fairly prolonged period in which the value of stocks has increased faster than the value of businesses. That does not go on forever. Investors should remember some of the old-fashioned virtues: Invest only in what you believe in, and remember that it's not the worst thing in the world to hold some cash.
 The biggest finance story of the decade is the spread of the American style of financial markets around the world. That suggests that the investment banking community has been doing something very right.
 It takes a real global scale to compete in these markets. I don't see any risk that consolidation will go so far as to reduce competition, but, as the industry operates globally, there will be a need for more regulatory coordination. Also, the experience in Asia points up the importance of improving banking regulations outside of the G-7 countries and the need for greater transparency in the financial condition of banks.

HEADLINE: The 10 Biggest Little Stories of 1997;
Record Numbers On the Food Line

BYLINE: By AMY WALDMAN 

 BODY:
As news goes -- the big single events that chill spines, get the pulse racing and last for weeks -- it was not the busiest of years.
Not that the big news did not exist. There was Abner Louima and the awful brutality against him and the plight of the deaf Mexicans in horrific conditions in Queens. These stories seemed to capture deeper urban truths. But the mayoral election, part political funeral, part coronation, was largely a dud, devoid of the kind of psychological venting a mayoral race can provide. Even the shooting rampage atop the Empire State Building, with its potential for intense symbolism, was a tribute to one individual's madness, providing little illumination, and it passed from notice quickly. 
 Crime continued to drop; tourists continued to come; a Metropolitan Transportation Authority surplus led to new fare structures. But to understand the year it might be better to look at the city as it unfolded day to day, beyond the biggest headlines -- its tiny dramas; its newest playthings; the neighborhoods that grew; the great desire for order that seemed to grip many neighborhoods; the degree of commercial vitality.
So instead of the biggest stories of 1997, we examine the biggest little ones.
AS the stock market was reaching new highs, the Brooklyn offices of Catholic Charities were witnessing records of their own: the numbers of people seeking food assistance.
In the fall, employees at food pantries in Sheepshead Bay and Brooklyn Heights reported that requests had doubled over the previous year. Citywide, the story was the same (although the percentage increases were lower). The rise in need prompted the city to agree in November to provide an extra $2 million to restock pantries.
Exactly why requests were increasing was unclear. The cutoff of food stamps to some 50,000 legal immigrants prompted more demand for emergency aid, but the increases largely predated that cutoff. The people coming in were not welfare mothers being forced off the rolls but the working poor, unable to cover rent and buy food on minimum-wage jobs.
The same sense of mystery swirled around the beginnings of welfare reform. The numbers of people receiving public assistance continued to drop, but it was unclear where they were going. Some, certainly, swelled the ranks of workfare program; some may have found jobs, as unemployment dropped, to 8.1 percent, over the past six months. Others may have simply done with less. Merchants in areas where people depend heavily on welfare complained of less ready cash, particularly for luxury items.
That more people were working, or anticipating work, was suggested by the length of the waiting lists at child care centers like Nuestros Ninos, in Williamsburg, where 1,000 children are awaiting slots and 600 are enrolled.
But there were no certainties about how people were getting by as government's new hard line became reality. Certainly, as conversations in housing projects and food pantries suggested, many poor people remained heavily dependent on government benefits.
The affordable-housing market continued to tighten, as deaf Mexicans and countless others living in illegal, and sometimes dangerous, sublets, demonstrated. Cuts in housing funds at the Federal, state and local levels have pushed more people into shelters, and have made it harder to get out.
But the talk among families waiting for frozen turkey and toys at the St. John's Bread and Life food pantry in Bedford-Stuyvesant on Tuesday was not of seismic shifts wrought by policy changes, although some did speak of cuts in benefits and baffling new rules. Instead, they spoke of enduring struggles: no jobs, or jobs too skimpy to make ends meet and still buy a child a Christmas basketball. They described scrabbling together help from family, church, charity and government to survive.
"Nobody is rich," said a woman at St. John's, summing up her neighborhood's economic situation. "Because if we were rich, we wouldn't be sitting here." AMY WALDMAN
