HEADLINE: The 10 Biggest Little Stories of 1997;
Carry Nation, New York City

BYLINE: By JESSE MCKINLEY 

 BODY:
As news goes -- the big single events that chill spines, get the pulse racing and last for weeks -- it was not the busiest of years.
Not that the big news did not exist. There was Abner Louima and the awful brutality against him and the plight of the deaf Mexicans in horrific conditions in Queens. These stories seemed to capture deeper urban truths. But the mayoral election, part political funeral, part coronation, was largely a dud, devoid of the kind of psychological venting a mayoral race can provide. Even the shooting rampage atop the Empire State Building, with its potential for intense symbolism, was a tribute to one individual's madness, providing little illumination, and it passed from notice quickly. 
 Crime continued to drop; tourists continued to come; a Metropolitan Transportation Authority surplus led to new fare structures. But to understand the year it might be better to look at the city as it unfolded day to day, beyond the biggest headlines -- its tiny dramas; its newest playthings; the neighborhoods that grew; the great desire for order that seemed to grip many neighborhoods; the degree of commercial vitality.
So instead of the biggest stories of 1997, we examine the biggest little ones.
A YEAR ago, Alan Whelan, a veteran bar owner, had three establishments up and running in Greenwich Village, a mini-fiefdom partly supported by drunken fraternity boys, New Jersey adventurer-seekers and refugees from the Lion's Head, the legendary Christopher Street pub, which closed.
Today, Mr. Whelan is down to one bar, the Lion's Den, on Sullivan Street. In early August, he closed his Red Lion, on Bleecker Street, after he said he grew tired of fighting about zoning restrictions and noise complaints. Three weeks later, his upscale bistro, Peter's Backyard, on 10th Street, was closed by a Federal bankruptcy judge because of mounting debt arising from a lack of business and from legal fees paid to fight a block association over a proposed backyard dining area.
Sometimes it seems as if the spirit of Carry Nation were sweeping New York. Mr. Whelan's situation has become increasingly common for night life entrepreneurs as citizens' groups, local and state authorities and new legislation have been cracking down on what some consider an overload of bothersome bars. All this has come at a time when the city's economy has primed people to party.
In Washington Heights, this month Joseph Lechle found Community Board 12 strongly opposed to his plan to open a club at West 157th Street and Broadway despite his 40-year career of running bars in the area. In the same neighborhood, three Hispanic-owned dining spots were recently fined and temporarily closed by inspectors, who cited a law that bars more than three people from dancing in establishments that are not licensed as cabarets.
Similar problems beset Greenwich Village's venerable Stonewall Inn, at 53 Sheridan Square, the birthplace of the gay rights movement. The bar's owner, Dominic DeSimone, said he was in financial straits because of enforcement of the dancing ban, which followed run-ins with the neighbors complaining about noise. In Chelsea, a popular gay bar, the Rome Bar, at Eighth Avenue and 25th Street, was threatened with losing its liquor license because of community complaints to the State Liquor Authority about noise and underage drinking. And the China Club was forced to move from the Upper West Side to near Times Square.
In October, three councilmen introduced a zoning measure that would prohibit the conversion of small retail spaces on residential streets into new bars. The Council is expected to consider it in 1998.
Things got so bad for rock clubs that they were forced into an un-rock-and-roll maneuver: organization. The New York Nightlife Association, consisting of about 50 establishments, was founded in late 1996 to fight for their customers' right to party. It didn't help much. The Council passed a number of stiff new antinoise penalties in October.
Still, there has been plenty of merrymaking. The East Village continued its metamorphosis from countercultural hot spot to alcoholic theme park, with new bars seemingly opening wherever entrepreneurs could put a beer tap and moldy couches.
Even there, neighborhood advocates have begun to rein things in. In two separate votes, Community Board 3 approved moratoriums on new liquor licenses on Avenue A and on Ludlow Street, two bar-laden streets.
Some establishments are fighting back. Limelight, a dance club at West 20th Street and Avenue of the Americas, was closed last year after its owner, Peter Gatien, was arrested on Federal drug charges. It looks as if it will reopen next year. But community members have said they will fight it. JESSE MCKINLEY

SECTION: Section 14; Page 9; Column 3; The City Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 724 words

HEADLINE: The 10 Biggest Little Stories of 1997;
The Showdown At Brooklyn Junction

BYLINE: By AMY WALDMAN 

 BODY:
As news goes -- the big single events that chill spines, get the pulse racing and last for weeks -- it was not the busiest of years.
Not that the big news did not exist. There was Abner Louima and the awful brutality against him and the plight of the deaf Mexicans in horrific conditions in Queens. These stories seemed to capture deeper urban truths. But the mayoral election, part political funeral, part coronation, was largely a dud, devoid of the kind of psychological venting a mayoral race can provide. Even the shooting rampage atop the Empire State Building, with its potential for intense symbolism, was a tribute to one individual's madness, providing little illumination, and it passed from notice quickly. 
 Crime continued to drop; tourists continued to come; a Metropolitan Transportation Authority surplus led to new fare structures. But to understand the year it might be better to look at the city as it unfolded day to day, beyond the biggest headlines -- its tiny dramas; its newest playthings; the neighborhoods that grew; the great desire for order that seemed to grip many neighborhoods; the degree of commercial vitality.
So instead of the biggest stories of 1997, we examine the biggest little ones.
CALL it citizens standing up to the joined forces of money and power. Or call it a middle-class Brooklyn neighborhood screaming, "Not in My Backyard." But certainly call it bitter.
The battle over whether to build a 500,000-square-foot mall, with space for 2,000 cars, between Eighth and 14th Avenues at 61st and 62d Street pitted the developer Charles Katz against residents of Bay Ridge and Dyker Heights. They said the mall would clot streets with cars and make the air thick with pollution. After a year of organizing, opponents of the mall, a project tentatively called Brooklyn Junction, appeared to have scored a victory when the Mayor and Gov. George E. Pataki announced their opposition shortly before the November election.
Whether suburban-style shopping -- megamalls, superstores -- should come into the city continued to be a hotly debated issue this year, with residents, developers and politicians in the fray. The larger question masked by zoning fights was whether giant, uniform chains should be allowed to pave over a city known for neighborhood character. And the fights were about process, too: Could ordinary citizens influence how their backyards took shape?
A proposed super-shopping center and multiscreen movie theater became the bete noire of Forest Hills, Queens. The Mayor, his appetite for economic development possibly balanced by his hunger for re-election, made his opposition to the movie theater known, effectively killing it and at least partly appeasing residents. The rest of the development will proceed.
The coming year, however, promises to bring renewed conflicts, large and small, over the shape new urban retail developments should take. Under the leadership of the Speaker, Peter Vallone, the City Council is formulating a rezoning plan to make it easier for "big box" stores in the city (the Mayor's rezoning proposal was rejected in 1996). Under the Speaker's plan, community boards would pre-select sites for superstores, allowing them to bypass community review. Expect a fight from neighborhood advocates who oppose any reduction in such review.
Charles Katz, too, will be back. He plans to continue with plans for Brooklyn Junction, possibly in scaled-down form. This time he may have community support. Jeffrey Reznick, who made a failed run for Councilman Anthony Weiner's seat in the Democratic primary, has organized the Community Improvement Council to support the project. He and his supporters said they had collected 4,000 signatures in support of the mall, and asked for a meeting with Joseph B. Rose, the City Planning Commissioner.
Mr. Rose was booed at the meeting by some mall proponents who are residents of Borough Park, where Mr. Katz also has his business. And the president of the Brooklyn Chinese-American Association, Paul Mak, said many Chinese-Americans living in Sunset Park believed the economic benefits of the mall could outweigh the negative impact on quality of life. "It's going to be a pitched battle," said Carmel Lopez, whose Residents and Business Coalition has led opposition to the mall. AMY WALDMAN

HEADLINE: Report Undercuts Proposal To Import More Farm Labor

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

 BODY:
The General Accounting Office has thrown water on a legislative effort, pushed by the nation's agricultural employers, to allow entry of more foreign farm workers by concluding in a new report that there is "no national agricultural labor shortage at this time."
Several leading farm industry associations have been pressing to expand the number of temporary work visas for the so-called guest workers, arguing that some regions already face labor shortages, which are bound to increase as immigration officials step up efforts to keep out -- and send back -- illegal aliens. 
 Farm worker advocates are fighting the proposed legislation, asserting that increasing the number of such guest workers will undercut the wages of field laborers nationwide and weaken efforts to unionize them.
Congress asked the G.A.O. to study whether there was a farm labor shortage after Republicans lawmakers asserted that the current law requires farmers to surmount too many bureaucratic hurdles to win permission to import foreign workers, and introduced legislation to liberalize the guest-worker program.
In a draft of the G.A.O. report made available to The New York Times by an opponent of the proposed legislation, the agency essentially backed the assertions of the United Farm Workers Union and other farm worker advocates who insisted that there was no national labor shortage.
"A sudden widespread farm labor shortage requiring the importation of large numbers of foreign workers is unlikely to occur in the near future," the draft report said, though it acknowledged that localized shortages might exist for specific crops or geographical areas.
Rejecting another argument made by farmers and ranchers, the report added that enforcement efforts by the Immigration and Naturalization Service were not expected "to significantly reduce the aggregate supply of farm workers." Farmers and ranchers fear that the new immigration restrictions may work too well, cutting them off from a large part of their labor supply.
The G.A.O.'s final report is scheduled to be made public on Wednesday. Agency officials said they might revise the 108-page draft before issuing the final report.
Under the current farm guest-worker program, known as H-2A, farm employers brought in 15,000 foreign workers last year, a small portion of the estimated labor force of 2 million farm workers, about 40 percent of whom are illegal aliens.
The guest workers typically work for two or three months on jobs ranging from sheep herding to apple picking. The bill proposed by Senator Gordon Smith, an Oregon Republican, would allow 25,000 more guest workers each year.
Under current law, farmers who want to bring in guest workers must ask the United States Department of Labor to certify that no labor surplus exists in the farmer's region. In addition, the farmer must make efforts to hire workers already in the United States and must wait for the state job service to look for workers. Senator Smith's proposal would eliminate these requirements, making it far easier to import such workers. The Smith bill, like the current law, would require farmers to pay guest workers the prevailing wage received by farm workers.
"We are facing a crisis in agriculture -- a crisis born of an inadequate labor supply, bureaucratic red tape and burdensome regulation," Mr. Smith said last month in introducing the legislation.
Bob L. Vice, former president of the California Farm Bureau Federation, told Congress in September about an Orange County vegetable farmer who lost most of his workers when immigration officials apprehended them as illegal aliens. Mr. Vice said the farmer lost 60 acres of beans and 20 acres of cabbage because the local job service referred only three workers even though he had asked for 150.
But Dolores Huerta, secretary-treasurer of the United Farm Workers, strongly denied that shortages existed. "There's definitely a surplus of farm workers," she said in a telephone interview. "That explains why there has been a drop in farm-worker wages over the last 10 to 15 years. They have dropped wages substantially because they always know there's a large pool of workers they can get."
As evidence that there is no shortage of farm labor, the G.A.O. said that of the 20 large agricultural counties around the nation that it surveyed last summer, 11 had unemployment rates more than twice the national average of 5 percent, and 15 had jobless rates 2 percentage points higher than the national rate.
The agency also noted that after accounting for inflation, the average hourly wage of farm workers fell by 17 percent from 1989 to 1995. That, the agency said, pointed to a labor surplus, rather than shortage.
And in a recent nine-month period, the agency noted, the Labor Department approved 99 percent of farmers' applications for guest workers, undercutting their assertions of a shortage.

HEADLINE: Fitting Managed Care Into Emergency Rooms

BYLINE: By PETER T. KILBORN 

DATELINE: KANSAS CITY, Mo.

 BODY:
"A kid playing soccer fell and broke his arm," said Dr. Michael Weaver, beginning the sort of anecdote that emergency room doctors routinely reel off. "His mom scoops him up and rushes him here, the nearest hospital. I start an IV to give him pain medication. Then mom runs in and says, 'Stop!'
 "She's called her insurance company," said Dr. Weaver, 44, chief emergency physician for the St. Luke's-Shawnee Mission group of hospitals here. "They tell her to take him to a hospital four miles away. Mom's in tears. The kid's in tears. We give him oral pain medication, an ice bag and a splint. Then Mom drives him to the next hospital. 
 "And for what?"
The rest of the treatment, including a cast, might have cost $300 or $400. What, Dr. Weaver asked, were the costs of the insurance intermediary who stopped the treatment and of cranking up another emergency room to treat the boy? What was the cost of the mother's anguish, gasoline and time lost from work?
"Are we just shifting money around in different pots?" Dr. Weaver asked.
With the onset of managed care, episodes like this one are increasingly common for the nation's 4,900 hospital emergency departments and the patients who turn to them 100 million times a year, according to the American College of Emergency Physicians. Partly as a result of the harsh new economics of the health system, the nation has lost 400 emergency departments in five years, mostly in inner cities and rural communities.
Managed care has intruded in a variety of ways: in determining where people get emergency care, how much treatment they get, what services hospitals provide and how it is all paid for.
A look at St. Luke's state-of-the-art department shows a hospital coping with this new environment, caught between its mission to serve patients and the shrinking resources to meet those needs as managed care seeks to control costs.
In some ways, this 24-hour-a-day pocket of the health system's safety net remains a refuge from the turbulence in medicine. Crisis and pain, not wealth or an insurance plan, still open the door. Under a Federal law that threatens fines of up to $50,000, emergency departments must see all comers, screen them, take a medical history and stabilize their wounds and infirmities.
Yet the laws that mandate care for all comers say nothing about who pays for the care, particularly for the nation's 41 million uninsured. Once, hospitals covered their emergency care costs by using profits from hospital services for customers with insurance. Under managed care, though, insurance companies have squeezed their payments to hospitals to the point where there is little, if any, excess to cover nonpaying patients.
Dr. Robert Bonness, a St. Luke's emergency physician, said doctors' fees for evaluation and treatment in the emergency room run from $60 to $120. "We're collecting 52 percent of what we charge" from insurers, he said. A decade ago, he said, he collected 75 percent.
These pressures have pushed emergency departments themselves to adopt the cost-cutting disciplines of managed care, some for the better, physicians and patients say, and some for the worse.
Fast, efficient, low-cost care is often better care, they have found. A quick CT scan that a St. Luke's patient can get right inside the department, a quick diagnosis of a stroke and quick intervention to break the clot raises the odds that the patient will go back to work, saving him and his insurer the $30,000-a-year cost of a nursing home.
But tight control of costs and services can work the other way, too. Under managed care, patients have lost their prerogatives as customers, the freedom to seek the help they and their physicians deem best. Managed care organizations that pay for the service now make those decisions.
The results, patients and doctors say, are often hassles and disruptions of care. Except for the uninsured, patients carry cards with their health plans' numbers because often they need permission before dialing 911 and going to an emergency room. Those who forget or who are too frightened or too rushed to call can be denied coverage.
Then, even after approving an emergency room visit, plans sometimes refuse payment if the doctor's eventual diagnosis -- indigestion as the cause of chest pains, for example, and not a heart attack -- reveals a benign condition.
Like patients, doctors need permission. They need approval to treat patients after stabilizing them -- to admit them to the intensive care unit, for example, or the heart, stroke or pediatric units.
"Before," Dr. Weaver said, "if I felt a patient needed to be admitted from the emergency room to the hospital, we admitted him. Now I have to call someone peripheral to the relationship who reviews my data and agrees or doesn't agree.
"Sometimes, the person you call is another doctor, and that's ideal," Dr. Weaver said. "The majority of times you get no doctor at all, sometimes not even a nurse."
Here and there, in response to a political backlash against managed care, government is intervening. Fifteen states, including Missouri, New York and Connecticut, require that a health plan that covers emergency care provide it wherever and whenever a "prudent layperson" would deem it necessary. This year's Federal balanced budget agreement grants the same protections to Medicare and Medicaid recipients.
But the laws still leave most Americans unprotected, including many in states that have enacted the prudent layperson standard. The College of Emergency Physicians says half the work force is employed by companies, mostly the largest, that self-insure and that are exempt from state regulations. They pay their share of employee health care from their own funds rather than turning to an insurance company.
The St. Luke's emergency department throbs with the erratic rhythms of the flow and needs of patients. Monitors hanging from the nurses' station ceiling trace the pulses of patients in the 18 observation rooms and 2 trauma rooms. Each bed is a narrow, motorized mattress on wheels. Each room's walls are stacked with instruments and supplies, like a high-tech auto service shop. The influence of managed care is pervasive.
"We look at our staffing, our supplies and equipment, our lab work," said Betty Moon, director of emergency services for the St. Luke's-Shawnee Mission. "There are all sorts of ways we can be leaner and yet provide quality care."
Ms. Moon has completely reorganized the staffs of the group's four emergency departments. Until a year ago, 100 nurses, paid around $20,000 a year, provided all patient care. Now patient care technicians, hired after eight weeks of training and paid half as much, have replaced 20 nurses.
Because the technicians specialize in services like bathing patients, preparing their wounds for dressing, hooking up monitoring devices, drawing blood and teaching patients to use crutches, they perform their tasks better and more quickly than nurses, who now concentrate on higher levels of care, Ms. Moon said.
For all emergency rooms, a chronic cause of expense stems from being open to all comers. Emergency rooms receive uninsured patients who turn to them for all their care, and the homeless and lonely. While saying they have coughs or chest pains, many patients really just want a blanket or a meal.
So a year ago, St. Luke's hired two emergency department social workers. Those people counsel the homeless and scour the community for services they actually need and steer them out of the emergency room.
In this climate of managed care, it is the struggle for access to emergency care and getting someone to pay for it that is often most trying for patients and physicians.
Looking lost and lethargic at St. Luke's, clearly on the edge of a coma, Scott Downum, 17, a high school senior and cheerleader, was pushed to Observation Room B in a wheelchair. A team of doctors, medical students, nurses and aides swarmed in. He, too, was threaded with tubes and monitoring devices and an IV to start an insulin drip.
Dr. Darren Alexander, a first-year resident, rubbed his palms together to warm them before examining the boy. Dr. Alexander said the boy's blood sugar level of 740 was perilously high and his blood gas reading of 7.07 perilously low, both signs that Scott was at the edge of a coma and death.
Gloria Zamora, Scott's mother, sat at the side of his bed as she would for six hours, marking improvements in his readings on an index card. She said she had always been able to get him into emergency rooms and that this was probably the 25th time she had raced him to one since he was first diagnosed with diabetes at 4.
At that time, insurance plans started picking and choosing among whom they would cover. Scott has been covered just three years, one as a child and then the last two. Often, she said, she could not get him coverage at all because of his condition.
"When it was available," Ms. Zamora said, "it was $300 to $500 a month," far beyond what she could afford as a clerical worker. Yet she was too well off to qualify for Medicaid, the state-Federal insurance program for the indigent.
Her hopes rose three years ago when she became an administrative assistant at a museum. For $150 a month she enrolled in the museum's managed care plan. But to include Scott in her coverage, she was required to wait a year. Now, as long as she stays with the museum and the premiums stay within her means, his care is assured.
Upon spotting the approach of another crisis before this visit, Ms. Zamora called Scott's health plan physician. "He said, 'Go immediately to the emergency room,' " she said.
Then, once she saw her son settled in the emergency room, she called the insurance company.
"They took my information and wanted to know the diagnosis and if he would be admitted," she said. She suspected the doctors would later want to move him to the intensive care unit, as indeed they did.
"They gave me a referral number," Ms. Zamora said. "It was a smooth procedure."
For the moment, Ms. Zamora was relieved. But managed care has taken a toll on both her finances and those of the emergency rooms that have rescued her son. "I owe them at least $70,000," she said.

(V. Craig Sands for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: A. J. Jaffe, 85, A Labor Expert At Columbia U.

BYLINE: By WOLFGANG SAXON 

 BODY:
Abram J. Jaffe, a social scientist and labor expert at Columbia University who studied the economic conditions of migrants, working women and retirees, died on Monday at a nursing home in Englewood, N.J. He was 85 and lived in Leonia, N.J.
The cause was heart failure after a long illness, his family said. 
 A. J. Jaffe, as his seven books identified him, worked as a statistician, employment expert and anthropologist. He directed the Manpower and Population Program of Columbia's Bureau of Applied Research for 26 years. He retired in 1979 but continued as a senior research associate in business and anthropology.
At age 80, with W. C. Sperber, he published the results of a lifelong interest in early human migrations, "The First Immigrants From Asia: A Population History of the North American Indians" (Plenum, 1992). Also in print is his "Handbook of Statistical Methods for Demographers" (1977). With Herbert F. Spirer, he wrote "Misused Statistics: Straight Talk for Twisted Numbers" (1986). It told general readers and journalists how to find the facts hidden in statistics presented by advocates of various causes.
Dr. Jaffe was born in Chelsea, Mass. He received his bachelor's, master's and doctoral degrees in sociology from the University of Chicago. In the 1940's, he surveyed troop morale for the War Department and taught foreign nationals at the Census Bureau how to apply its methods in their own countries.
He joined the Columbia faculty in 1950, where he produced reports on such diverse matters as productivity, employment patterns of college graduates and the absorption of Hispanic-American migrants.
He was a fellow of the American Association for the Advancement of Science and for many years edited The New York Statistician, published by the state chapter of the American Statistical Association.
Dr. Jaffe is survived by his wife of 58 years, the former Betty Gomberg; two daughters, Anne Jaffe and Barbara Rios, both of Manhattan, and two grandsons.

HEADLINE: ALL IN ONE: A special report.;
Where All Doors Are Open for Disabled Students

BYLINE: By TAMAR LEWIN 

DATELINE: WOODSTOCK, Vt.

 BODY:
One classmate had to speak his lines for him and another had to push his wheelchair into place on stage. But it was still an achievement when Jeron Fox appeared as the ghost this fall in the four performances of "Hamlet" at the high school.
Jeron, a 17-year-old with cerebral palsy, has a luminous smile, a quick sense of humor and no control over the drool dribbling down his chin as he and an instructional aide prepare for a world history test on Alexander the Great. 
 Jeron cannot talk or read well. He communicates mostly by pointing to a book of drawings that show the important people, classes, foods and feelings in his life. For formal pronouncements, the laptop computer in his wheelchair tray can use voices.
Nationally, most students like Jeron are educated in separate classrooms or separate schools, as are those with mental retardation, autism or emotional disturbances, and many of those in the far larger group of children with less pervasive problems like dyslexia or other specific learning disabilities.
But Jeron is very much part of Woodstock Union High School, where he attends some regular classes, spends some periods in one-on-one tutoring and, with his best friend, who has Down syndrome, serves as "12th man" for the football team, cheering from the bench.
As the debate over how and where to educate the nation's six million children with disabilities has become increasingly polarized -- and litigious -- Vermont is singular in its commitment to integrating severely disabled students into regular classrooms, keeping almost 9 out of 10 in regular classes for most of the day.
And unlike most states, Vermont has always kept students with learning disabilities, who make up nearly half the state's 11,000 children with disabilities, in regular classes.
Although Vermont's record is unlikely to be matched anytime soon, most states, in response to prodding from the Federal Government and recent amendments to the Federal disability law, are also moving toward greater integration.
In Vermont, where the rugged terrain has helped produce a strong tradition of local control, experiments with integration -- the latest term for what is also called mainstreaming or inclusion -- began in a few districts in the early 1980's. But they did not take off statewide until 1989, when the state changed its school financing formulas to remove financial incentives for keeping children in segregated placements, giving districts just as much money to provide services in a child's home school.
Since 1975, the Federal Individuals With Disabilities in Education Act has guaranteed children with disabilities the right to free and appropriate educations in the least restrictive environment. Under amendments this year, all states have to adopt financing plans that, as with Vermont's, offer no financial incentive to place disabled children outside their home schools.
Special education costs $30 billion a year in Federal, state and local money, and it accounts for 19 percent of all money spent on schools, up from 17.8 percent in 1991, according to a study of nine representative districts by the Economic Policy Institute in Washington, a nonpartisan research group.
There are huge differences from state to state on the placement of children with disabilities, who make up 11 percent of the school population. Where such children are best taught is a complicated question with pedagogical, ethical and financial overtones.
The number of children who have diagnoses of disabilities, and especially learning disabilities, has been steadily increasing. That has led some educators to contend that disabilities are vastly overdiagnosed and that special education is too often used as a catchall for poor minority-group students whose difficulties may stem from poor teaching methods, behavior problems or difficult family environments, and not disabilities.
In many states, most students with disabilities go to separate schools, separate classrooms for children with a particular disability or "resource rooms," where special-education teachers work with them individually for parts of the day.
There are classrooms for the retarded, the autistic, the deaf, the emotionally disturbed -- and hundreds and hundreds of classes for children with learning disabilities, who are painfully slow to learn reading or mathematics skills.
Many educators and parents believe that segregating children with disabilities is bad, both educationally and morally. They say such a policy undermines the development of both disabled children, by failing to give them a chance to develop the skills and relationships that they will need as adults, and other children, by preventing beneficial contact with the full range of people in their communities.
"Every child is part of a community, and public schools should serve all the children in the community," said Richard Schattman, a principal in Swanton whose office wall has a political button that says, "Release the Hostages: Full Inclusion."
"It's the right thing to do."
 
The Two Sides
Inclusion Vs. Exclusion
The Federal official in charge of enforcing the disability law, Judith E. Heumann, has her reasons for believing in integration.
"I use a motorized wheelchair, I can't lift my arms, and I can't go to the bathroom by myself," said Ms. Heumann, Assistant Education Secretary for the Office of Special Education and Rehabilitation Services. "When I was a kid in Brooklyn and my mother wheeled me over to the neighborhood school for kindergarten, they said I couldn't come there, I was a fire hazard. I was sent away to another school."
These days, Ms. Heumann tries to convince parents that integration has benefits.
"Education is academic, but it's also social, learning how to live in a community, learning about differences," she said. "I tell parents who are afraid to send a child with disabilities into a regular setting that overprotection does no service when that disabled child becomes an adult. If your child was out of sight, out of mind, that doesn't change. People who might have become their friends in school won't know them."
Academically, too, she said, all children can benefit from inclusion.
"The methods that teachers learn from working with the disabled, and individualizing instruction, are useful with other students, as well," Ms. Heumann said. "In a way, you can see every child as having special needs. So the ideal is a system in which every child gets an individualized education."
But many educators and parents say separate classes can better meet the needs of those with disabilities -- and of the other children whose educations may be disrupted.
"Even if most kids can be fully included, there has to be an option to pull out a student for some one-on-one instruction," said Nancy Safer, executive director of the Council for Exceptional Children. "We'll always need the full continuum of services."
Inclusion is a particular challenge in big urban systems like New York City's, where a huge special-education program has more than 100,000 children, disproportionately minority members. Last year, the Schools Chancellor, Rudy Crew, announced plans to overhaul the system, with an eye to moving thousands of pupils to regular classrooms that would then have extra teachers.
But simply moving children into regular classrooms does not guarantee integration.
"A student can be more isolated and segregated in a normal classroom than in special education," Mr. Schattman said. "Inclusion isn't about placing the kid. It's about making the placement successful both for the kid and for the rest of the class. And it's not easy. You need small classes, lots of planning time and staff that believes in it."
Some special-education experts worry that the inclusion movement may lead to dumping children with special needs into classes where they will be ignored or taunted, and eliminating the special services and support that they receive in settings intended just for them.
"It has not been demonstrated that regular classrooms, even fortified regular classrooms using the best practices, can accommodate all children all the time," said Douglas Fuchs, a professor of special education at Vanderbilt University. "The full inclusionists honestly believe that creating a situation in which teachers individualize instruction for each student is a terrific goal we should all dedicate our lives to. So we should kick away the crutch of special education. But that's a high-stakes game, and I'm not sure it's realistic."
Nor are all parents and advocates for children with disabilities convinced that it is the correct goal.
"With severe retardation," Mr. Fuchs said, "the goal is usually to have the child accepted and treated well in the community, social goals that can be met in a classroom where the teacher and other children interact with them and show some tenderness. It's more complex for children with learning disabilities, for whom the academic goals are more ambitious. Since there are no studies showing that being in a regular classroom improves their performance, most parents and advocates feel that time doing special drills in a resource room or a separate class is O.K., if that will better help the child learn."
By all accounts, the most difficult children to integrate are those in the growing category of emotional disabilities, children who may be disruptive, abusive, sexually aggressive or violent.
Even in Vermont, an increasing number of emotionally disabled children are being placed in separate settings.
 
The Problems
Balances, Social And Financial
For families and schools alike, placement involves a complicated calculus, balancing academic and social needs.
Jeron's mother, Bethany Fox, raves about the support and attention her son received from the school system. She said she stayed in Vermont, even though other states offered better work opportunities, because she did not believe that anywhere else would do as well for Jeron. But even she occasionally wonders whether a special school like the one that he attended for two years before Woodstock became so inclusive would be better.
"Sometimes I still think it might be a good thing," she said. "But I know they're pulling for him here, and they think he belongs here."
Vermont's commitment to inclusion came in part from the Education Commissioner, Marc Hull, a former minister who was the state's Special Education Director in the 1980's. Mr. Hull worked with school districts that wanted to become more inclusive and helped convince the Legislature that a financing formula that did not pay more to place the disabled outside regular classrooms would not only enrich lives and fit the tradition of local control, but would also help control runaway costs of special education.
But costs have continued to rise, as special-education money followed the children to regular classes. In the early 1970's, the state spent 85 percent of its $3.6 million annual budget for disabled students on separate classes and separate schools, including $100,000-a-year-per-pupil residential schools like the one that Jeron had attended.
But these days, 85 percent of the special-education budget, now at $110 million, is spent on extra teachers, aides, therapists and services in regular schools.
Some Vermont officials worry that the leadership on inclusion may become a mixed blessing.
"If you get people with serious disabilities moving in for the services they can get, it's like Mexicans coming across the border," said a Vermont education official who insisted on anonymity. "On one hand you're happy that they're going to get a better quality of life. On the other you worry that at some point, if you get too many, it will break the bank and change the nature of what you have."
 
A Town's Reaction
Fears and Strains Are Confronted
Families in Woodstock, a tiny picture-postcard town where tourists flock to admire the fall foliage, know one another pretty well. So it was no secret among the parents of preschoolers that the group starting kindergarten this fall posed unusual challenges. The 30 children would include autistic twins, a child who has been developmentally delayed in the aftermath of leukemia, two with speech and language problems and another, not yet fully evaluated, who shows autistic characteristics.
Some parents worried about how their children would fare, especially with Joe and Eddie Lawlor, the autistic twins whom they had seen around town with their mother, Lisa. To allay those fears, the school invited all the incoming kindergarten parents to a meeting, introduced the special-education staff and talked about plans for the year.
"We'd heard whispers in the wind and we were real concerned, and Lisa was concerned about how her kids would be received," said the principal, Mark Genovesi. "We were hopeful that if we took the offensive we would take the wind out of their sails. We explained that inclusion means all kids get what they need, not just disabled kids, and this wasn't going to affect the quality of education. And it's worked. To date no one's come and beat down the door to complain."
Officials have been working with the Lawlor boys since they were 2. The first goal was to have them sit in a chair for 20 seconds, an effort that took four months. Making eye contact was even harder. So it has gone, task by painful task.
Each boy was assigned an individual aide. For a year, the aides made an unused room in the Lawlor house into the "schoolroom," where the boys learned about sitting for circle time and putting away toys.
The special teaching does not stop. Because of the boys' sensitivity to loud noises and crowds, they needed individual instruction about fire drills.
They cannot yet manage the noisy confusion of arrival. So each day, they enter school by a separate door. A closetlike room outside their classroom has been soundproofed to make a place where an aide can calm them for a few minutes if the classroom noise becomes too upsetting, or the boys' behavior becomes too upsetting to the class.
The victory of the class is that a visitor who walks in is not immediately aware of anything beyond a calm teacher who is working with a lively, wriggly, happy class. On one morning, Joe, a sweet-faced, brown-haired boy who has not begun to talk, sat quietly with his classmates as the teacher read "The Very Busy Spider."
The aide who works with Joe sat next to him, just off the rug, occasionally pointing his face toward the teacher or handing him a piece of Silly Putty or an egg timer to keep his hands occupied.
At one point, Joe began making noises, an "unh-unh" chant. The other children seemed unfazed, and the noise stopped after a few minutes.
It took Joe a long time to focus, But by midway through the book he was following along like the others.
The class uses a "choice board," with schematic pictures of different activities that the children can choose. They stick their name cards under the one they want, an approach to make communication easier for Joe and Eddie, and one that has proved popular with the whole class.
"It's a lot of work," Ms. Lawlor said. "But the bottom line for me is that I can have my children live with me, and they're part of this community. And that wouldn't be true everywhere."
Woodstock was not always so inclusionary. The change occurred in the late 1980's, after the district, made up of several small towns, had hired Kathleen Callahan to lead its special-education staff.
Since then, the district has eliminated separate classrooms for children with disabilities, stopped sending people to residential programs and strengthened its special-education staff to 26 professionals, plus dozens of paraprofessionals.
At the yearly Town Meeting, there are always questions about services to children with disabilities, which last year accounted for $500,000 of the $2.5 million budget for the elementary school.
"It's always an issue, since education is 60 percent of our tax bill here, and special education is a big chunk of that," said the chairwoman of the Woodstock Elementary board, Marian Koetsier. "Actually, I ran for the board in 1991 on the platform of figuring out why this budget was getting out of hand. And right around then a group was charged with studying our special-education services. They went in skeptical, but they came out believing, as I do, that we're mostly doing things right."
 
The Hopes
'Breaking Down Some of the Lines'
Across the state, in midafternoon at South Burlington High School, 20 students were working in the Enrichment Center, a large sunny space with 10 tables, computer carrels and a group of offices around the edge.
At one table, two ninth graders, one learning disabled and one not, worked with an art teacher, who spent the period going over their English homework with them.
At another table, a special-education teacher prepared five boys -- two with learning disabilities, one with attention deficit disorder and two without disabilities -- for a American history examination, reviewing the factors leading to the Industrial Revolution.
One boy had a pair of crutches at the table with him, and a wheelchair waited against the wall, for another student who uses a walker most of the day but sometimes needs the wheelchair.
Among the 860 students in the school, 82 are on the official caseload of the special-education staff. Most have specific learning disabilities, usually involving dyslexia, a reading disability. About one-fourth are emotionally disabled, and 12 are retarded, with I.Q.'s lower than 70.
More than 100 students pass through the enrichment center daily. Usually students without disabilities can schedule only one period a day there. Those with disabilities may receive special services there two periods a day.
Some of the brightest students go to the center to tutor their classmates or receive help on advanced-placement courses.
"We wanted to get away from the stigma of the resource room," said the coordinator of the center, Tim Warren. "At the beginning I know some kids called this the dummy room. So we still have a ways to go. But I think we're getting there. We're breaking down some of the lines."

SECTION: Section 1; Page 1; Column 6; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1582 words

HEADLINE: PARTIES RETHINK THEIR CORE GOALS AS DEFICIT SHRINKS

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 27

 BODY:
After years dominated by Federal budget deficits, the capital faces a new quandary: how to deal with the prospect of surpluses.
Ever since the early years of the Reagan Presidency, the Federal deficit has defined the political debate and limited the range of social policy. It caused George Bush to break with his party's dogma of tax cutting. It frustrated Democrats who wanted activist government. It gave a brief impetus to Ross Perot. 
 Now, though, good times are presenting both President Clinton and the Republican leaders of Congress with a challenge to keep their political parties from fracturing and to prevent a new burst of unrestrained tax cutting and spending.
To tamp down the enthusiasm as the strong economy brings a surge of revenue to the Government, Mr. Clinton has taken to warning that the Federal deficit has yet to disappear. But while he urges caution, some analysts on Wall Street are predicting that a surplus will appear, perhaps as early as this fiscal year -- and well before the target date of 2002, which was set in the summer's balanced budget agreement.
Just as the end of the cold war left America's foreign policy makers on uncharted terrain, this prospect of surpluses radically alters the domestic debate, causing both parties to re-examine what they should stand for. "This is a historic starting point for one of the most important dialogues that we are going to have in our generation," Speaker Newt Gingrich said before Congress adjourned this year.
The last time the Government ran a surplus was in the 1969 fiscal year. The last time the Government ran two successive surpluses was in 1956 and in 1957 -- when Bill Clinton and Newt Gingrich were schoolboys.
The budget act of 1990, which set in place the budgeting procedures used on Capitol Hill, does not even have mechanisms to deal with a surplus. In a discipline geared to keeping the deficit from growing, it requires that any tax cuts or recurring spending increases enacted by Congress be offset by equal spending reductions or revenue increases.
"There's been a remarkable turnaround," said Gene Sperling, Mr. Clinton's chief economic adviser. "In the blink of an eye, we've gone from being accused that our balanced budget would only balance for one second to being questioned about why we won't admit there's a huge surplus and why we're being so stingy about talking about spending it."
The visions of plenty could turn out to be premature. No one is certain how the financial turmoil in Asia will affect the growth of the United States economy, and unexpected downturns could always occur.
White House and Congressional budget forecasters are planning to release new projections next month showing a positive development: that the deficit for the current fiscal year will be $20 billion to $30 billion less than the $58 billion the White House had previously forecast.
Recent Treasury Department figures go even further and suggest the possibility of a surplus. They show that in the last 12 months the Federal Government took in $2.4 billion more than it spent. And some Wall Street analysts are already projecting a $20 billion to $40 billion surplus by the time the current fiscal year ends in September.
Ever since an enormous structural deficit developed after the 1981 Reagan tax cut plan was approved by Congress, the red ink has constrained the domestic debate.
Even the most liberal Democrats acknowledged that insolvency hemmed in the possibility of activist government. And Republicans fractured between a wing of "deficit hawks" devoted to balancing the budget and a supply-side, tax-cutting faction, many of whose members believed that lower tax rates would bring higher revenues.
Now, just the glimmer of a post-deficit era has set off political pressures in Washington to break free of the balanced budget agreement struck by President Clinton and the Congress during the summer and allow more social spending and more tax cuts, or a more radical tax overhaul.
"Ultimately, the biggest problem this year will be: Can the leadership keep its troops in line?" said Stanley Collender, the managing director of the Federal budget consulting group for Burson-Marsteller, the public relations firm.
In the Republican Party, divisions are already sharpening between the balanced budget advocates and the ardent tax cutters. Disputes are also looming between those Republicans who want a business-oriented tax cut, like a further reduction in the capital gains tax, and those who want a family-oriented tax cut, like elimination of the "marriage penalty," under which many married couples who file jointly are pushed into higher tax brackets because of their combined income.
Some Republicans are pushing an entire revision of the income tax code to put in place a flat tax or a national sales tax.
The Republicans also have their spenders. Some want to increase military outlays or, like Representative Bud Shuster of Pennsylvania, chairman of the House Transportation and Infrastructure Committee, they want to spend billions on new highway and bridge projects.
Democrats, meanwhile, are trying to sort out how to promote a new social agenda without reviving their image as big spenders.
Like the Republicans, the Democrats have their fiscally conservative wing urging restraint. But there is strong sentiment in the party for programs to rebuild crumbling schools, address urban decay, provide health care for the uninsured and sponsor retraining programs for workers displaced by the information age economy.
Mr. Clinton has already indicated that in some policy areas he will be looking to use tax incentives instead of spending increases as a tool of social policy. He plans to propose tax incentives next year to promote day-care programs and to spur development of energy efficient technologies to curb global warming.
In both parties, new political strategies are being formulated. Some Republicans are trying to move away from advocating a balanced budget, per se, to emphasize shrinking government.
"I'm not a conservative because I believe in a balanced budget," said Representative Bill Archer, the chairman of the Ways and Means Committee. "I'm a conservative because I believe that a smaller government with less power means greater freedom and more opportunity for the American people."
Democrats in the Administration and Congress, meanwhile, say they are trying to guard against being stampeded by the Republicans into large tax cuts, like the Reagan tax cuts of 1981. The Democrats say they fear such tax cuts could bring new deficits early in the next century -- which, in turn, could then bring decades of cutting back government.
"The worst thing that could be done," Mr. Sperling said, "is if -- based on a surplus -- long-term promises on either tax cuts or spending were made that would prove completely unsustainable if the surplus went away or was less than projected. Repeating the mistakes of 1981 should not be an option."
This ferment inside and between the parties is likely to drive the 1998 midterm Congressional elections and carry into the Presidential campaign of 2000. But it carries a high risk -- that it will unravel the very balanced budget agreement that is helping usher the nation into a new period of plenty.
Administration officials and Congressional aides said Mr. Clinton, who wants to protect his legacy as a fiscally prudent leader, was acutely aware of this problem. "There is a general concern that there could be a feeding frenzy that could blow away the fiscal progress," one Administration official said.
Representative John R. Kasich, the Ohio Republican who is chairman of the House Budget Committee, is also trying to head off sentiment for deviating from the five-year budget agreement.
"This is something we have to be vigilant about," he said. "I don't want any additional Federal spending above the agreement."
Mr. Gingrich recently weighed in by calling on Mr. Clinton to propose a balanced budget for the new fiscal year instead of waiting until 2002 to bring the budget into balance. An actual balanced budget -- as opposed to a plan to balance the budget -- would be a potent achievement on which incumbents could run in the 1998 Congressional elections.
Some fiscal watchdog groups and some lawmakers want the Government to preserve any surplus, rather than devoting it to new programs or tax cuts. Such action would effectively allow the Treasury to begin retiring some of the nation's $5.5 trillion debt and have more resources on hand for when the baby boom generation begins retiring and putting a strain on Medicare and Social Security.
The position of those who want to protect a potential surplus could be strengthened if the first surpluses to appear were small -- less than $10 billion, for example.
That would not be enough money to accommodate many big initiatives. And spending it could involve some political risk if revenue projections prove faulty. Who, after all, would want to be the politician to propose the tax cut or new program that would be blamed for taking this first surplus, a true political milestone, and spending it -- thus sending the Federal Government back into deficit?
"With it being an election year," said Representative David M. McIntosh, an Indiana Republican who is promoting the elimination of the marriage penalty, "people are not going to be willing to be the one who will say they had a balanced budget and threw it away."

HEADLINE: Charges by Mexico Link PRI Mayor to Killings

BYLINE: By IAN FISHER 

DATELINE: POLHO, Mexico, Dec. 27

 BODY:
The Attorney General's office said tonight that there was a link between the local government and the massacre of 45 people earlier this week in southern Mexico. The federal officials charged the top regional administrator not only with murder but also with providing a paramilitary group with the high-caliber weapons used in the killings.
Few details were released last night, but Everardo Moreno, the Deputy Attorney General in charge of the investigation, said Jacinto Aria Cruz, municipal president of Chenalho, a region of Chiapas, had confessed to sending the weapons in a municipal car. Mr. Moreno spoke at a news conference in the Chiapas capital of Tuxtla Gutierrez. 
 Mr. Arias was sent to a state prison, along with 23 other people charged today with murder, assault and organized crime in connection with the killings. On Friday, another 16 people were charged.
The arrest was significant because it seemed to confirm, at least on the lowest level, that members of the nation's ruling party, the Institutional Revolutionary Party, had a hand in the killings. Survivors of the attack said the assassins were members of the party. Both the Government of President Ernesto Zedillo and that of the state of Chiapas have strongly denied any involvement.
During the day as many as 3,000 people from villages deep in the mountains of southern Mexico, many trudging barefoot for five hours in the rain, fled from their homes for fear of other attacks from bands of gunmen who carried out the massacre of 45 Tzotzil Indians on Monday.
Carrying what little they owned -- cooking pots, machetes, wooden stools -- they arrived in exhausted groups this afternoon at a refugee center in this small town, only a mile from the site of the massacre. The town has already swelled to several times its normal population as people have fled growing violence that human rights groups say is being carried out by gunmen hostile those who sympathize with an Indian guerrilla group, the Zapatista National Liberation Army.
"We need to eat," a 39-year-old man who would give only his first name, Marcelino, said as he walked up the muddy side of a hill with his wife and eight children. "The children are crying because they are hungry."
The people fleeing told stories of being under virtual siege in recent months by paramilitary groups. They said the gunmen, who opposition leaders say are loyal to the nation's ruling party, have blocked off tiny dirt roads in several small towns that are the only supply route for food and have barred anyone from the leaving. The gunman also stole people's possessions and stole or extorted money, they said.
"First they demanded 10,000 pesos," said Vincente Jimenez Hernandez, 31, who was one of perhaps 500 people who fled the village of Los Chorros, reportedly a stronghold for the paramilitary groups. "Where do I get that? Then they said, 5,000 pesos. If you didn't give them the money, you were going to die. They were going to shoot you."
In his second communique this week, Subcommander Marcos, leader of the Zapatistas, released his own investigation into the killing today, saying that responsibility went much higher than the regional government. He accused both the state and federal governments of paying other Indians to fight a covert war against the Zapatistas. Nearly all of the people arrested have been other Tzotzil.
"It is not only about buying loyalties," he said. "It is a real recruiting system, a system to make governmental war against the Indians with Indians."
The exodus of refugees may come as yet another embarrassment to the PRI and to the Chiapas state government, both already under harsh public criticism because of questions about whether local police could have stopped the attack in Acteal on Monday in light of the growing violence in the region.
The events today may again raise the question of why the government did not step in earlier. The exodus was coordinated by an unusual coalition of human rights groups and nongovernmental organizations, along with the federal Army, the state of Chiapas and the Attorney General's office.
It was unclear this evening which of the groups began the effort to lead the people out of the besieged towns. Uriel Jarquin, deputy secretary of government for the state of Chiapas, which has come under the harshest criticism since the massacre, said his agency had sent 10 trucks to carry people from the villages, along with food and clothing.
But even as he emphasized the government's eagerness to help, Mr. Jarquin took at swipe at the human rights groups that have accused the government of not preventing the attack. "It's easy to make an accusation," he said. "If they were sure something was about to happen why didn't they do this 15 days ago? Why did they wait to act? An action is something that is done, not said."
The exact number of people who fled was not certain; aid officials said they had counted well over 3,000 people. Several hundred were seen plodding up a muddy road into Polho, and in shelters inside the town.
People from several of the towns said that they needed to leave because food was running short with no supplies coming in. Arturo Sanabria, a doctor with a nongovernmental health organization, said many of the children were sick, with severe diarrhea and respiratory problems. "You can see they are afraid," he said. "There is undernourishment, and not only among the children."
Mariano Perez Vasquez, 27, said the paramilitary groups had been becoming more aggressive in recent weeks in his village of Los Chorros, robbing and threatening people they suspected of being sympathic with the Zapatistas, who began an uprising four years ago for greater economic and political rights.
He said they forbade anyone to leave the town, and their fear was justified the day of the massacre, in which he said several of the paramilitary members in his town participated. "When they got back, they were like zombies -- the living dead," Mr. Perez said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Tzotzil Indians arriving at a refugee center in Polho in southern Mexico yesterday after fleeing their homes in fear of further attacks by gunmen like the one that killed 45 people in a nearby village on Monday. (Gerardo Magallon for The New York Times)

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: INVESTING WITH: HOWARD F. WARD;
Gabelli Growth Fund

BYLINE: By CAROLE GOULD 

 BODY:
LIKE the alchemists of old, Howard F. Ward, who runs the Gabelli Growth fund, attempts to take common ingredients -- familiar big companies, common themes, standard financial measures -- and spin them into gold.
And he has succeeded, at least, in beating his peers recently. Gabelli Growth gained 34.5 percent for the 12 months through Wednesday, compared with 21 percent for all large-capitalization growth funds. Mr. Ward also beat the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, which gained 27.3 percent in that time, according to Morningstar Inc., the fund tracking firm in Chicago. 
 For the three years ended on Wednesday, Gabelli Growth rose 29.6 percent a year, on average, handily beating its peer group (24.1 percent) and just ahead of the S.& P. 500, (29.5 percent).
A growth-at-a-price manager, Mr. Ward, 41, aims to buy large companies with solid earnings growth where the stock price is at least 20 percent below where he figures it should be. He looks for earnings growth rates of at least 12 percent a year.
Mr. Ward devised his own earnings model for valuing a five-year projected earnings stream. He compares the earnings to the yield on long-term Treasury bonds and adds a premium to compensate for equity risk.
The model produces what he calls a "fair value" price for companies that he uses as a benchmark to buy or sell. At present, the model is identifying many companies with multiples below prevailing rates.
"There are a lot of stocks that look overvalued on a short-term basis that we think look attractive on a long-term basis," he said. "When current earnings growth is compounded at a 15 percent rate, multiples come down rapidly." For example, at a 15 percent annual earnings growth rate, a company's price-to-earnings multiple is halved every five years.
Regardless of his earnings value model, Mr. Ward will not buy shares in a company if the price is more than 30 times 12-month estimated earnings. The average for the fund's companies is 22 times earnings.
Over all, he expects earnings growth of American companies to be "subdued" in 1998, averaging 5 percent. But that gain will "mask some companies' sharply falling earnings," especially in technology stocks, a result of Asia's troubles, he said.
"The volatility that we've seen in the last few months is not going to go away soon," he said. "I'd be really happy to see a 10 percent return, not 20 percent like we've had."
 Based on his earnings model, Mr. Ward has reduced the fund's stake in consumer staples to 8 percent of the portfolio, from 15 percent, tossing out companies like Procter & Gamble. He has shrunk aerospace companies, like Boeing, to 5 percent, from 12 percent, and technology to 12 percent, from 20 percent.
He called Procter & Gamble a "great company," and sold shares reluctantly as the price rose. But he said he would buy back Procter & Gamble shares if they fell into the high $60's, down from nearly $80. He also plans to buy back shares in General Electric if prices fall into the low $60's, from the $70's.
The fund's money has mostly gone into health care and financial services, two sectors that could benefit from three long-term trends he sees: the aging of the populations of the United States, Europe and Japan, the information-technology revolution and economic globalization.
The biggest sector in the fund is now financial services, at 25 percent, which stretches his unwritten rule limiting any sector to 20 percent of the fund's assets.
One reason, he said, was "the lack of attractively priced stocks in a number of sectors." He also said that "the interest rate environment favors financial services issues."
The next-biggest sectors are health care (15 percent of the portfolio), technology (12 percent) and media and business services, (10 percent each).
Mr. Ward examines how companies fit his earnings model and how they reflect the three long-term trends. He whittles the list further by looking for companies with a competitive edge: a dominant brand name, superior management, a proprietary product or limited competition.
Mr. Ward inherited 130 stocks when he took over Gabelli Growth in January 1995 from Elizabeth Bramwell, who left to start her own shop. He began selling off stocks, cutting the portfolio to 90 companies by June 1997, and has since reduced the total even more, to 55.
"We don't want our best ideas diluted by more marginal ones," said Mr. Ward, who plans to keep the fund at 50 to 60 stocks. The fund's top 10 stocks account for 40 percent of its portfolio.
Mr. Ward joined Gabelli Asset Management from Scudder, Stevens & Clark, where he worked with large-cap growth investments but ran no mutual funds.
In addition to the $930 million fund, he runs a few small institutional accounts at Gabelli, but his focus is on Gabelli Growth, which, he says is the largest holding in both his personal retirement and his nonretirement accounts. Fellow shareholders include his mother- and brothers-in-law.
 
A Fee Generator
Playing off his financial services theme, Mr. Ward is adding to his position in Northern Trust, a financial services holding company in Chicago. Northern Trust banks derive 65 percent of their revenues from fee-based businesses, primarily asset management for the wealthy, he said.
Unlike mutual funds, this type of business has few competitors, Mr. Ward said. Also unlike fund investors, these wealthy clients "won't up and leave if the market turns down, because the bank manages their entire portfolio," he said.
The bank has offices throughout the country. Its business "has a high service component, and they're good at it," he said.
He predicts a 16 percent earnings growth rate annually over the next five years, above consensus earnings forecasts of roughly 12 percent. The company's price-to-earnings multiple, based on the consensus earnings forecast for 1998 of $3.01 and Friday's close of $64.50, is 21.4.
 
Confidence in a Drug Maker
Mr. Ward's push into health care has led him into Merck, one of the nation's largest pharmaceutical companies. He said Merck had consistently produced new products because of big spending on research and development and because of a strong distribution system that was strengthened by its acquisition of Medco Containment Services.
Moreover, Merck, another of the fund's top 10 holdings, has "great management and proprietary products," he said. Wall Street consistently prices shares too low based on incomplete information, he added, noting that the shares recently jumped to $105 from $90 after the company disclosed several new products to analysts. Last week, Merck gained Food and Drug Administration approval to sell Propecia, a prescription drug for baldness.
He predicts annual earnings growth of about 20 percent over the next five years, and expects earnings of $4.40 a share in 1998, meaning that the company sells for just under 24 times projected 1998 earnings.
"We don't think that's much of a premium at all, compared to the market at 21 times earnings and the three companies that Wall Street favors," he said. By comparison, Eli Lilly sells for 35 times the consensus 1998 earnings forecast; Pfizer, for 36 and Warner-Lambert, 29.
 
A Global Brand
His second-largest holding is Gillette, which accounts for nearly 5 percent of the fund's portfolio. "It's a well-known story that goes back to the globalization theme and dominant brands," Mr. Ward said.
"Gillette is the razor business," he said. The company also produces Duracell batteries, "and both businesses travel well across different cultures around the world," he said.
He expects the company's earnings to grow at 17 percent a year over the next five years. With earnings of $3 a share expected in 1998, Gillette has a multiple of 32, just over Mr. Ward's limit of 30, and he has stopped buying shares. But he plans to add to the position if shares fall below $90.

HEADLINE: EARNING IT;
A Company Where Retirement Is a Dirty Word

BYLINE: By JULIE FLAHERTY 

DATELINE: NEEDHAM, Mass.

 BODY:
AS far as he can recall, Frederick Hartman has never dismissed an employee. In its 65-year history, his company has never had a layoff. And he has never asked anyone to retire.
Quite the contrary. When Mary Boyt retired recently -- at the age of 89 -- Mr. Hartman was not at all happy about it. 
 "That got me ticked off," he said, shaking his head in disgust. "Her daughter pushed her into retirement. She was a great worker. I hope I'm as sharp at that age."
Mr. Hartman is president of the Vita Needle Company in Needham. And at Vita Needle, there is no such thing as mandatory retirement; even the suggestion is scoffed at. After all, the average age here is 73. Most of the 35 employees joined the small factory as a second career, after retiring from jobs as engineers, nurses, bakers or what have you.
Not that Mr. Hartman, who is 45, is just being nice. He says he recruits older people because he finds them loyal, responsible and eligible for Medicare -- eliminating the need for company-paid health coverage.
Rosa Finnegan, a retired waitress and a widow, took a job here a year ago because her Social Security check was not paying the taxes on her Needham home.
Like most of those who work and chat at the wood benches on the factory floor, Ms. Finnegan praises the flexible hours, the plant's location a few miles from her home and the opportunity just to keep busy. The work, assembling small metal components by hand, is less than exciting, but, she asked, "Who else is going to hire me at 86?"
Actually, in today's tight labor market, someone just might. The national unemployment rate was 4.6 percent in November, the lowest since 1970, and as it has fallen, employers have sought out workers in age brackets they might not otherwise have considered. In 1995, some 3.8 million people 65 and older had jobs; that is 2.9 percent of that age group, up from 2.5 percent a decade earlier, according to the Bureau of Labor Statistics.
When the American Association of Retired Persons Bulletin asked its readers to complete a questionnaire about their employment, 2,700 people working after age 65 wrote in, many of them former retirees. "We think it's a growing phenomenon as the labor market tightens and employers are finding it hard to fill jobs," said Robert Lewis, senior editor of the publication.
At Vita Needle, though, hiring older workers is nothing new. In 1934, Mr. Hartman's great-grandfather, Oscar E. Nutter, came out of retirement from the textile industry to start the business, which makes a variety of industrial and medical needles. He was 68, and he ran the company until three days before his death at 96. His nephew Carl Nutter worked until he was 88. And despite gall bladder surgery recently, Fred Hartman's father, Mason, at 72, the employee with the most seniority, was soon back at his desk keeping the books.
When Fred Hartman took over the family business 10 years ago, he saw that recruiting retirees would be in keeping with the company's experience with older workers. Mr. Hartman hired Bill Ferson, who was 68, as a design engineer. Now 79, Mr. Ferson is still here because, he said, "they treat us like human beings."
Lena Ferrara, who is 73, said Mr. Hartman called her not long after she left her job at an oil company and asked her to help out. Last week, she put in 40 hours. "He didn't give me a chance to retire," she said.
Having a staff of predominantly older workers has its ups and downs, Mr. Hartman acknowledged. But he is committed to them.
"They are motivated; they take care of the equipment; they don't have the P.T.A. meetings or the kids in day care," Mr. Hartman said. Most important, he said, "coming to work is a high priority" for them.
According to a 1993 report by the Commonwealth Fund in New York, most employers surveyed nationally said workers over 55 were better than younger workers when it came to work attitude, turnover and absenteeism. Another report by the fund found that of older Americans who did not work, one in seven was willing and able to do so.
"This work is kind of like therapy," Ms. Ferrara said. "Getting up early, getting dressed every day, not sitting around in your pajamas. It was too boring to be at home when no one's there. You clean your house for two days in a row, and then what?"
Almost all the older workers in Vita Needle's nonunion work force are part-time, some working as little as 15 hours a week. Two-thirds of them are women, with the workers' pay ranging from $6 to $12 an hour. The company promotes flex time, giving workers a lot of leeway in their choice of workdays and hours. Many employees have keys to the building, so some come in before daylight while others work into the evening; the last ones out shut off the lights.
"Older people are more likely to leave jobs that are physically demanding or have rigid schedules, and more likely to re-enter jobs with flexible schedules," said Diane Herz, an economist with the Bureau of Labor Statistics.
THE downside of such laissez-faire scheduling, Mr. Hartman said, is occasionally not having all the hands he needs when he needs them. "Summers and winters are harder on us," he said. "People want to go to Florida, or go down to Cape Cod with their grandchildren for a few weeks."
But his workers, he noted, do not need as much supervision. "People are letting themselves in at 5 in the morning," he said. "I'm not here telling them what to do."
A paper tacked on the bulletin board reads: "Remember, old folks are worth a fortune -- with silver in their hair, gold in their teeth, stones in their kidneys, lead in their feet and gas in their stomachs." And the physical limitations of aging do not go unnoticed. Jim Connolly, a design engineer, remembers one woman who would doze off at her workbench. And, he said, "you get the occasional oddball who makes you a little nervous."
Because the factory is housed in what was once a second-floor movie theater, "the stairs are a bone of contention," Mr. Hartman said. Although no previous experience in the field is necessary, applicants must be able to make it up the steps.
The aging building that houses the business has no loading dock -- it did not even have hot water until the late 80's. It is also cramped for space; the former theater stage is packed with yards of steel tubing. But moving to another site would mean losing employees; many walk or take a short ride to work. Last year, the company, which had been renting its space, bought the whole building. "We're in it for the long haul," Mr. Hartman said.
OLDER workers, of course, can be more resistant to something new. "Change isn't always easy when you have a labor force that is advanced in years," Mr. Hartman said. Mary Bianchi, 76, the office manager, threatened to quit when he replaced her typewriter with a word processor. There were arguments over the addition of the fax machine ("No one thought it would work"), the air-conditioner, even the microwave. "I still think a third of our people won't go next to it," Mr. Hartman said.
On the other hand, the company has remade itself in recent years. In the 1980's, the spread of AIDS caused a huge shift toward the use of disposable needles, making obsolete the re-usable medical needles that were Vita's specialty.
To reinvent itself, Vita found other uses for its type of product -- developing tubes for embalming, tagging salmon, vaccinating wild animals and injecting foam into car seats. One customer, Sea World, contracts with Vita for 4-foot-long needles used to medicate killer whales.
The workers, many of them widows, hardly blush when discussing the special needles for piercing navels, noses, tongues and, um, other body parts. "We're kind of a player in that market," Mr. Hartman said, with slight embarrassment.
By adapting to new manufacturing procedures, the staff has proved itself as versatile as the products. Mr. Ferson used his 30 years of experience in a machine shop to create a gauge that allows him to work with tubing the size of a human hair.
With its new customer base, the company has grown to 35 employees, from 15 in 1984. Sales have doubled in the last five years, and are expected to grow 15 to 20 percent this year.
More change is on the way. Although Ms. Bianchi, the office manager, keeps the company paperwork in files on a shelf behind her desk, computerization is in the works. As he helped her pull down a box, Mr. Hartman, 31 years her junior, joked about when she would retire.
"You're here until three figures, Mary, three figures," he said.
She smiled and said, "You'll have to put the elevator in for me."
