HEADLINE: Political Memo;
In Close Race, Rowland Has New Interest In Education

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ 

 BODY:
Gov. John G. Rowland of Connecticut was giving his annual end-of-the-year interview, but he didn't want to talk about the issue that has marked his three years in office: his drive to cut taxes and limit state spending.
What Mr. Rowland had on his mind that morning earlier this month was education, a topic that some might find odd because at the start of the year he wanted to cut Connecticut's higher-education budget by 10 percent. Mr. Rowland said he was now excited about "lifetime learning" -- a program of continuing education that he says responds to the fact that workers can no longer count on a job for life, but will need periodic retraining. 
 With 11 months until the gubernatorial election, Mr. Rowland, a Republican, has begun a metamorphosis from the man who was going to eliminate the income tax into the education Governor. And the change is motivated by a reality that seems to be haunting many of his most recent actions: Mr. Rowland is embroiled in the political fight of his life.
The Cook Political Report, a nonpartisan newsletter that handicaps elections, has given Mr. Rowland the unenviable distinction of being the only Republican Governor elected in 1994 whose re-election bid is considered a tossup. While the Quinnipiac College Poll has shown Mr. Rowland's neighbor and fellow Republican, Gov. George E. Pataki of New York, to have a lead of at least 25 percentage points over the leading potential Democratic challengers, the poll has Mr. Rowland in a virtual dead heat with his likely opponent, United States Representative Barbara B. Kennelly.
You would think that Mr. Rowland would have little to worry about. Connecticut has finally emerged from a recession, adding more than 50,000 jobs since he took office. The budget has run a surplus for six years in a row. Growth in state spending has been severely restricted, and Mr. Rowland can boast of enacting more than $1 billion in cuts to income, corporate, inheritance, gasoline and property taxes.
But while Mr. Pataki has scared away his strongest potential challengers, Mr. Rowland has admitted that his race is competitive, with a formidable opponent in the eight-term Congresswoman, a Democrat from one of Connecticut's most esteemed political families. Jennifer Duffy, a political analyst at The Cook Report, remarked, "Looking nationally at the Democrats' recruiting this year, getting Kennelly to run may have been their shining moment."
Mr. Rowland said that Connecticut remains a Democratic state, and he seems to be seeing Mrs. Kennelly's shadow wherever he turns. Earlier this month, the Governor appeared to be looking over his shoulder when he unceremoniously dropped his nominee to head the state police, William T. McGuire, because of allegations that Mr. McGuire had verbally harassed women officers. In the fall, Mr. Rowland abruptly curtailed his support for a $100 million football stadium for the University of Connecticut after Mrs. Kennelly criticized the plan. And Mr. Rowland, who went from abortion foe to supporter of abortion rights in the year before his first campaign for governor in 1990, has perhaps best demonstrated his political dexterity with the way he is dancing around the income tax issue.
Mr. Rowland was elected in 1994 on a pledge to eliminate the state income tax by 2000. He has cut the tax by less than one-third and it remains the single largest source of revenue for the state. In the interview, Mr. Rowland at first blamed Democrats in the Legislature for his failure to cut it more deeply, but then he played down its significance.
"The problem when I came into office was not the income tax," he explained. "It was jobs, and that's why I thought it was important to cut the corporation tax." While cutting the corporation tax took away money that could have meant deeper cuts to the income tax, he maintains that it was a smart decision.
"Reducing the income tax isn't going to do any good if you don't have any jobs," he said, adding that he was not sure whether eliminating the income tax would be a goal in the future.
"Christie Todd Whitman cut taxes 19 times and people didn't notice," Mr. Rowland said, referring to the lesson he learned from the New Jersey Governor's narrow election victory. "You know what the moral is? If you're an incumbent and the economy is bad, you're in trouble. And if you're an incumbent and the economy is good, then it's, 'What have you done for me lately?' "

GRAPHIC: Photo: Gov. John G. Rowland of Connecticut has switched issues.

HEADLINE: A Master Builder's Mixed Legacy;
Forgotten by the Public, 'Mr. Urban Renewal' Looks Back

BYLINE: By JIM YARDLEY 

 BODY:
Until early this year, Edward J. Logue served on a local task force grappling with traffic problems in Martha's Vineyard, Mass. There were many meetings peppered with many opinions, and finally, Mr. Logue simply quit.
"They were nice people, and they were doing the right thing, but they were taking too long," said Mr. Logue, who is 76. "The pace was not to my liking." 
 Patience was never Mr. Logue's strongest virtue. As an urban developer vested with enormous power and resources during the 1950's and 60's, Mr. Logue reshaped New Haven and Boston, and he did so in a hurry. He arrived in New York in 1968 to comparisons with Robert Moses, and though Mr. Logue's legacy over 17 years is decidedly uneven, his projects included Roosevelt Island, Rich Stadium in Buffalo and many housing developments.
But it is Charlotte Gardens, one of Mr. Logue's final and most modest projects, that is attracting the most attention today. Built atop a destroyed South Bronx neighborhood, the tract of 90 single-family homes was hailed this month by President Clinton as a national model of inner-city rebirth. Yet Mr. Logue was conspicuously absent during Mr. Clinton's visit. No one called him.
Once one of the most powerful figures in the Northeast, Mr. Logue is largely forgotten by the public. The man once called "Mr. Urban Renewal" now lives on the island of Martha's Vineyard, far away from any city.
"Still alive, right?" inquired Edward I. Koch, who as Mayor in 1978 picked Mr. Logue to rebuild the South Bronx.
Very much so, and still full of strong opinions. As much as anyone, Mr. Logue embodied the urban renewal era of massive Federal housing subsidies that begat massive construction projects. Even as many of those projects have come under criticism, Mr. Logue remains wistful for the time when he says construction of affordable housing was considered a national imperative.
"We're at a low ebb," Mr. Logue said from his small office behind his house. "We have no national housing policy that's worth a damn, in my view."
Louis Winnick, who oversaw urban development grants at the Ford Foundation for 26 years, said, "A Logue could not be in business today." Not only are the large Federal housing subsidies gone, Mr. Winnick said, but the administrative model under which he thrived -- heading a quasi-governmental agency with sweeping powers -- is now mostly abandoned.
"Power has been dispersed and atomized in a hundred directions," said Mr. Winnick, now vice chairman of the private Institute of Public Administration, which deals with urban affairs. "Today, it's nothing but a cat's cradle of regulation."
For more than 30 years, first as development administrator in New Haven in 1954, then in Boston and finally in New York, Mr. Logue held immense power, undertaking dozens of construction and rehabilitation projects financed largely through the Federal Urban Renewal Act. Trained as a lawyer, he read every line of the act, navigating its dense language to learn how to win grants.
In 1960, he took charge of the moribund Boston Redevelopment Authority, demanding and receiving planning powers usually reserved for the City Planning Commission. He also had the power to carry out his plans. He initiated construction in Boston of the 60-acre, $300 million Government Center, designed by I. M. Pei. The project was hailed by The New York Times in 1972 as "one of the most important groups of urban structures in the country." More controversial were his efforts to renovate and build affordable housing in two Boston neighborhoods, Roxbury and Charlestown, where hundreds of families were relocated after often-contentious public forums.
His critics lambasted him as an arrogant administrator who ignored the interests of people in existing neighborhoods -- accusations Mr. Logue still strongly denies.
"I thought he was very ruthless toward cities," said Jane Jacobs, the author of "The Death and Life of Great American Cities" and a longtime Logue critic. "That top-down control, that notion that you sort of wave your wand and do your transformation, and it's going to be wonderful. They don't see a community as a sort of living, changing thing."
But in 1968, Gov. Nelson A. Rockefeller recruited Mr. Logue to run the New York State Urban Development Corporation. Created in response to the riots that followed the assassination of the Rev. Dr. Martin Luther King Jr., the corporation enjoyed extraordinary powers, including the right to supersede local zoning laws. Mr. Logue was compared to Robert Moses, who had dominated public construction in the New York region for decades.
Mr. Logue said he first understood the depth of Moses's power when he attended a meeting about slum clearance in New York City. Each of the city commissioners, he recalled, sat in small classroom chairs like students.
"And Moses and his guys were sitting behind desks," Mr. Logue said. "That's when I noticed everything was approved."
Mr. Winnick said the comparisons with Moses were partly accurate, though he said Mr. Logue was careful to avoid the indifference that Moses often held for people in the path of his projects. "He had learned the lesson from Moses that you have to be sensitive about the poor and about blacks," Mr. Winnick said. "What he did inherit from Moses was the power urge. In order to do big things, he had to have big power."
Under the auspices of the Urban Development Corporation, Mr. Logue built or started at least 117 housing developments in 49 communities. Some of his projects were typical high-rise public housing complexes, like Jackie Robinson Houses in Brooklyn and Shorter Houses in Coney Island.
But most notably, construction on Roosevelt Island began under his watch. Plans to build on the island, formerly an asylum known as Welfare Island, had sat dormant for 20 years until Mr. Logue cut through the red tape. Designed by Philip Johnson, Roosevelt Island became a self-contained community with housing for people of all incomes.
"I was determined to prove I could get in the city of New York people of different income levels to live harmoniously and send their children to the same schools," Mr. Logue said.
Not all of his efforts at social engineering succeeded. His proposal to build low-income housing in Westchester County met with fierce local opposition, and the project failed. Mr. Logue's fall came in 1975, when the Urban Development Corporation went bankrupt. A freeze on Federal housing subsidies, coupled with banks calling in outstanding loans, left the agency overextended. When Hugh L. Carey won the governorship, Mr. Logue was finished.
 
President's Visit Begins His Comeback
His comeback came in the South Bronx. In 1977, President Jimmy Carter had visited Charlotte Street, pledging to rebuild the neighborhood. To do so, Mr. Koch and Herman Badillo, then Deputy Mayor, hired Mr. Logue in 1978 to lead the South Bronx Development Organization. Accustomed to autonomy, Mr. Logue found his powers vastly curtailed and the pot of Federal housing money almost empty.
"Logue came in very optimistic, and he soon found out the apathy and the heartache the local activist groups were feeling," said Genevieve Brooks, a co-founder of the Mid-Bronx Desperados, one of the early groups pushing for affordable housing in the South Bronx.
Initially, Mr. Badillo said the Carter Administration had promised $1.5 billion to rebuild the South Bronx. But as Mr. Logue began formulating an ambitious rebuilding plan, Mr. Badillo and Mr. Koch said, they met at Mr. Badillo's Riverdale home with a Carter aide, Jack Watson, to discuss the project.
"He said, 'I have some very bad news for you,' " Mr. Koch recalled of Mr. Watson. " 'The President cannot keep his commitment to you on the billion. He doesn't have it to give.' "
Eventually, after the City Board of Estimate rejected a proposal to build a low-income apartment building on Charlotte Street, Mr. Logue started over. He regularly drove up and down the cracked, empty streets of the South Bronx. One day he noticed something.
"I'd see this abandoned five-story walk-up, and the rubble-strewn streets, then I'd see a little three-story house in the middle of this," he recalled. "I saw that homeowner surviving in the worst of conditions. I said if home ownership works in the rest of America, it can work in the South Bronx."
At the time, the South Bronx was dominated by Stanley M. Friedman, the local Democratic leader, and Borough President Stanley Simon, both of whom would later go to prison for corruption. Wary, Mr. Logue said he sought alliances with grass-roots leaders like Ms. Brooks and the Rev. Louis Gigante. Working with them, and using seed money from the Local Initiatives Support Corporation, a nonprofit agency that finances affordable-housing projects, Mr. Logue announced Charlotte Gardens.
"They thought Roosevelt Island was crazy," Mr. Logue said. "I can assure you, they thought Charlotte Gardens was insane."
 
90 Houses Provide Model of Ownership
Accustomed to hiring the best architects, Mr. Logue ordered prefabricated houses from Pennsylvania to save money. On a cold night in 1983, the first two houses came by truck over the George Washington Bridge.
When those two houses went on the market at a subsidized price of $49,500, more than 500 people applied. The last of the 90 houses was completed in 1987.
Roberta Gratz, author of "The Living City," which includes a chapter on rebuilding efforts in the South Bronx, said that for all its positive publicity, Charlotte Gardens is actually a poor model for urban development because of its low density. She credited other grass-roots activists, like Father Gigante and the Banana Kelly Community Development Association, with triggering the construction boom in the South Bronx.
"He's a builder," Ms. Gratz said of Mr. Logue. "Builders are single focus. They're not nurturers of urban neighborhoods."
But the Bronx Borough President, Fernando Ferrer, an early supporter of Charlotte Gardens, said the project was never intended to be a model for later development. It simply proved that home ownership would work in the South Bronx, encouraging the development that followed, he said. Mr. Ferrer also credits Mr. Logue with discovering a new stream of Federal money -- a Federal Housing Authority subsidy previously used only in suburban projects.
"Logue was the architect of proving the model for home ownership," Mr. Ferrer said.
That Charlotte Gardens would eventually be regarded as a dramatic success was not yet evident when Mr. Logue left the South Bronx, partly in frustration, in 1985. His ambitious plans kept falling short, usually for lack of Federal money.
Since Mr. Logue is best known for large-scale projects like Roosevelt Island, it is no small irony that a modest project like Charlotte Gardens -- a "piece of whimsy," Mr. Winnick calls it -- may turn out to be how he is remembered in New York. But Mr. Logue said he was proud that he helped implant the idea of home ownership in the South Bronx.
Since suffering a minor stroke three years ago, Mr. Logue said, he has been in partial retirement. Nonetheless, he said he met recently with officials from a city looking for planning help. He is also culling through his voluminous files with the help of an assistant, preparing to write his autobiography.
He said he lives on Martha's Vineyard mostly as a concession to his wife, Margaret, a retired educator, but he admits he enjoys the tranquillity of island life.
He said he did not worry about his legacy, or how he would be remembered. He said he could return to any of his projects "and be cheerful and not be afraid to go where I've been.
"Down there in New York, do they think I'm up here crying in my beer?" Mr. Logue asked later, incredulous. "I'm having a good time. I have a wonderful family."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Edward J. Logue, a major force in the urban redevelopment of the 1950's and 60's, outside his home in Martha's Vineyard, Mass. INTERNATIONAL A3-10
 
Hong Kong to Kill Chickens
The Hong Kong government announced that starting today it would kill every chicken in the territory -- more than 1.2 million -- to combat a potentially deadly strain of flu. The virus, which scientists now believe is generally contracted from chickens carrying it, has caused four deaths and left at least eight people seriously ill. A1
 
U.S. Helps Mexican Army In War on Drug Traffickers
Hoping to build a new bulwark against the flow of illegal drugs from Latin America, the United States is providing the Mexican military with extensive covert intelligence support and training hundreds of its officers to help shape a network of anti-drug troops around the country, United States and Mexican officials said. A1 

Link Denied in Mexican Killings
A top official in Mexico's governing party strongly denied any link between the party itself and the leader of a local municipality who has been charged in the killing of 45 people in the southern State of Chiapas. At least 1,500 people have fled several small villages in the area, which they said were being terrorized by local paramilitary members. A10
 
Elections in Kenya
More than nine million Kenyans are expected to vote in one of the most watched elections in Kenya's history. Despite widespread dissatisfaction with the administration of President Daniel arap Moi, he is still favored to win because his 13 challengers remain deeply divided along ethnic lines. A1
 
Egypt Bans Genital Cutting
Egypt's highest court upheld a ban on the genital cutting of girls and women. The decision marks the culmination of years of debate between Government officials and some Islamic conservatives, who contend that the practice is a cultural issue and not a matter for the courts. A3
 
Death Caused by Air Turbulence
A United Airlines flight from Tokyo hit massive air turbulence over the Pacific, killing one woman and injuring dozens of passengers. The Boeing 747 returned to Narita International Airport, 40 miles east of Tokyo, about two hours later. All of the injured, except for nine passengers and a flight attendant, were treated and released. A8
 
Violence Threatens Ulster Talks
Participants in the Northern Ireland peace talks struggled to keep negotiations on track after an imprisoned Protestant and a Catholic man were killed over the weekend. Breakaway groups opposed to the talks took responsibility for the killings. A8
 
Algeria Expects More Attacks
Bracing for a surge in violence before the Islamic holy month of Ramadan, Algerian authorities launched a security crackdown. A3
 
NATIONAL A12-17
 
Clinton Pressing to Sign Up Poor Children for Medicaid
President Clinton will soon order a major effort to locate and sign up millions of children who are eligible for Medicaid but are not enrolled in the program, White House officials said. A1
 
No Deal in Unabom Case
Theodore J. Kaczynski recently offered to plead guilty to Unabom charges in exchange for avoiding the death penalty, a participant in high-level Justice Department proceedings said. The offer was rejected by Attorney General Janet Reno's death penalty review committee, the participant said. A1
 
Islamic Symbol Vandalized
Arabs condemned the desecration on the Ellipse of Washington's first official crescent and star, and the National Park Service police called the vandalism a hate crime. A12
 
Blood Supplies Questioned
Administrators at the National Naval Medical Center have ordered a halt to services at the hospital's blood bank after a report by the Food and Drug Administration questioned the reliability of some blood used for transfusions. The F.D.A. report uncovered clerical errors and cited 126 units of blood that were not properly screened for H.I.V., the virus that causes AIDS. A13
 
Dispute Over Pesticides
A year and a half after both houses of Congress unanimously passed comprehensive pesticide legislation, the Environmental Protection Agency faces growing disputes over how aggressively to enforce the law's extensive new safeguards for children's health. A16
 
Terry Sanford Has Cancer
Terry Sanford, the 80-year-old former Governor of North Carolina, United States Senator and president of Duke University, entered Duke University Medical Center in Durham, N.C., yesterday for tests after doctors said he had inoperable cancer of the esophagus and liver. (AP)
 
Storm Damage Set at $6 Million
Stormy weather, including a tornado, that tore through Central Florida early Saturday caused at least $6 million in damage but no deaths, officials said yesterday. Most residents of Haines City and Winter Haven were asleep when the tornado hit, at 4 A.M., destroying 25 homes and damaging perhaps 200 others. About 20 people were injured. (Reuters)
 
NEW YORK/REGION B1-6
 
Illegal Immigrants Face Loss of Some AIDS Help
Illegal immigrants with AIDS who live in New York City have long been able to obtain a range of government benefits usually beyond the reach of illegal residents, including Medicaid. Now, under the sweeping changes to Federal immigration and welfare laws enacted last year, they are about to lose their Medicaid benefits. Moreover, Federal authorities in New York who had once granted temporary legal status to some of those immigrants have begun the lengthy process that could lead to deportation. B1
 
Mixed Reviews for Crew
Schools Chancellor Rudy Crew relishes his authority to hire and supervise district superintendents, saying it will eventually transform the system by banishing politics and establishing a clear chain of command and accountability. But many educators say that Dr. Crew has been slow to use it to address the system's educational failings. B1
 
Police Rule Under Scrutiny
The fact that investigators have not yet interviewed the police officer accused of shooting an unarmed Brooklyn man to death last week has again focused public scrutiny on the little-understood piece of police procedure known as the 48-hour rule, which gives officers two business days before they must answer questions. B3
 
Church Consumed by Fire
A fire engulfed a church in St. Albans, in Queens, before dawn yesterday, destroying the wood-frame building in minutes and leaving six people homeless. B3
 
Barricades Are Ignored
Only a few police officers were at the pedestrian barricades on 49th and 50th Streets, and they seemed all too willing to watch as the new rules for easing traffic through midtown Manhattan were blatantly broken. B3
 
Snake Handler Bitten
A 24-year-old snake handler was bitten by one of the world's most poisonous vipers at a commercial reptile showplace on Long Island. B10

An Anxious Eye on Asia
The Asian economic crisis is about to move out of the "market phase" and into a more painful and challenging phase, many believe, one that will shift from the trading floor and the boardroom to the factory floor and the street. "We're one or two months away from what could be real trouble -- when the factories close, when the unemployment rates shoot up," said a senior American official. A17
 
The yen should not be allowed to weaken further against the dollar, and the Japanese Government is prepared "to intervene if necessary on a scale that will surprise the market," the Vice Finance Minister of Japan, Eisuke Sakakibara, said. A17
 
Long-Distance Competition
German telephone customers are about to have something they have never had before: a choice in long-distance service. And Germany is not alone. Until now, most countries in Europe simply outlawed most phone competition. But this week, the prohibitions disappear. D1
 
A Resistance to Change
Patricia Scott, the executive director of the foundation that operates the Pacifica Radio network, has been ruffling left-wing feathers as she overhauls an operation as proud of its contrarian nature as it is worried about its dwindling audience. D1

HEADLINE: The Soul of a New Economy

BYLINE: By JOHN MARKOFF 

DATELINE: PALO ALTO, Calif.

 BODY:
Overflowing the physical boundaries of four counties and the digital limits of three area codes, Silicon Valley is as much a state of mind as a location.
 The area's signature industry was born in a garage here in Palo Alto, where William Hewlett and David Packard built an oscillator for Walt Disney. But the term Silicon Valley was first coined in 1972 by Don Hoeffler, an electronics writer, in his own newsletter. In the generation since, those two words have become a metaphor for a unique engine of innovation that has helped transform the American economy. 
 To appreciate the magic associated with those five syllables, look at the litter of sound-alike clones: Silicon Alley in New York, Silicon Glen in Scotland, Silicon Desert in Arizona. The name of the metal used as the base for the microprocessors that were invented here has become a totem of success, touched with a spark of economic divinity.
 On its way to its current almost mythic status, the area that residents call simply the Valley has had many defining moments. One of these came at a 1982 meeting of the Silicon Valley Computer Club packed with several hundred engineers, many in white shirts with obligatory pocket protectors.
 The I.B.M. PC had only recently been introduced and the Valley was alive with an entrepreneurial fervor, inspired by a hobbyist culture that had become an industry overnight. In an auditorium in the town of Sunnyvale, a writer for The San Jose Mercury News asked the assembled engineers: How many of you plan to start your own companies?
 Two-thirds of those in the room raised their hands.
 The belief that it is only necessary to have a great idea or create a new technology to transform the world has long been a driving force for the engineers, hackers, marketeers and venture capitalists who make up the Valley's rank and file. From these were born corporations that went on to fame, like Apple and Oracle, and others that went bankrupt in a matter of months, like Trilogy and Go.
 Unlike the prospectors who were lured to the Sierra foothills by the promise of gold 125 years ago, these workers are not motivated only by greed. Instead, a passion for computing -- what the writer Ellen Ullman calls the desire to be close to the machine -- underlies the fascination that has spawned a work force legendary for its 90-hour workweeks.
 But beyond that psychic connection there are larger forces at work. Products can age and die here in a matter of months. This brutal pace has been governed by a dictum known as Moore's Law. The law, first depicted by the Intel Corporation's co-founder Gordon Moore, states that the number of transistors on a silicon chip -- and thus the chip's computing power -- will double every 18 months.
 That relentless rate of change has insured the Valley's fecundity: the digital watch, the desktop calculator, the video game, the personal computer and the Internet have taken off in quick succession.
 That, in turn, has meant new changes in a region that once grew 25 percent of the nation's fruit. As chip makers searched the globe for cheap labor during the 1970's, the Valley shifted from manufacturing toward research and development.
 Superheated by the new Internet economy in the past three years, the Valley is now in the midst of its steepest growth ever in everything from jobs to capital investments. Many employees cannot afford to live within its boundaries, and spend hours on clogged freeways trying to get to their work stations. Many others, with stock options to spare, are paying $1 million for converted three-bedroom cottages here.
 The question that remains: How long will it be before the engineers and programmers riding on Moore's Law and living at the hyperspeed of Internet Time burn out?

CORRECTION-DATE: January 1, 1998, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day on Monday about the symbolic status of Silicon Valley referred incorrectly to the origin of the term for the region. It first appeared in a headline with an article by Don C. Hoefler in Electronic News on Jan. 11, 1971; he did not coin it in 1972.

GRAPHIC: Map of Silicon Valley: "The Land of Chips and Money"
Here is a sampling of the history and highlights of life in Silicon Valley, a 1,500-square-mile region that embraces Santa Clara County and parts of Santa Cruz County in the south, as well as parts of San Mateo and Alameda Counties in the north. The area is home to 2.3 million people and 52,300 businesses, from bicycle shops to the headquarters of Intel, whose market value is now about $115 billion. (Kris Goodfellow and Dona Wong/The New York Times; illustration by Joe Shoulak)
 
Oracle
The offices of the data base company, which were built on San Francisco Bay landfill, are known as the Emerald City.
 
Pacific Athletic Club
The place to sweat.
 
Venture-Capital Center
With residents like Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, Sand Hill Road has become synonymous with venture capital.
 
Stanford University
Frederick Terman, Stanford's dean of engineering from 1945 to 1958, saw the university as an incubator for the new electronics industry. Stanford graduates and their ideas have been an integral part of the Valley ever since.
 
Xerox Palo Alto Research Center
At Xerox Parc, the pioneers of the modern personal computer developed basic concepts for products like the computer mouse, window-based displays and local area networks.
 
Hewlett and Packard's Garage
In 1939, the duo invented an oscillator for testing sound quality. The device was first used to produce sound for Walt Disney's "Fantasia."
 
Fairchild Semiconductor
In 1959, the micro-electronics era began with the creation of the integrated circuit.
 
Netscape
The Web browser start-up.
 
Rooster T. Feathers, formerly Andy Capp's
Soon after Atari's Pong was installed, the manager reported it broken. Actually, it was stuffed with quarters.
 
Apple Computer
The campus at One Infinite Loop was constructed to reflect the company's egalitarian values. Now it also houses a latte stand.
 
@Home
Fremont was the first city in the nation to the @Home service, a super-fast Internet connection.
 
Fry's Electronics
The place to buy comuters and all the accoutrements.
 
Eagle Computer Tragedy
Dennis R. Barnhard, the 40-year-old president of Eagle Computer, was killed in a crash as he drove in his Ferrari the day his company went public in 1983.
 
Cisco Systems
Cisco has rocketed to prominence by becoming the premier provider of Internet switches.
 
Intel
Known today for its ads featuring workers cruising around in futuristic suits, Intel invented the microprocessor chip in 1972.
 
Adobe Systems
Founded by two Xerox researchers, this developer of publishing software recently broke the Valley's tradition of low-slung corporate campuses by moving to an 18-story high-rise.
 
I.B.M.
At Big Blue's biggest Silicon Valley factory, the company developed the first computer hard disk drive. The maker of this hard disk drive named it the "Winchester," for the rifle.
 
Graphs: "Better Than Ever?"
With investment money pouring into Silicon Valley, the economy has rebounded from painful cutback in the military-contracting industry. Statistics compiled by Joint Venture, an industry-government research group. Graphs show employment, jobs, venture capital, real estate and quality of life figures for Silicon Valley. (Sources: Joint Venture's Index of Silicon Valley, 1997 report; California Departments of Employment Development and Transportation; Bureau of Labor Statistics; Price Waterhouse; National Association of Home Builders; Santa Clara County Pollution Prevention Program)

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Crude Oil Prices Fall Sharply As Iraqi Shipments Are Seen

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
Crude oil prices tumbled yesterday on the New York Mercantile Exchange on signs that Iraq would resume oil exports sooner than expected, contributing to a world supply glut.
On other markets, copper prices fell to a four-year low 
 The decline in crude oil came on expectations that the Secretary General of the United Nations, Kofi Annan, would approve a new Iraqi plan to distribute food and medicine bought with the proceeds of limited oil sales.
Iraq, which accounts for 1 percent of world oil supply, halted exports under a previous agreement with the United Nations on Dec. 5. Yesterday, United Nations representatives said that they had received a copy of the Iraqi plan and that Mr. Annan could approve it by the end of the week.
Oil from Iraq would arrive on world markets just as the 11-member Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, which accounts for a third of world supply, prepares to increase its official production target 10 percent, to 27.5 million barrels a day, beginning in January.
"The oversupply will probably get worse" because producers will pump more oil than consumers need at least through the second quarter, said Alan Struth, chief oil economist at the Phillips Petroleum Company in Bartlesville, Okla.
Crude oil for February delivery fell 58 cents, or 3 percent, to $17.62 a barrel -- the biggest one-day percentage decline since Nov. 20 and the lowest for a contract closest to expiration since Feb. 5, 1996. Crude oil is down 32 percent so far this year.
Copper fell 1.5 percent to a four-year low on concern that slumping economies in Asia would reduce demand for goods that contain copper.
"We're starting to feel the effects of South Korea's crisis," said Clive Ramsey, president of U.S. Bronze Powder in Flemington, N.J., a maker of copper-based powders for products that include explosives and hardware. "Our salespeople over there are telling us to expect a few months of slow business before things pick back up."
Copper for delivery in March fell 1.2 cents, to 77.7 cents a pound, on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, the lowest closing price since Dec. 9, 1993.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Moves Mostly Higher As Korea Worries Ease a Bit

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
The dollar rose against most currencies yesterday after United States stocks rallied on optimism that South Korea would avert financial collapse.
But the dollar weakened against the yen after a top Japanese foreign-exchange policy maker said Japan would not let its currency depreciate further, a signal that intervention by the central bank to defend the yen might be looming. 
 Traders said much of the dollar's strength was derived from a broad rally on Wall Street on news that major banks had agreed to reschedule debt owed by South Korean companies. The Dow Jones industrial average rose 113.10 points, or 1.5 percent, to 7,792.41. The lender relief for South Korea raised optimism that the nation, the world's 11th-largest economy, would surmount the crisis.
Gains in United States stocks tend to bolster the dollar because global investors buying financial assets need dollars to pay for them. Stocks had suffered in recent weeks amid speculation that economic slowdowns in Asia -- South Korea in particular -- could hurt the earnings of United States corporations.
"The dollar's move is very closely correlated with stocks," said Jonathan Coughtery, an analyst at Standard & Poor's MMS in London. "It's relief-buying this time because a Korean crisis has been avoided."
In late New York trading, the dollar cost 1.7893 marks, up from 1.7725 Friday. The dollar also ended higher against most other European currencies including the British pound, which weakened to $1.6705, from $1.6779.
The dollar cost 129.30 yen, down from 130.41. Traders said the dollar's decline against the yen reflected the impact of remarks on Sunday by Eisuke Sakakibara, the Vice Finance Minister for international affairs, who warned that the Government would "intervene if necessary on a scale that will surprise the market."
The remark was viewed as a strong hint the Bank of Japan might sell dollars to keep the yen from extending its 11 percent slide versus the dollar this year.
"Talk the Bank of Japan will intervene to support the yen is keeping the yen up," said Agostino Floro, a currency trader at Bank Austria in New York.

HEADLINE: Home Resales Off Last Month After 3 Rises to Record Levels

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 29

 BODY:
Home resales fell two-tenths of 1 percent in November, breaking a string of three months in which they rose to records.
Analysts said the decline was to have been expected. R. Layne Morrill, president of the National Association of Realtors, which reported the data today, said: "Up until November, we had three consecutive phenomenal months. It was a matter of time before we saw some downward trend, however slight. 
 "We expect a continued slowdown in the coming months, though nothing dramatic."
The November decline lowered home resales to an annual pace of 4.38 million. In October, sales increased a revised 1.9 percent, to an annual pace of 4.39 million units.
The figures measure the number of closings on homes, which lag behind contract signings by a few months.
Resales of existing houses make up about 85 percent of all single-family home sales in the country. Demand for houses, new and older, is an indicator of economic activity because the buyers also tend to purchase products like furniture, appliances and garden supplies.
In November, the association said, resales fell 1.6 percent in the Northeast and 2.7 percent in the Midwest. They rose 1.8 percent in the South and were unchanged in the West.
Those who think that the housing market has room to grow cite low interest rates and high employment. Mortgage rates have been falling since April. In November, the average rate on a 30-year fixed mortgage fell to 7.21 percent from 7.30 percent the month before, pushing down the average principal and interest payment on a $100,000 loan to $679.47 from $685.57.
The demand for Treasury bonds, notes and bills in response to the economic crisis in Asia has pushed down yields and mortgage rates. Last week, the average rate on a 30-year mortgage fell to 6.99 percent, the first time it had been below 7 percent since February 1996.
The Asian economic weakness is expected to slow the American economy by reducing the demand for exports, but so far job creation has remained strong, giving consumers encouraged by low rates the additional confidence needed to buy a house. The national unemployment rate fell to 4.6 percent in November, the lowest level since October 1973.

HEADLINE: French Jobless Seize Unemployment Offices to Press for $500 Bonus

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: PARIS, Dec. 29

 BODY:
In France, where being unemployed has become a full-time occupation for hundreds of thousands of people, jobless demonstrators occupied nine employment offices today to demand year-end bonuses of $500.
About 200 unemployed people also blocked trains at the main station in Marseilles, where unemployment is higher than the 12.5 percent national average, and 40 demonstrators occupied tracks on the main rail line between Paris and Bordeaux at Chatellerault for brief periods today, causing delays of several hours for thousands of travelers. 
 In Chatellerault, demonstrators had been thrown out of the local employment office after occupying it for several days in support of the demand for a year-end bonus. In Marseilles, demonstrators had been refused admittance to the main office of the regional government headquarters in the Mediterranean port.
Protests began there and in the economically bleak north of France last month after the Socialist Government elected in June said it would raise welfare payments by 2 percent this year for 500,000 people whose unemployment benefits had run out.
The increase -- which amounted to about $7.50 a month, bringing the payments to a little over $400 a month -- was described by some demonstrators as "an insult." The Government plans a 1 percent increase next July. Unemployment benefits in France decrease over time and expire, for most people, after two and a half years; the unemployment rate for the last two and a half years has hovered around 12 percent.
Employer groups and the conservative opposition say that high taxes and employer-paid benefits required by law discourage businesses from hiring. This, they say, explains why it is so much harder for people here to find jobs than it is in the United States, where the jobless rate is about a third of what it is in France.
The protesters chose the year-end bonus issue as a way of dramatizing the inability of all recent French governments, Socialist or conservative, to reduce unemployment significantly. Year-end bonuses have long been expected by salaried workers in France and Germany, and another month's pay is something most French workers lucky enough to have jobs take for granted.
The French Minister of Unemployment and Solidarity, Martine Aubry, has urged local officials to be generous in considering applications for additional aid to jobless people in financial distress. But the Government is trying to keep spending down to make sure France qualifies among the European countries that hope to start a common currency in 1999.
Christophe Aguiton, one of the founders of a group of unemployed workers called Act Together Against Unemployment, said, "We shouldn't forget that 80 percent of all the unemployed in France receive less than $500 a month in benefits."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
A Korean Company Town Suffers as Steel Mill Fails

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON 

DATELINE: TANGJIN, South Korea, Dec. 29

 BODY:
In a company town, the common assumption is that the company itself is as solid as bedrock.
In South Korea, company towns became so prevalent in recent years that they recalled a bygone era in the United States. Yet today, big companies are falling bankrupt so fast that the country is littered with towns like this one, devastated by the sudden loss of the local economic engine that provided an area with its identity as well as its livelihood. 
 In Tangjin, a typical Korean town of 40,000 residents, many are still in shock over the collapse of nearby Hanbo Steel, the nation's second-largest steelmaker.
It is an economic disaster, felt by almost everyone here. But on top of that, many residents said they are finding it hard to accept the notion that big companies as a way of life -- in South Korea, conglomerates are credited with fueling the nation's tremendous growth in the last 20 years -- may be a thing of the past.
"No one thinks big companies are infallible anymore," said Kim Jong Kuen, 29, a Hanbo employee wearing a coffee-colored baseball cap. "When I graduated from school, we all wanted to work at a big company. It was about image, a way to impress people."
Image may have been foremost in the minds of many young men who went to work at a big company -- known here by a special term, chaebol -- but they also settled into the high pay, rock-hard job security and professionalism that came along with it. No longer.
Though Hanbo is one of more than 15,000 company bankruptcies this year, and in many ways represents the quintessential chaebol story, it is also a special case. Some South Koreans consider Hanbo and its chairman responsible for triggering the whole financial disaster that has enveloped their country.
When a court began administering Hanbo's affairs, it gradually became clear that its chairman, Chung Tae Soo, had borrowed more than $6 billion, a hefty sum for a company with about $500 million in revenue this year. Investigators determined that Mr. Chung could never have secured the loans without Government help.
The more Hanbo's affairs were exposed, the worse they turned out to be. By June, Mr. Chung was jailed for having brazenly bribed Government officials, customarily hiding stacks of cash inside a crate of apples that his chauffeur would deliver.
On more than one occasion, prosecutors said, Mr. Chung invited an official to dinner at a restaurant and while the two men dined inside, the chauffeur placed the money-laden apple cart in the official's car.
In court receivership, Hanbo still labored to make steel, though construction was halted on the second half of its plant here. But in the autumn, when many Southeast Asian customers canceled orders for steel, Hanbo went from bad to worse.
By November, Hanbo had let go 8,000 workers from its 10,000-member staff, full-time and part-time. Most of the plant now stands idle, though the company continues with a skeleton staff.
The scandal involving Mr. Chung, which enveloped several legislators and the son of President Kim Young Sam, led lawmakers to investigate loan practices and bribery at other big companies, fueling a financial panic and drastically affected lending by banks.
By December, 45 companies were filing for bankruptcy each day. Mr. Chung was sentenced to 15 years in prison for bribery, and on Friday an appeals court upheld his conviction and sentence.
As financial troubles spread all over South Korea, people in Tangjin began to see that Hanbo's troubles were not unique.
"At first I thought it was all the chairman's fault," said Kim Young Shik, 22, a machinery repairman at Hanbo. "But then I started to see that this was how the chaebols did business. Chung was just trying to protect the company. The problem lies in the whole system."
Mr. Kim, who started work here last year in a group of 30 men, said 24 of them had left, disillusioned with the evaporating idea of a stable workplace, which had drawn them in the first place. Yet Mr. Kim, like many others who still work here, expressed a loyal determination to do whatever possible to try to keep Hanbo from closing completely.
"Yes, I feel loyal to Hanbo, even more so now that it is in so much trouble," he said.
Inside Hanbo's cavernous steel plant, eerily quiet at midmorning, a lone executive offered a reporter a seat in a bare conference room.
"I am sorry I can not offer you tea," said the executive, In-Don Hong. "But there is no one here to serve it. We dismissed all those people." Mr. Hong offered the best-case scenario, saying the company would try to produce two million tons of steel in 1998, down from its original projection of five million tons. But there was no way to pretend that things were the same.
"In the past, people came here for a lifetime job," Mr. Hong said. "That's different now. The idea of a lifetime job is gone."
Back in Tangjin, shopkeepers and restaurant owners and pool hall managers all complained that their businesses had suffered drastically.
"I'm the only one left here," said Park Jong Chan, whose restaurant was one of five lined up next to each other on a street, and now the only one open. "That's the only reason I have any business at all."
Beyond the Hanbo plant, Mr. Park pointed out, several other factories in Tangjin have closed down. "Hanbo was just the first," he said.
Though store owners are desperate for customers who still spend money, they, too, are prone to the defensive crouch that many Koreans seem to have adopted, saying that if everyone simply saves more and spends less, the country will emerge from its troubles.
"We have to be frugal," said Oh Hae Ryun, 39, who runs a cosmetics store at the center of town. "If everyone can conserve more and spend less, we'll get through this."
Patting her 11-year-old son's head, Ms. Oh said: "I'm telling my son not to work for a big company. There's no such thing as a lifetime job."

HEADLINE: Stocks Surge, Led by Banks; Dow Up 113

BYLINE: By SHARON R. KING 

 BODY:
Stocks in the United States were buoyed yesterday by the news that leading commercial and investment banks were cooperating to avert a short-term financial crisis in South Korea.
Expectations that their efforts will reduce the threat of default on billions of dollars of short-term loans made by American banks to South Korean institutions helped fuel a year-end rally. 
 The Dow Jones industrial average rose 113.10 points, or 1.47 percent, erasing last week's entire 77-point drop in a single session. The Dow closed at 7,792.41.
Banking shares including those of J. P. Morgan, Citicorp, Bankers Trust and Chase Manhattan were among the leading gainers. Shares of Merrill Lynch and Morgan Stanley, Dean Witter, Discover & Company also rose after they and three other investment banks said they would assist South Korea.
Despite the rally, some market analysts were wary that the agreement with the banks had changed South Korea's prospects.
"This happens all the time," said George Salem, a senior vice president and research analyst at Gerard Klauer Mattison in New York. "You get something that looks like a bailout and the market celebrates. But this is a short-term view. The South Korean market is very ill."
In moving bank stocks higher, "what the market is doing today is looking ahead 24 hours at most," he said. "They don't even know the details of the package."
In addition to bank stocks, technology shares rebounded yesterday. Shares of companies that do a lot of business in the region, like Microsoft, Cisco and Dell Computer -- which had been beaten down in recent weeks as each wave of financial distress from Southeast Asia swept American shores -- were higher, lifting the Nasdaq composite index 26.07 points, to 1,537.45, a 1.7 percent gain.
Standard & Poor's 500-company stock index rose 1.8 percent, to 953.35, a 16.89-point gain.
Korea's benchmark stock index rose 6.74 percent in the short session of Saturday trading in anticipation of a pact with banks. The Korean stock market is now closed until this Saturday.
While the news on South Korea heartened American investors, it may have only accelerated a usual year-end rise in stocks. This rally traditionally occurs during the last five days of the year and the first two days of January, said Yale Hirsch, the editor of the Stock Trader's Almanac.
Although the rally does not always occur, the Dow has risen in about 72 percent of the Decembers since 1901, producing an average 1.5 percent gain, according to Birinyi Associates. During the final week of December, the Dow has posted gains about 76 percent of the time as investors scoop up cheap stocks.
Because stocks tend to rally at the end of the year, some investors and analysts played down the impact of a South Korean banking pact. But if South Korea were to stumble deeper into financial crisis, stocks in the United States could tumble, said Charles Pradilla, chief investment strategist at Cowen & Company.
"If they hit the skids, it would have a very negative impact on stocks," he said.
Still, some portfolio managers and analysts remain skeptical of the quick fix.
The latest bailout package, "isn't changing anything about the way I think about things," said Barbara Marcin, a senior equity portfolio manager at Citibank Global Asset Management in New York. "I still think it's going to take a while before things reverse there," said Ms. Marcin, who attributed yesterday's rally to "year-end looking for cheap stocks."
Indeed, Ms. Marcin said she was looking at some technology shares like Applied Materials and health care stocks like the managed-care companies Wellpoint Health Networks and Oxford Health Plans for possible purchase.
And some international portfolio managers are not ready to begin a bargain-hunting spree in South Korea. "It looks cheap," said Stacey Ho, a portfolio manager with Wells Capital Management, but "valuation is never enough."
"The risk is very high. We are still avoiding Korea and Southeast Asia," she said.

HEADLINE: Looking for an Opposition in Japan

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Dec. 29

 BODY:
Japan is in disarray politically and economically, lurching through a financial crisis as its Prime Minister plumbs new depths of unpopularity. So where is the main opposition party in the world's second largest economic power?
Instead of rubbing its hands with glee, it is committing suicide. 
 The New Frontier Party, founded just three years ago, decided over the weekend to disband itself. Its leaders hope to resurrect it with a new name and membership, but the vacuum and chaos in Japan's opposition underscore the frailties of the political structure here.
Despite years of political upheavals and efforts to nurture a two-party system, Japan still has no credible political party to challenge the governing Liberal Democratic Party and keep it on its best behavior. The lack of a two-party system means not only that the opposition is weak, but that the Liberal Democratic Party, seemingly unconcerned with the opposition or with voters, can devote its energies to internal squabbles.
While politicians and scholars agree that the lack of a strong opposition is a fundamental weakness in the Japanese political system, some believe that the latest moves may be a prelude to a political realignment that will finally generate a credible opposition.
"I am trying to bring together several groups to form a new coalition of opposition forces," Naoto Kan, perhaps Japan's most popular politician, said by cellular telephone as he bustled about Tokyo today negotiating with other players in the changing political landscape. "If we can get together, we may be able to establish a new opposition."
Mr. Kan, the head of a small opposition group called the Democratic Party, is enormously popular because of a stint as Health Minister in which he took on the national bureaucracy and won. But his party has never attracted much backing. Now he is trying to recruit some of the New Frontier politicians as well as a couple of other small parties to create a liberal party that could emerge as the main opposition to the Liberal Democrats, or L.D.P., who despite their name are more akin to what in America would be known as conservative Republicans.
"This is another sign of a realignment," Ikuo Kabashima, a political scientist at Tokyo University, said of the New Frontier's breakup. "Whether it's going to be leading toward a strong opposition party against the L.D.P., we'll have to see."
The Liberal Democrats would seem to be vulnerable, if only a credible opposition party could emerge. A poll this month by the Nihon Keizai Shimbun, the leading economic newspaper, found that support for the Government of Prime Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto had dipped to 35 percent, its lowest level since Mr. Hashimoto took office two years ago. Disapproval rose to 45 percent, its highest level yet.
Moreover, at a time when many Japanese are profoundly alarmed by the nation's economic troubles, Mr. Hashimoto has been absorbed by efforts to maintain the peace within his party and the governing coalition. For many Japanese, it is a bit as if they were passengers in a plane with a couple of engine fires and what they hear over the loudspeaker is a fierce argument between the pilot and co-pilot about what to do next.
At this juncture, however, the passengers still seem unwilling to send in the opposition to take over the cockpit. The Nihon Keizai Shimbun poll found that New Frontier had only 5 percent support, less than even the Communists received.
Ichiro Ozawa, who was re-elected head of New Frontier just before he decided to disband it, argued that the party was too broad to be coherent. He hopes to create a new opposition party in early January, smaller but also shorn of factions that had led to internal bickering.
It remains to be seen whether Mr. Ozawa's new party will be smaller and more cohesive or simply smaller and less relevant. The ambiguities surrounding the party are underscored by the contradictory names that are being considered for it. One possible name is the Reform Party, another the Conservative Party.
While most New Frontier members of Parliament will probably join Mr. Ozawa's new party, others are forming other parties or joining other opposition groups. In an odd scene in an advanced democracy, opposition figures are scrambling to figure out what party they should belong to.
Yoshitake Masuhara, a New Frontier leader in Hiroshima, said he would spend New Year's Eve with eight other New Frontier figures from around the country to try to figure out what party to join.
"We've debated a bit, and we still don't know what party we'll enter," Mr. Masuhara said. "We don't even know what parties will appear, and so for now it seems best to take our time and study the situation closely."
Mr. Ozawa and the New Frontier Party attracted widespread sympathy during the last few years from American officials and business executives who saw Mr. Ozawa as Japan's best hope for opening up the economy and restructuring it along more market-friendly lines.
But most Japanese voters do not take Mr. Ozawa seriously as a reformer. They see him as a career operative in a corrupt and broken political machine and doubt that he really would dismantle it.
One of the central uncertainties is whether Mr. Ozawa's new party, whatever it is called, can capture public imagination any better than the New Frontier did.
"The problem hasn't been the name of the party, or even the people in it, but the leader," said John F. Neuffer, a political analyst for a research institute in Tokyo. "Ozawa's not a very popular guy."

HEADLINE: The Neediest Cases;
A More Satisfying Form of Giving

BYLINE: By ANDREW JACOBS 

 BODY:
Call it the tyranny of the holiday season: the tradition of gift-giving at Christmas and Hanukkah fills some people with more dread than joy. It can seem impossible to match each recipient with the perfect gift, and the resulting bills can inspire months of regret.
For many donors to The New York Times Neediest Cases Fund, charity proved far more satisfying than buying, receiving and returning gifts this holiday season. 
 Instead of accepting a Hanukkah present this year, Jackie Loeb asked her parents to send the money to the fund. "It just seemed like the right thing to do," said Jackie, who lives with her parents in Yorktown Heights, N.Y. Her mother, Pamela, mailed a $36 check with a note extolling Jackie's decision to forgo a gift this year. "Our daughter's choice brings us great joy," she wrote.
Fred and Celeste Burg of Queens gave up exchanging presents in 1974, when Mr. Burg decided that the ritual subverted the meaning of Christmas. "Why give something to someone who doesn't really need it when you can give to someone who does?" asked Mr. Burg, who sent $35 to the fund last week, along with a homemade card. ("I no longer spend money on pretentious cards," he added, "because they're a waste of money.")
Robert and Ilene Ginsberg of Stamford, Conn., stopped exchanging presents more than 35 years ago when the practice became a chore. "After you've been comfortable for so long, it seems frivolous to spend money on gifts that will just sit in the closet," Ms. Ginsberg said.
Charity has long since replaced consumerism for Adele Morgan Green, 77, who made her first donation to the fund as a 9-year-old. "I was quite an overstuffed child, and I didn't know anything about the poor," said Mrs. Green, who grew up on the Upper East Side of Manhattan but now lives in Sarasota, Fla.
Mrs. Green vividly recalls that first year, when she sat on her grandmother's knee and listened as "New York's 100 Neediest Cases" were read aloud from The New York Times. Afterward, she was told to choose a poor family to receive her $5, her total allowance that year. "It was an important lesson in philanthropy," she said. "I guess it's a tradition that stuck."
As usual, Mrs. Green sent the fund a donation this year -- $50 -- and decided to use the occasion to pass on the tradition to her four teen-age grandchildren. She wrote to them: "It occurred to me that the best gifts we could give to each of you would be for you to give gifts to some people who are not as blessed as we are in family, friends and resources. Your grandfather and I take great pleasure in sending a gift in your name to whichever charity you choose to remember this Christmas."
It was 86 years ago that Adolph S. Ochs, then the publisher of The Times, asked a reporter to delve into the files of the city's biggest charities and find the 100 most desperate cases, which were profiled the following Christmas in brief, unsentimentalized articles. That year, $3,630 was raised. So far this year, the fund has received $3.14 million, up from $2.43 million at the same time last year. It will be distributed to seven of New York City's oldest and largest charities. Because The Times pays all administrative costs for the fund, donations go directly to the needy.
With each passing holiday season, Arlene Albert has grown more distressed by the sight of brightly wrapped packages crowding the base of her family's Christmas tree. "I find it upsetting to see such excess," said Ms. Albert, 40, of Astoria, Queens. In years past, she spent more than $1,000 on presents for friends and the 10 members of her extended family.
This year, however, Ms. Albert's angst has intensified for different reasons. Since June, when the marketing company she worked for went out of business, she has been living on unemployment insurance. Her family insisted that she not buy them presents, but she wanted to do something to mark the holidays.
"I decided to make a point about materialism," she said. She contributed $120 to the fund and mailed notes to 12 people to whom she used to give presents, announcing that the donation had been made in their names. Her family and friends, however, may have missed the point.
"Of course, I got gifts from everybody," she said. "Next year, I'll have to write a pre-emptive note, asking people to please refrain from buying me anything."

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasuries Decline Modestly, With the Long Bond at 5.92%

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities slumped in thin trading yesterday, despite a housing report that showed a decline in the sales of existing homes. A rally in stocks and news of more expected help for South Korea weighed on bond prices as investors moved money from the Treasury market into equities.
The price of the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond declined 9/32 to 102 28/32 . The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 5.92 percent from 5.90 percent Friday. 
 This week is among the market's slowest, noted Ken Logan, a managing analyst with Technical Data in Boston, and yesterday's trading was about half the normal daily volume. "It was a type of session that many in the market anticipated and hoped for as they prepared to put another trading year in the books," he said. "When the new year hits, look for all market eyes to be on the fluid situation in Asia and all ears to be on Fed Chairman Greenspan's Jan. 3 speech to the American Economic Association in Chicago for the market's next directional move."
The National Association of Realtors said yesterday that sales of existing homes fell two-tenths of 1 percent in November, after three months of gains. Although the decline was expected, it suggested that the economy is slowing to a pace that could keep inflation in check and the Federal Reserve from increasing interest rates.
But the bond market was under other pressures yesterday, including the seasonal reality that market participants are not likely to take big positions going into the close of the quarter and the year-end period.
The shaky Asian markets, which have supplied the energy for gains in Treasury securities as investors sought safety, failed to provide much impetus for buying yesterday.
Robert Pugliese, senior vice president and director of the fixed income department at J. W. Charles Securities in Boca Raton, Fla., showed little concern about the decline. "If you had a wish list," he said, "a bond bull couldn't ask for a better scenario than this. When investors come back and reassess the big picture, they will find many compelling reasons to be optimistic about bonds."
Among some of the reasons, Mr. Pugliese cited talk in Washington of a prospective Federal budget surplus, increases in productivity, declining import prices, a strong dollar and stable-to-declining wholesale and consumer prices. "Simply put, an inflation outlook that couldn't look any more favorable," he remarked.
Analysts said the Treasury's weekly bill auction was about average, with the three-month and the six-month maturities selling at identical rates: 5.285 percent. The three-month bill was down from 5.295 percent last week, and the six-month bill down from 5.315 percent. Following are results of yesterday's Treasury auction of three-and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: Guilty Plea in $445,000 Welfare Fraud

BYLINE: By BENJAMIN WEISER 

 BODY:
For more than seven years, Jose Rodriguez used at least eight different identities -- some borrowed from dead relatives, others taken from living people without their knowledge, and still others created by him -- to obtain hundreds of thousands of dollars in Government benefits to which he was not entitled, court papers show.
But like many schemes, there were flaws, the authorities said. For example, when Mr. Rodriguez withdrew cash from automated teller machines linked to the accounts of some of those identities, the machines snapped photographs of a single person: himself. 
 Yesterday, Mr. Rodriguez pleaded guilty in Federal District Court in Manhattan to one count of stealing about $445,000 in the scheme, which investigators said they unraveled by tracking the A.T.M. withdrawals and other evidence, court papers show.
Mr. Rodriguez said in court that he had carried out the scheme using false names and fake social security numbers. Prosecutors accused him of setting up bank accounts under the different identities. The benefits were then deposited, sometimes electronically, into the various accounts, from which Mr. Rodriguez withdrew money, often with his A.T.M. card.
Through the scheme, Mr. Rodriguez was able to "finance a comfortable life style in Manhattan and Orlando, Florida," said Edward J. Kuriansky, Commissioner of the New York City Department of Investigation, whose office initiated the investigation last year that led to Mr. Rodriguez's arrest last month.
"Indeed, the defendant was so brazen in his fraud that he allegedly flew to New York on a monthly basis to redeem the food stamps he received under various aliases," Mr. Kuriansky said.
An investigator in Mr. Kuriansky's office said the department began the inquiry after receiving a tip from an informant.
The bulk of the stolen benefits, or about $354,000, came from the United States Social Security Administration's Supplemental Security Income Program for the disabled, said a Federal complaint in the case. Prosecutors also accused Mr. Rodriguez of stealing $64,000 from the Department of Agriculture's Food Stamp program and obtaining $27,000 in illegal rent subsidies under a program run by the New York City Housing Authority.
"I knew I was not entitled to the money, and I knew what I was doing was wrong and unlawful," Mr. Rodriguez, 49, said in court.
Mr. Rodriguez, who has a mustache and wore distinctive red-rimmed eyeglasses in court yesterday, has been unable to raise a $50,000 bond, and is being held pending his sentencing on March 30. The court documents did not indicate that Mr. Rodriguez had tried to disguise himself when he made the cash withdrawals. He spoke clearly in court as he told a Federal magistrate, Henry B. Pitman, that he was pleading guilty.
Magistrate Pitman told Mr. Rodriguez that he faced up to 10 years in prison, that he would be disqualified from receiving future Government benefits, and that restitution would be mandatory.
But it was unclear yesterday just what had happened to the $445,000 that the Government said Mr. Rodriguez had stolen, or whether he would ever be able to repay it.
In court, he was represented by a public defender, Steven M. Statsinger, who declined to comment on the case. An assistant United States attorney, Joseph V. De Marco, would not discuss the money's whereabouts, saying it was confidential grand jury information.
Mr. Rodriguez submitted a financial affidavit to the court describing himself as unemployed. He listed property and cash worth about $3,500, and said he had received several hundred dollars in the past year in Federal disability benefits, though he gave no details of any disability, nor was one evident in court.
Prosecutors asserted that Mr. Rodriguez was able to get the $64,000 in food stamps by obtaining the necessary photo identification cards from the city's Human Resources Administration under each of the eight identities that he used.
The complaint also asserts that in carrying out the scheme to obtain the Federal disability benefits, Mr. Rodriguez used medical documents signed by a physician in Manhattan; the doctor, however, later said the signatures were not his.

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
A Year of Awakening

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL 

 BODY:
By two journalistic tests, 1997 turned out to be one of the most awakening years in my lifetime of newspapering.
Did you report the full story on every important subject you wrote about? 
 No. Early this year I realized that in decades of reporting, writing or assigning stories on human rights, I rarely touched on one of the most important. Political human rights, legal, civil and press rights, emphatically often; but the right to worship where and how God or conscience leads, almost never.
In your belated awareness have you discovered any new and important developments that must be reported consistently?
Yes, two. One is the growing American public awareness of the persecution of Christians and desire to help the victims. They are persecuted in a half-dozen Muslim countries and China. Beijing adds a persecuting specialty -- the unrelenting drive to wipe out free Buddhism in occupied Tibet.
These Americans understand that we are dealing with persecution, not just bigotry or prejudice but persecution -- torture, incarceration, enslavement, abduction, confiscatory fines, mass deportation -- against congregations and clergy.
The crimes charged are to belong to any of the unapproved religions, as in some Muslim countries, or loyalty to the spiritual leader of the nation, as in Tibet. In China, crime is the refusal to attend only government-regulated and -approved "patriotic" Protestant or Catholic churches and risking arrest by worshiping at underground "house churches."
All this is not an incident now and then. It is persecution of scores of millions -- government policy enforced by police terrorism.
What is also new and important is this: The natural desire of American Christians to help persecuted Christians abroad is being mocked and bitterly fought in America. The opposition is not from some fringe anti-Christians but from some Americans of great power and standing, almost all Christian themselves. They include President Clinton, those who serve him to obey him, and the chiefs of America's major companies and trade associations.
At the moment, their particular target is a bill introduced in Congress by Representative Frank Wolf of Virginia and Senator Arlen Specter of Pennsylvania, both Republicans. The Freedom From Religious Persecution Act calls for penalties that are mild, but important as signifying American refusal to be a silent witness. They include ending "non-humanitarian" U.S. aid and loans to these countries.
Like the anti-persecution movement itself the bill is backed by members of both parties, and organizations normally far apart, like the A.F.L.-C.I.O. and the Christian Coalition.
The range of support is why the lobby against the bill is hysterically harsh, well financed and organized and could defeat it if Americans allow. No mystery about motives.
Mr. Clinton knows the full failure of his promise that he could enrich China economically but somehow sweet-talk Beijing into loosening its strangulation of human rights; a fraud from the beginning. U.S. businesses know that investment in China makes them servants of its war against human rights.
The Administration-business partnership has dredged up some classic pieces of hypocrisy. Most shameful is that opposing persecution would just bring more. Once that was said about the Nazis. Ask the prisoners.
And there is organized nastiness about the anti-persecution coalition's drawing attention to Christians. Why not? They happen to be the largest victim group. And focusing attention on specific victims is the heart of the struggle for human rights. That is what Clintonians do on issues like women's rights that might bring cost-free votes.
America's press has been disappointing. When it does in rare moments pay attention to the persecution, it often misses the point: the suffering. In a recent 5,600-word cover story in The New York Times Magazine, I am sorry to say, only a few hundred touched on that. The rest was how the coalition might split the Republican Party.
But, colleagues of the press, secure in the knowledge that my news judgment about religious human rights is correct at last, and yours still wrong, I wish you a happy new year, and an awakening one.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed
