HEADLINE: Dow Rises 123.56, on a 2d Vibrant Day

BYLINE: By DAVID BARBOZA 

 BODY:
The stock market rallied again yesterday, as investors went on an end-of-the-year buying spree in the hope that the economic crisis in Asia would not seriously disrupt United States corporate profits in the coming year.
After weeks of seesaw trading, jittery investors pushed prices higher for the second consecutive session. 
 The Dow Jones industrial average soared 123.56 points, or 1.59 percent, to close at 7,915.97, putting the blue-chip index up 22.76 percent to date in 1997. If the Dow holds, it will be the first time it has gained 20 percent or more for three years in succession.
Broader indexes were stronger yet. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index gained 17.49 points, or 1.83 percent, to close at 970.84, putting that stock barometer up 31 percent so far this year.
The Nasdaq composite index, filled with big-name technology issues, rose 1.79 percent, to 1,565.03, and the Russell 2000 index of smaller-company stocks climbed 1.72 percent.
Wall Street analysts said the rally had been propelled by late-year flows of cash into mutual funds, including hefty savings and bonuses, as well as some evidence of stability in South Korea after several leading banks moved to roll over loans there.
And then, the analysts added, there was a familiar end-of-year buoyancy, the period between Christmas and New Year's Day when the market typically rises.
"It is the time of seasonal strength," said Eric Miller, chief investment officer at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette in San Francisco. "One compilation says that 80 percent of the time, the week between Christmas and New Year's Day has been up."
Indeed, yesterday's big rally came a day after the Dow index had jumped 113 points. And so while the blue-chip indicator is well below an Aug. 6 high of 8,259.31, it remains far above where it was late in October, after a one-day decline of 554 points. The day after that plunge, which had been set in motion by financial developments in Asia, the Dow dipped as low as 6,971.32.
Yesterday's rally in the United States may have been buoyed, in part, by a report showing that consumer confidence in December reached the highest level in nearly three decades, a sign that the American economy remained healthy despite the turbulence in much of Asia.
Investors may also have been soothed by reports that influential commercial and investment banks decided to roll over more than $15 billion worth of loans to South Korea, staving off default.
"There's a sense of relief that the crisis in Asia has been put off by some of the rollover of loans in Korea," Mr. Miller said. "Whether it's correct or not, there's no immediate crisis in Korea."
That news helped send Asian and European stock markets higher early yesterday, adding strength and momentum in the United States.
 In Tokyo, the benchmark Nikkei index of 225 issues ended the trading year there with a surge of 3.27 percent, to 15,258.74, in a half-day session yesterday. The Hang Seng index in Hong Kong gained 2.4 percent, finishing 1997 at 10,755.21.
In New York, trading on the Big Board was robust, about 504.4 million shares, on the next-to-last market day of the year. Nearly three stocks rose for every issue that fell.
Among the strongest issues yesterday were technology stocks, many of which were badly hit by the crisis in Asia as semiconductor companies have huge exposure there.
Yesterday, though, technology issues outperformed the broader market. The Pacific Stock Exchange Technology Index gained nearly 2 percent. And shares of companies like Intel and Microsoft -- the twin towers of the Nasdaq composite -- were in heavy demand. Microsoft rose 3 15/16 , to 130 1/4, putting that stock up 58 percent to date this year, and strengthening its position as the second-largest-capitalization company in the world, after General Electric.
Intel rose 49/64 , to 71 11/16 . Dell Computer was also strong, up 2 3/16 , to 85 1/16 . Shares of the Internet browser company Yahoo gained 2 1/16 , to 71. For the year; Yahoo stock is up more than 500 percent.
Among Dow issues, 28 gained yesterday, led by United Technologies, which rose 2 13/16 , to 72 1/4, and the Walt Disney Company, which gained 2, to close at 99.
Shares of the Dayton Hudson Corporation, which operates retailers like Target and Mervyn's, rose about 10 percent yesterday after the company said that strong holiday sales would help bolster its profit.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Dow Surges 123.56 Points In Year-End Buying Spree
The Dow Jones industrial average goes into the last trading session of the year with a gain of 22.3 percent. If the gain holds today, it will be the first time that the Dow has gained 20 percent or more in three consecutive years. 
 A year-end buying spree, fueled by optimism that Asia's economic crisis would not cripple profits of American companies, powered yesterday's gains. The Dow rose 123.56 points, or 1.59 percent. Broader market indexes also surged. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index gained 17.49 points, or 1.83 percent, while the Nasdaq composite index rose 27.58 points, or 1.79 percent. Page D8.
 
Treasury prices fell in thin trading. A report on consumer confidence that was stronger than expected weighed on bond prices. The benchmark yield of the 30-year Treasury bond rose to 5.96 percent from 5.92 percent. D6.
 
True North Shareholders Clear Deal
True North shareholders approved the company's $440 million acquisition of Bozell, Jacobs, Kenyon & Eckhardt of New York. Publicis of France, True North's former partner, had tried to derail the deal, which will create the world's sixth-largest advertising company. Advertising. D6.

Underground Banks Boom in Korea
In South Korea, so-called underground banks -- unofficial lenders for people who lack the credit a regular bank requires -- have been busy as the country wallows in financial crisis. Since regular banks have essentially stopped lending, businesses and individuals have had little alternative. D3.
 
A Suitor for Three Mile Island
GPU said it was negotiating with a possible buyer of the Three Mile Island, shown at left, as well as the Oyster Creek nuclear power plants. The New Jersey company, which announced earlier this year that it wanted to sell the plants, did not identify the suitor. D5.
 
British Concern in Chemicals Deal
Harrisons & Crosfield of Britain took a big step toward becoming largely a specialty chemical company by buying Rheox from NL Industries of Houston for $465 million. D2.

Court Expedites Microsoft Hearing
A Federal appeals court agreed to give Microsoft a quick hearing on its appeal of a preliminary injunction restricting distribution of the company's browser program. D8.
 
China Clamps Down on the Internet
China adopted elaborate new rules restricting use of the Internet. The new rules apply to companies providing Internet access as well as individuals using it. A3.
 
Cloud Over France's Recovery
Effects from the Asian financial crisis and a shorter 35-hour workweek have led some to question how well France can sustain an economic recovery. D2.
 
Wheat and Hog Prices Slump
The prospect of abundant wheat and pork supplies pushed futures prices down sharply in trading on commodity exchanges in Chicago. The Commodity Research Bureau index, a barometer of trends in commodity prices, slid to its lowest level since July 13, 1995. D7.
 
Hong Kong Extends Chicken Ban
Hong Kong officials extended for at least several weeks a ban on chicken imports from mainland China, saying that mainland chickens were the probable source of the bird flu outbreak that left four people dead. The extension will have a great economic impact on Guangdong Province in southern China, which normally exports 75,000 chickens to Hong Kong every day. A3.

HEADLINE: Deft French Premier Unscathed by Jobless Rate

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: PARIS, Dec. 30

 BODY:
France's Socialist Prime Minister, Lionel Jospin, has President Jacques Chirac and his conservative opposition all tied up in knots six months after defeating them at the polls.
"The real President these days is not Chirac, but Jospin," a conservative member of Parliament fumed the other day. The legislator was commenting on Mr. Jospin's ability to pick his way unscathed through the minefield of budget cuts, soaring unemployment and European fiscal policy that blasted Mr. Chirac's protege Alain Juppe out of the Prime Minister's job in the legislative elections last spring. 
 Unemployment is still nearly as high as it was when the conservatives were in power, at 12.4 percent of the labor force. But by incessantly repeating that jobs were his first priority while quietly continuing deficit-cutting measures that had made his predecessor unpopular, Mr. Jospin has managed to keep his ratings high, to the grudging admiration of his opponents.
Diplomats here say the balance of power has clearly shifted from the President's office to the Prime Minister's.
"Jospin and his ministers are calling the shots here now in foreign policy, the economy, and everything else," a diplomat from an allied country said. "I have trouble persuading my people back home that they shouldn't just be talking to Chirac and his aides, but to Jospin and his ministers as well."
That is apparently true in Washington. Mr. Jospin's aides say the Prime Minister is eager to go to Washington in 1998 to get to know President Clinton and key lawmakers better, but has not received an invitation yet.
The Prime Minister, 60, may lack Mr. Chirac's suave gravity, and may not be as intellectually brilliant as his conservative critics say Mr. Juppe was. But Mr. Jospin outmaneuvered both conservative leaders in the elections last spring, called after Mr. Chirac dissolved Parliament in hopes that Mr. Juppe would get a fresh mandate from voters before they felt the bite from a new round of spending cuts needed to qualify France for a common European currency in 1999.
The election results sent Mr. Juppe scuttling back to Bordeaux, where he is Mayor, and made Mr. Jospin master of a sometimes unruly coalition of environmentalist, Socialist and Communist deputies in the National Assembly that has tested all his skill as a national leader.
His Finance Minister, Dominique Strauss-Kahn, helped by a recovery in the French economy, juggled figures with agility and came up with a budget that looks likely to qualify France for the common currency and put a little more money into the pockets of wage-earning taxpayers by shifting some of the costs of health insurance onto the rich.
Mr. Jospin has not had a totally free ride, particularly on the subject of his plan to introduce a 35-hour workweek after the turn of the century, an idea that many French business leaders and conservatives warn will choke off whatever hope there was of getting France out of its unemployment rut.
But after President Chirac warned against such "hazardous experiments," Mr. Jospin retorted that "the other head of the executive branch" had practically written the book on those, by calling elections that had backfired on him.
The riposte appealed to the French love of Voltairian wit, and it left opposition leaders spluttering.
The head of Mr. Chirac's neo-Gaullist Rally for the Republic party, Philippe Seguin, recently protested that having a Prime Minister from one party under a President from another one was "a detestable system." But the Constitution that makes it possible was devised for Charles de Gaulle himself, and the Gaullists are stuck with it for the time being.
Mr. Jospin's own ranks grew restive over changes he proposed to French asylum and nationality laws to fulfill a campaign promise demanded by his Communist allies to roll back some of the most Draconian anti-immigration measures that had been passed by the conservatives after the Iron Curtain came down.
In more than 100 hours of debate earlier this month, the National Assembly adopted changes in the law restoring the automatic right of French citizenship at age 18 to any child born on French soil, whether of French or foreign parents, and eliminating bureaucratic requirements that discouraged immigrants already here from bringing in members of their extended families.
But the conservative opposition, fearing voter backlash against foreign immigrants that has produced a surge in support for the far-right National Front party at Gaullist expense, blocked the citizenship law in the upper house of Parliament, forcing a new National Assembly debate in January.
During a trip to Africa this month, Mr. Jospin added fuel to the fire by telling an audience in Mali that France would stop chartering planes to return illegal immigrants to Mali against their will.
"He has revealed his true colors," the conservative former Interior Minister Jean-Louis Debre asserted. "Mr. Jospin's Government and the Socialists have no intention of fighting against illegal immigration."
Mr. Jospin said: "We ask our Malian friends not to try to come and settle in our country illegally. The state of our economy and the high level of unemployment do not allow us to accept them."

HEADLINE: Gilbert Fitzhugh, 88, Dies; Ex-Metropolitan Life Chief

BYLINE: By CONSTANCE L. HAYS 

 BODY:
Gilbert W. Fitzhugh, a former chairman and chief executive of the Metropolitan Life Insurance Company, died on Monday at the Meadow Lakes Health Care Center in Hightstown, N.J. He was 88 years old and had been ailing for several years with Alzheimer's disease, said his son, Gilbert Van Ingh Fitzhugh.
Mr. Fitzhugh retired in 1973 after 10 years at the helm of Met Life. While insurance was his first love -- he even met his wife, who had joined the company's London office, when her desk wound up near his at Met Life's Manhattan headquarters -- Mr. Fitzhugh also became an agent of political change. At a White House news conference in 1967, he announced the formation of a $1 billion fund in which insurance companies would invest in rehabilitating rundown urban housing. 
 That fund, which has since grown to $2 billion, was hailed by President Lyndon B. Johnson as "a historic contribution to our country," at a time when other urban-renewal programs pushed by the Administration were encountering fierce opposition from Congress.
While Mr. Fitzhugh in a speech a few months earlier had obliquely criticized President Johnson for what he called the "new economics," which entailed increased Government involvement in American business, he had also been grimly impressed with conditions in a Brooklyn neighborhood he toured with Mayor John V. Lindsay of New York.
Mr. Fitzhugh also led a Government-appointed panel from 1971 to 1972 that assessed the Pentagon's procurement practices and called for change in the way the Government girded itself for war. "We're amazed that it works at all," he said in announcing his findings, which some hailed for exposing some of the system's worst flaws. The panel's report was attacked by critics like Senator William Proxmire, a Wisconsin Democrat, for taking too kindly an attitude toward military contractors' profits and the ties between industry and the Pentagon.
In 1972, as head of the New York Chamber of Commerce, Mr. Fitzhugh proposed a merger of the Colonial-era group with the more recently arrived Industry Association of America. The effort had fallen flat eight years earlier, but the chamber voted overwhelmingly in favor of the merger the second time around.
While he headed Met Life, the company added automobile and home insurance to its policy offerings, becoming the third company to do so.
Born in Brooklyn, Mr. Fitzhugh spent the first 12 years of his life with a cleft palate that badly garbled his speech, his son said. The problem was eventually corrected with surgery, but by that time Mr. Fitzhugh had developed formidable powers of concentration that, at age 23, made him the youngest person to pass all the examinations required to become a fellow in the Society of Actuaries. The society, though, had a rule at the time that no one under 25 could be admitted, so Mr. Fitzhugh, to his great annoyance, had to wait until his 25th birthday for his membership to become official. He later became president of the society.
Although he was a quick study when it came to actuarial concepts, starting off as a clerk in Met Life's social insurance division shortly after he graduated from Princeton University in 1930, he often struggled with simple arithmetic. In a 1970 interview, he said, "I can add up a row of figures four times and get four different answers." After 33 years with Met Life, including a brief tour in Canada, he was named chief executive in 1963.
While he spent much of his career calculating risk for others, he scaled mountain peaks like Mount Marcy in the Adirondacks and the 14,000-foot-high Mount Whitney in California for recreation.
In addition to his son, Gilbert of Morristown, N.J., Mr. Fitzhugh is survived by his wife, the former Lea Van Ingh of Hightstown; a daughter, Lea Fitzhugh Welch of Yakima, Wash.; a sister, Sarah Fitzhugh Dawson; two grandchildren; three step-grandchildren; two great-grandchildren, and one step-great-grandchild.

HEADLINE: China Cracks Down on Dissent in Cyberspace

BYLINE: By ERIK ECKHOLM 

DATELINE: BEIJING, Dec. 30

 BODY:
China adopted elaborate new rules today restricting use of the Internet, a medium that is fast fraying the Government's monopoly on information.
The new rules spell out in more detail than before the Government's definition of computer crimes, which include use of the Internet to defame Government agencies, to promote separatist movements or to divulge state secrets. 
 Officials cited a need to "to safeguard national security and social stability" as they annouced the rules, which are clearly aimed at squelching the rapidly growing use of electronic mail and Web sites by dissidents to spread their message.
Announcing the new regulation, Zhu Entao, an Assistant Minister of Public Security, said computer information networks are indispensable to the country's economic and scientific advance.
"But the connection has also brought about some security problems, including manufacturing and publicizing harmful information, as well as leaking state secrets through the Internet," Mr. Zhu said, according to the official New China News Agency. Mr. Zhu spoke on Monday at a news conference to which foreign journalists were not invited.
In 1996 the Government declared that laws against pornography, social disturbances or breaches of state security would apply to the Internet, and the practical impact of the new regulation will depend on how it is enforced.
But the announcement is a warning to dissenters and their sympathizers here and abroad.
Earlier this month, for example, after he was released from prison and sent to the United States, the democracy advocate Wei Jingsheng said he planned to use the Internet and other forms of telecommunications to press his cause from abroad.
 The new rules will not affect Hong Kong, Reuters reported, quoting Anthony Wong, director general of the territory's telecommunications. Mr. Wong said the Internet was covered by the "one country, two systems" doctrine adopted when Britain ceded Hong Kong in July.
Use of the Internet to retrieve information and send E-mail has soared since China first allowed global connections in 1994. At the end of October, 620,000 Internet subscriptions had been established, the Internet Information Center of China, an arm of the state-run company that oversees Internet services, reports.
Many of those accounts are shared by 10 or 20 people, though a growing number of people have computers at home and may pay more than $200 to establish a personal account. Though the connecting lines are often crowded and slow, users can still tap the bountiful resources of the global Internet.
The Cabinet approved the new rules on Dec. 11. The Government did not publish the text, but Reuters reported that among other things it prohibits using the Internet to "split the country" -- Chinese terminology applied to supporters of the Dalai Lama or of formal independence for Taiwan. Another article bans "defaming of Government agencies," which could apply to statements by democracy advocates.
The regulation calls for unspecified "criminal punishments" and fines of up to $1,800, Reuters reported, and applies to companies providing Internet access as well as to individuals using it.
In 1996, inveighing against the "spiritual pollution" of China, the Government used its control over the telecommunications system to block access to more than a hundred Web sites, including those of many foreign newspapers, human rights and dissident groups and Taiwanese agencies, as well as sites considered pornographic, like Playboy magazine's. In early 1997, it reopened access to some sites.
This evening, access through Chinese Internet providers to some newspapers, including The New York Times, and to the Web pages of well-known dissident groups abroad was apparently blocked. A user trying to sign on received no response.
But the pages of many other foreign news sources, including The Washington Post, were accessible, as were many X-rated sites reached via a common Web browser.
Controlling Web sites is a Sisyphean task because so many new ones are constantly being created, and people with skill and determination can find ways to bypass the Government barriers. Though a monitor may be able to identify messages by their labels -- for example, www.amnesty.com -- seditious information can be disguised.
Still, one blocked site, that of the group Human Rights in China, based in New York, still gets dozens of "hits" from inside China each week, said the executive director, Xiao Qiang. Another banned site from the United States, China News Digest, gets hundreds each week, he said.
E-mail is the Government's real Achilles' heel. Security agents can tap phones and monitor the contents of some among the blizzard of letters, but there is no way to censor them. More likely, a monitoring program could be used to gather evidence against addressees should the Government wish to suppress their access to information.
The scattered, usually silent remnant of democracy advocates here has found ways, together with exiles, to exploit electronic mail.
It is not so useful, they say, for sending sensitive messages directly to one person in China, because the line might be tapped and because so many accounts have numerous readers. "But it's great for the mass distribution of information," Mr. Xiao said by telephone from New York, noting that news or articles can be sent to dozens or hundreds of addresses to be widely read, with no one clearly responsible.
He pointed to an electronic magazine called "Tunnel," a weekly forum for free political discussion that is mainly written and edited inside China. The contents are sent by E-mail to a Silicon Valley address where it is electronically mailed back into China to thousands of addresses. Some 20 issues have appeared since it began in June.
China's struggle to tame the squirming Internet octopus reflects what many experts see as the Government's central conundrum: how to foster economic growth and freedom while keeping tight screws on politics.
Mr. Zhu, the security official who announced the new rules, gave no nod to the possible trade-offs. "The safe and effective management of computer information networks," he said, "is a prerequisite for the smooth implementation of the country's modernization drive."

SECTION: Section A; Page 14; Column 1; Editorial Desk

HEADLINE: Temptations of a Balanced Budget

 BODY:
By some estimates, the Federal budget is headed for surplus for the first time in many years. The surplus, if it occurs, is likely to be temporary because Medicare costs will begin to soar within a decade. Even so, the prospect of a more or less balanced budget has inspired a lot of politicians to start waving tax cuts in front of the voters. Premature cuts can only hurt the poor. When the economy slows, Congress is far more likely to cut the housing, day care and training on which the poor rely before they rescind their ill-advised tax cuts.
Even President Clinton cannot resist the urge to cut. Mr. Clinton is expected to propose modest tax credits, perhaps costing $4 billion a year, for child care and education expenses. Because tax credits do not help people too poor to owe much tax, his plan will probably not help families most in need. The White House no doubt regrets this, but it is trapped between its desire to avoid unaffordable cuts and its need to neutralize the expected Republican effort to reduce taxes for the rich and the middle class. 
 Individual Republicans have proposed many types of tax cuts. But the one idea that has captivated much of the party is eliminating the so-called marriage tax, a feature of the tax code that can impose higher taxes on two individuals after they marry. The G.O.P. plan would cost a daunting $20 billion a year.
Eliminating a tax on marriage sounds like a great idea. But it is also a sound idea to set higher rates on wealthier people and to tax families with the same total income the same no matter how their income is split between spouses. No tax code can satisfy these three goals simultaneously. Two people whose individual incomes are low enough to be taxed at 15 percent can, under a progressive code, hit the 28 percent bracket when their incomes are combined. Congress can eliminate the marriage tax, but only by sacrificing progressivity.
The current code produces a more complicated array of winners and losers than the politicians let on. Indeed, many couples owe less tax after they marry. The sobering fact is that 90 percent of the savings from eliminating the marriage tax will go to families earning at least $50,000 a year.
With future deficits looming, income inequality soaring and a year after Congress handed wealthy families a hefty cut in capital gains and tax cuts, this is no time for Congress to tilt the Federal budget toward them.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Big Lenders Press Seoul to Repackage $15 Billion of Bad Debt

BYLINE: By TIMOTHY L. O'BRIEN with SETH FAISON 

 BODY:
Several of the world's largest banks, trying to advance their effort to avert a default by South Korea, are circulating a proposal to convert at least $15 billion of the country's bad debt into new debt backed by the country's Government, but Seoul at least for the moment is resisting that idea.
Also under consideration, but as yet not part of the bank proposal, is the creation of a South Korean Government agency that would oversee the repackaged debt. Bankers said the agency would be similar to the Resolution Trust Corporation, which was created in the United States to manage savings and loan institutions that failed in the 1980's and early 1990's. 
 A draft of the proposal, written by J. P. Morgan & Company and first presented at a meeting at the American bank's New York headquarters on Monday, was shared with the South Korean Government sometime in the last day, the bankers said.
But a senior South Korean finance official said yesterday in Seoul that his country would not automatically agree to foreign bank demands that the Government guarantee the new debt without first considering the matter independently.
The official, Chung Duk Koo, the Deputy Finance Minister, said he was encouraged by a decision by international lenders on Monday to give South Korean borrowers a one-month reprieve on $10 billion to $15 billion of troubled loans that were due today.
"We cannot make any blanket guarantee," Mr. Chung said in an interview. "Many alternatives are still open. We could use some kind of central bank guarantee, or Government bonds. It is under consideration and has not yet been decided."
Nonetheless, the South Korean Government has little room to maneuver because its economy has been hurt so badly.
Although the Korean stock market is closed until Saturday for a year-end holiday, the nation's currency, the won, tumbled 12 percent against the dollar yesterday and fell another 5.9 percent by midday today. The decline yesterday snapped a three-day gain and partly reflected a year-end effort by South Korean companies to buy dollars to settle foreign accounts. But the won's decline also was an illustration of persistent skepticism about the country's prospects.
If the bad debt is not replaced, banks would be forced to extend, or roll over, the debt again at the end of January -- a process that cannot go on indefinitely because it will only cause South Korea's economic crisis to escalate.
Moreover, the $10 billion to $15 billion in bad debt rolled over is only part of the problem. South Korea has about $40 billion in short-term debt that will become due at the end of March that also must be repackaged in some fashion.
South Korea said in August that it would back up the country's banks if they defaulted but stopped short of issuing a more formal guarantee to the struggling nation's creditors.
"The rollovers are obviously a short-term measure for the next 30 days," one banker said. "Something has to follow the rollovers."
The proposal presented to the South Korean Government would involve replacing a portion of the short-term bad debt with at least $15 billion worth of longer-term bonds issued by the the Government.
The bonds would have to pay a higher interest rate than the Government and banks are currently paying and might carry rates of about 10 to 11 percent.
The bonds would have three ranges of maturities: one year, 3 or 5 years, and 10 years.
The interest rates might not be too much for the South Korean Government to bear. Through most of 1997, South Korea paid about 5 percent, or $7 billion, to finance its external debt. That represented about 4 percent of what the country earned on exports.
If the interest rate paid to finance the debt doubled, the rate would only represent about 7.5 percent of 1997's year's export earnings. And South Korea's export earnings could rise sharply next year because of the sharp depreciation of the won.
The won's decline already appears to be helping exports.
The Bank of Korea announced yesterday that South Korea had a current account surplus of $540 million in November -- its first in four years -- compared with a $710 million deficit in October. The current account is a measure of the balance between a nation's imports and exports of goods and services.
The main goal of the revamping is to convert short-term loans that require immediate payment into long-term bonds that can be repaid more slowly and thus ease South Korea's shortage of money.
The rolled-over loans consist primarily of private bank debt and exclude other types of corporate debt. For the time being, international lenders are apparently willing to let some South Korean corporate borrowers -- other than banks -- default on their loans.
Still, the bulk of South Korea's $81 billion in short-term debt is held by private banks. Banks have $55 billion in short-term debt, while other companies have $26 billion in short-term debt, according to the International Monetary Fund.
None of the short-term debt is held by the Government. Of the country's approximately $73 billion in long-term debt, the I.M.F. said, $44 billion is held by banks, $18 billion by other companies and $9 billion by the Government.
Private banks in South Korea have been singled out for special attention in the rollovers because they are linchpins in the local economy.
The credit situation is so serious in South Korea that an average of 45 companies a day declared bankruptcy in December.
The Bank of Korea urged banks to lend money to companies trying to make bond payments, but in trying to meet I.M.F. lending standards, many banks have been unable to make any loans. Since late summer, about $20 billion in capital has left the country, according to American bankers.
One I.M.F. official, who insisted on anonymity, noted that the international banks' proposal to revamp the South Korean debt is just "one of many suggestions floating around" and that the financial community "wants to find ways of addressing South Korea's liquidity problem."
J. P. Morgan, Citicorp and the Chase Manhattan Corporation are leading American bank efforts to restructure the bad loans. Other international giants like the Bank of Tokyo-Mitsubishi Ltd., Deutsche Bank A.G., and Societe Generale are also involved.
While some new lending may accompany the revamping at some point, American commercial bankers disputed reports yesterday that $10 billion in fresh bank financing was being arranged for South Korea.
It had previously been reported that the South Korean Government might try to issue $9 billion in new bonds -- an offering that would apparently have to wait until all the short-term loans were cleaned up.
Meanwhile, the I.M.F. released $2 billion to South Korea yesterday, part of $10 billion in accelerated aid promised to the country by the agency and other publicly financed institutions.
Another $8 billion in backup financing was expected to be disbursed to South Korea by 13 countries next week, depending on how the rollovers proceed.
 But one underlying problem for South Korea is that the won's decline makes it harder for South Korean banks to meet the minimum ratio of capital to outstanding loans that the I.M.F. insisted upon when it agreed earlier this month to provide $57 billion in emergency assistance.
"We will demonstrate our creditworthiness," Mr. Chung asserted. "We will do something every day. We will expedite the I.M.F. package. We are open and transparent. That's the only way to demonstrate our will and our attitude to the international community."

Halla May Sell to Bowater
 SEOUL, South Korea, Wednesday, Dec. 31 (Dow Jones) -- South Korea's troubled Halla Group said today it is in talks with Bowater Inc. of the United States to sell a piece of Halla Pulp & Paper Company, part of the parent company's effort to survive its financial crisis.
Halla said through a spokesman that nothing had been decided and he denied local press reports that the country's 12th largest conglomerate plans to sell the entire paper business to Bowater, a leading American forest-products company.
Halla Group, which has major business interests in heavy industries, cement and car parts, was declared bankrupt in early December after failing to honor its debts.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 6; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1249 words

HEADLINE: CALIFORNIA'S BAN TO CLEAR SMOKE INSIDE MOST BARS

BYLINE: By DON TERRY 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Dec. 30

 BODY:
In the biggest assault yet on cigarette smoking in the United States, bars, casinos and nightclubs across California will be forced to ban smoking at the stroke of midnight on Jan. 1, as part of a virtual ban on indoor smoking in public places.
The policy is part of a 1994 labor law in the nation's most populous state, meant to protect the health of employees like bartenders, waitresses and bellhops from the risks of secondhand smoke. It extends the state's no-smoking policy to bars and clubs from mountain ski resorts to beach-side cafes and every watering hole in between. 
 The law's critics and smoking advocates say the ban is Draconian and unenforceable and that bars are unlikely to keep patrons from smoking. They also say it will be financially disastrous for bars and nightclubs.
But anti-tobacco forces say the fines included in the law will insure it will be enforced and experts say that it signals a new battleground in the war over smoking.
"I think this bar ban going into force is the most important thing happening in tobacco in the country today," said Stanton A. Glantz, a professor of medicine at the University of California at San Francisco. "When this happens in the biggest, most diverse state in the country and the sky doesn't fall, it will spread across the country."
California is the first state to so thoroughly restrict smoking.
Since 1995, there has been a ban on smoking in restaurants without bars and in manufacturing plants, offices and other enclosed work places. An exemption was granted to bars and nightclubs in the hope that sophisticated ventilation systems could be developed to reduce the health risks of secondhand smoke.
But California official officials say that the technology has not been developed and that there is no acceptable level of secondhand smoke indoors. So with the coming of the New Year, the smoke-filled bar -- where the broken-hearted go to cry and the company softball team gathers to celebrate or forget the latest game -- will become illegal.
"What you've got in California again is Prohibition," said Thomas Humber, president of the National Smokers Alliance, a pro-smoking group in Alexandria, Va., that receives some of its money from the tobacco industry.
Mr. Humber said there was already a campaign to get the California Legislature to repeal the ban next year. "Believe me," he said, "we'll be as active as any group can be to urge repeal."
Mr. Humber said that a few communities in California had enacted similar complete smoking bans but that he was not aware of such bans in other states.
Assemblyman Brett Granlund, a Republican who opposes the ban, said repealing the law would be very difficult. "I'm afraid it's going to be the law of the land," he said.
Mr. Granlund, who is trying to quit a two-pack-a-day smoking habit, said the law clearly infringed on the rights of business owners to operate the way they see fit. "Smokers on airplanes and in restaurants have voluntarily complied with smoking restrictions when the law has been reasonable," Mr. Granlund said. "But this law is unreasonable. You're going to have to hire an army to enforce it."
Indeed, there is talk of setting up "smoke-easy's" and of civil disobedience campaigns by smokers and bar owners.
Banning bar smoking, they say, will not only rob customers of an otherwise lawful pleasure, it will hurt owners because smokers will stop going to bars. Forrest Miller, a bartender at the Studio Lounge in Hollywood, said that about 100 people have already signed a petition he keeps behind the bar seeking a repeal of the law.
'I've been a bartender for 35 years, and they're trying to destroy my business," said Mr. Miller, 53. "I'm not a smoker and I'm healthy as a horse."
Some people have even suggested that bars should borrow from the tactics of civil rights and anti-war protesters of the 1960's and hold smoke-ins. "Smokers have rights too," said Allan Hill, a bartender at Studio Lounge in Hollywood. "On Jan. 1, I'm going to ask smokers to take a cigarette out and smoke it."
Even if the other bar owners and managers go along with the ban, said Brad McAllen, a 50-year-old bar manager, it would probably be half-hearted compliance. "We'll post a no-smoking sign," he said, "but I can't ask my bartenders to be cigarette police."
Three years ago, when smoking in restaurants was banned in California, there were plenty of dire predictions. But recent studies show no drop in business at bars that already prohibit smoking, said Colleen Stevens of the state health department's tobacco control section. "
Ms. Stevens said there are 850,000 people who work in the state's hospitality industry. "Until now, they were the only workers in California not protected en masse against secondhand smoke," she said. "This is a workers' health issue."
Enforcing the ban will be up to local city and county governments. In most parts of the state, if a business owner or manager sees a customer smoking, they are required to ask the patron to stop. But because of concerns for employee safety, they are not required to evict a patron who continues to smoke.
If someone files a complaint with their local health department, for example, an owner could be given a $100 fine for the first offense, $200 for the second and up to $7,000 by the fourth.
There are still some exemptions from the law, for example, for bars with no employees -- no bartender, no one to sweep up or to handle disorderly patrons. But there are not many of those in California. The state law does not ban smoking at outdoor arenas and stadiums, but some local governments do have outdoor restrictions.
For several years New York City has banned smoking in most restaurants and other indoor public places, but bars have been exempt because city officials were concerned about the potential effect on tourism.
"To get this through we had to perform a delicate balancing act between a lot of different groups," said Richard M. Weinberg, general counsel for New York's City Council. "We allow smoking in bars because there's a culture where people smoke and drink, and you have to be realistic. You don't want to destroy economic development and tourism."
New York does allow smoking in restaurants that have bar areas if the bar is at least six feet from the dining section. Mr. Weinberg said there has been overwhelming compliance with the law.
Kimberly Belshe, the head of California's health services department, said she expected compliance with the smoking bar ban.
"It will probably take some time," Ms. Belshe said, "but it will be as commonplace for smokers to step outside the bar to have a smoke as telling their woes to the bartender."
Robert Taylor, a 28-year-old smoker who installs telephone systems, was sipping on a beer the other day and puffing on a Winston at Steve Boardner's Bar & Grill, one of the oldest in Hollywood. He said he did not like the law but it would not make him stop going to bars.
Many workers have to take work where they find it, even in a bar filled with smoke.
Marc Burgat, of the American Heart Association in California, said it would take at least six months to see how well the law worked. "In terms of tobacco control, California has always been a leader of the nation," he said. "I think this will spread like a wave across the country. It's just a matter of time before I'm able to go into a bar in Washington or Dallas and watch the game and not be bothered by tobacco smoke."

INTERNATIONAL A3-9
 
New Internet Rules in China May Be Aimed at Dissent
China has introduced new regulations criminalizing the use of electronic mail and the Internet in ways that defame Government agencies or promote separatist movements. Officials cited a need "to safeguard national security and social stability." A3 

Avian Flu Safeguards
The bird-flu outbreak that has killed four people in Hong Kong originated from chickens imported from mainland China, Hong Kong officials declared. They said they had worked out an elaborate inspection system to screen poultry imports from the mainland and would extend a ban on imports until it was set up. A3
 
Vietnam Chooses Hard-Liner
Vietnam announced that Gen. Le Kha Phieu had been appointed to the country's top post, General Secretary of the Communist Party. General Phieu, reputed to be a law-and-order enthusiast, replaces Do Moui, who in the 1980's steered the country through a modest liberalizing phase. A9
 
Kenya Vote Ends in Confusion
Both President Daniel arap Moi and the opposition in Kenya accused the Electoral Commission of rigging the elections against them after a second day of voting. Chaos had blossomed at the polls amid bad weather and anger at an erratic distribution of ballots. The specter of resurgent political violence hovered as politicians on all sides armed themselves with reasons to reject the results. A4
 
Judge Disrupts U.S. Policy
Legal experts said that a Texas magistrate's unexpected decision to release a Rwandan Hutu charged with genocide and refuse a government request to surrender him to an international tribunal demonstrated that judges were clashing more and more often with Federal agencies trying to carry out international treaty obligations. Some warned that the precedent could prove "very disruptive" to the conduct of American foreign policy, which often pressures other countries to extradite people. A4
 
Population Growth Is Slowing
A study by the liberal-leaning Population Institute said the world's population growth was slowing but warned that it was still comparable to an overwhelming tidal wave and emphasized that it was concentrated in poorer countries. The authors of the report hope it will provide fodder for an upcoming Congressional debate on the funding of abortions overseas and counter a spate of recent articles contending that declining population rates in developed countries are the real crisis. A6
 
Conviction in Anti-Muslim Case
Tatyana Suskin was convicted in a Jerusalem court of committing an act of racism and trying to offend religious feelings with her posters depicting the prophet Mohammed as a pig. Rioting broke out in the West Bank town of Hebron when she put up the posters there in June. Miss Suskin is a follower of the banned anti-Arab group Kach. A7

California Bans Smoking Inside Most Public Places

Details on Scientology Accord
New details of a 1993 settlement between the Church of Scientology and the I.R.S. show that the church paid $12.5 million to the Federal Government, and, in turn, was granted tax-exempt status, saving the church millions of dollars in taxes. As part of the settlement, the church agreed to drop its lawsuits against the I.R.S. and to stop helping church members who, along with the church itself, had brought 2,200 lawsuits against the agency and its officials over the years. In exchange, the I.R.S. stopped its audits of 13 major Scientology organizations, dismissed tax penalties and liens against some church organizations and granted tax-exempt status to 114 Scientology-related entities. A1

The trustees of President Clinton's legal defense fund said they would close their operation because campaign financing scandals and restrictions on the fund had slowed donations to a trickle. But some friends and advisers of Mr. Clinton have already set about designing a new fund with greater leeway in raising money. A1
 
Elderly Challenge Health Rule
A new Federal law scheduled to take effect on Thursday has set off a fierce dispute over the right of elderly people to choose their own health care and to negotiate fees with their doctors. A conservative group that represents more than a half-million elderly people filed suit in Federal District Court to block the law, which was included in the balanced-budget bill signed earlier this year by President Clinton. A13

In a reversal of earlier denials, Tyson Foods admitted Monday to having made more than $12,000 in illegal gifts to Mr. Espy, including sports junkets and Clinton inaugural tickets worth $6,000. A12

A licensed practical nurse known for his tender touch at a hospital in a small Indiana town was charged with the murders of six patients, by killing them with lethal injections. The police said their investigation centered on perhaps 100 unexplained deaths at the hospital from 1993 to 1995. A10

The collapse caused no injuries, but it forced the shutdown of 42d Street between 7th and 8th Avenues and nearly forced the cancellation of a preview performance of "Ragtime" at the new Ford Theater. Consumer Confidence Rises
Consumer confidence surged in December to its highest level since mid-1969 as Americans seemed unruffled by Asia's crisis and buoyed by the exceptionally promising job market. Those calling jobs "plentiful" reached an unprecedented 40.6 percent of respondents in a monthly survey. The optimism in the survey was viewed as a strong signal that the economy will remain vibrant. SECTION: Section A; Page 14; Column 6; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 177 words

HEADLINE: New York Gained Empire, but Lost Democracy;
Colonies' First City

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Kenneth T. Jackson, as he mentions New York City's Dutch roots (Op-Ed, Dec. 28), brings to mind another event to celebrate: the 1653 incorporation of New Amsterdam as the first "city" in the original 13 colonies.
In Europe, incorporated cities were "incubators of capitalism," autonomous centers protected by civil law and commercial privileges. Seventeenth-century Amsterdam, the city after which New Amsterdam was modeled, dominated European trade and finance. 
 New Amsterdam's charter from the States-General of the Netherlands came in response to a petition from Manhattan merchants who sought to enhance their own burgeoning commerce. City government, they wrote, would "attract navigation and profitable trade" and "allure everyone hither."
The 1653 charter became the cornerstone of Manhattan's first merchant community and was responsible for New York City's economic growth during its formative years. In 2003, why not celebrate 350 years of being a city?
 
DENNIS J. MAIKA
Pleasantville, N.Y., Dec. 28, 1997
